		<item>
		<title>A potential quantum leap</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/a-potential-quantum-leap/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Kermit Pattison]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 12 Nov 2025 22:01:41 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Science & Tech]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Computers]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Quantum physics]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=419451</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Harvard physicists unveil system to solve long-standing barrier to new generation of supercomputers ]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below title-above-image centered-image"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/science-technology/"
		>
			Science &amp; Tech		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		A potential quantum leap	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Mikhail Lukin." class="wp-image-419452" height="992" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/042324_Quantum_Network_Node_0106-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Mikhail Lukin, co-director of the Quantum Science and Engineering Initiative.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">File photo by Niles Singer/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Kermit Pattison	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-12">
			November 12, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Harvard physicists unveil system to solve long-standing barrier to new generation of supercomputers		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>The dream of creating game-changing quantum computers — supermachines that encode information in single atoms rather than conventional bits — has been hampered by the formidable challenge known as quantum error correction.</p>



<p>In a <a href="https://www.nature.com/articles/s41586-025-09848-5">paper</a> published Monday in Nature, Harvard researchers demonstrated a new system capable of detecting and removing errors below a key performance threshold, potentially providing a workable solution to the problem.</p>



<p>“For the first time, we combined all essential elements for a scalable, error-corrected quantum computation in an integrated architecture,” said <a href="https://www.physics.harvard.edu/people/facpages/lukin">Mikhail Lukin</a>, co-director of the Quantum Science and Engineering Initiative, Joshua and Beth Friedman University Professor, and senior author of the new paper. “These experiments — by several measures the most advanced that have been done on any quantum platform to date — create the scientific foundation for practical large-scale quantum computation.”</p>



<p>In the new paper, the team demonstrated a “fault tolerant” system using 448 atomic quantum bits manipulated with an intricate sequence of techniques to detect and correct errors.</p>



<p>The key mechanisms include physical entanglement, logical entanglement, logical magic, and entropy removal. For example, the system employs the trick of “quantum teleportation” — transferring the quantum state of one particle to another elsewhere without physical contact.</p>



<p>“There are still a lot of technical challenges remaining to get to very large-scale computer with millions of qubits, but this is the first time we have an architecture that is conceptually scalable,” said lead author Dolev Bluvstein, Ph.D. ’25, who did the research during his graduate studies at Harvard and is now an assistant professor at Caltech. “It’s going to take a lot of effort and technical development, but it’s becoming clear that we can build fault-tolerant quantum computers.”</p>



<p>The Harvard-led collaboration included researchers from MIT and was jointly headed by Lukin; <a href="https://www.physics.harvard.edu/people/facpages/greiner">Markus Greiner</a>, George Vasmer Leverett Professor of Physics; and <a href="https://physics.mit.edu/faculty/vladan-vuletic/">Vladan Vuletić</a>, Lester Wolfe Professor of Physics at MIT. The team conducts research in collaboration with QuEra Computing, a startup company spun out from Harvard-MIT labs, the Joint Quantum Institute at University of Maryland, and the National Institute of Standards and Technology.</p>



<p>The new paper represents an important advance in a three-decade pursuit of quantum error correction.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32)"><blockquote><p>“In the end, physics is an experimental science. By realizing and testing these fundamental ideas in a lab, you really start seeing light at the end of the tunnel.”</p><cite>Mikhail Lukin</cite></blockquote></figure>



<p>Conventional computers encode information in a binary code of zeros and ones. Quantum computers store information in subatomic particles whose counterintuitive properties of quantum physics can achieve far more processing power.</p>



<p>In a conventional computer, the most basic unit of information is a “bit” (short for binary digit); in quantum systems, the basic unit is a “qubit” (or quantum bit).</p>



<p>In conventional computers, doubling the number of bits doubles the processing power; in quantum systems, adding qubits exponentially increases the power because of a phenomenon called quantum entanglement.</p>



<p>In theory, a system of 300 quantum bits can store more information than the number of particles in the known universe.</p>



<p>With such vast power, quantum computers have the potential to deliver breakthroughs in fields such as drug discovery, cryptography, machine learning, artificial intelligence, finance, and material design.</p>



<p>But there are hurdles to realizing that revolutionary potential. Chief among them is the error rate. Qubits are inherently susceptible to slipping out of their quantum states and losing their encoded information, making error correction a core prerequisite to achieving large quantum machines.</p>



<p>In the new paper, the team combined various methods to create complex circuits with dozens of error correction layers. The system suppresses errors below a critical threshold — the point where adding qubits further reduces errors rather than increasing them.</p>



<p>“There have been many important theoretical proposals for how you should implement error correction,” said Alexandra Geim, one of the lead authors on the new paper and a Ph.D. student in physics in the Kenneth C. Griffin Graduate School of Arts and Sciences. “In this paper, we really focused on understanding what are the core mechanisms for enabling scalable, deep-circuit computation.&nbsp;By understanding that, you can essentially remove things that you don’t need, reduce your overheads, and get to a practical regime much faster.”</p>



<p>Lukin said years of experiments showed how to overcome some technical challenges and avoid others.</p>



<p>“We realize which of these bottlenecks are real and which bottlenecks you just can bypass,” he said. “In the end, physics is an experimental science. By realizing and testing these fundamental ideas in a lab, you really start seeing light at the end of the tunnel.”</p>



<p>Researchers around the world are studying a variety of potential platforms for qubits, including different types of atoms, ions, and superconducting qubits.</p>



<p>The Harvard team specializes in neutral atoms (those with no electrical charge because they have equal numbers of protons and electrons) of the element rubidium. They use lasers to change the configuration of electrons to encode the atoms to become information-carrying qubits.</p>



<p>Hartmut Neven, vice president of engineering at the Google Quantum AI team, said the new paper came amid an “incredibly exciting” race between qubit platforms.</p>



<p>“This work represents a significant advance toward our shared goal of building a large-scale, useful quantum computer,” he said.</p>



<p>In September, the Harvard-MIT-QuEra group published another Nature paper demonstrating a <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/09/clearing-significant-hurdle-to-quantum-computing/">system of more than 3,000 qubits</a> that could operate continuously for more than two hours and overcome another technical hurdle of atom loss.</p>



<p>With recent advances, Lukin believes the core elements for building quantum computers are falling into place.</p>



<p>“This big dream that many of us had for several decades, for the first time, is really in direct sight,” he said.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p>This research received federal funding from the Defense Advanced Research Project Agency, Department of Energy, Intelligence Advanced Research Projects Activity, Army Research Office, National Science Foundation, and National Defense Science and Engineering Graduate Fellowship program.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Princeton leader defends campus free speech efforts amid ‘civic crisis’</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/princeton-leader-defends-campus-free-speech-efforts-amid-civic-crisis/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Anna Lamb]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 12 Nov 2025 19:27:26 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Nation & World]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Books]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Democracy]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Education]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[social media]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[U.S. Politics]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=419534</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Eisgruber, author of ‘Terms of Respect,’ says campus tensions reflect wider U.S. divisions ]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-classic has-media-on-the-left"
	style=" "
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="David Deming and Christopher Eisgruber." class="wp-image-419536" height="768" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=150,113 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=300,225 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=768,576 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=1024,768 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1152 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=43,32 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=85,64 85w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=1488,1116 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1260 1680w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/free-speech-1920.jpg?resize=600,450 600w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">David Deming (left) and Christopher Eisgruber.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photo by Anna Lamb</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Princeton leader defends campus free speech efforts amid ‘civic crisis’	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Eisgruber, author of ‘Terms of Respect,’ says tensions reflect wider U.S. divisions		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Anna Lamb	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-12">
			November 12, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-right is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-f1f2ed93 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>U.S. colleges and universities have for the most part done a good job protecting and promoting campus discourse in recent years, according to Princeton President <a href="https://president.princeton.edu/about-office/christopher-l-eisgruber-president">Christopher Eisgruber</a>. They have not, however, escaped the divisions affecting institutions and communities across the country.</p>



<p>“There’s a genuine civic crisis in America,” Eisgruber said in a conversation with David Deming, Danoff Dean of Harvard College, on Nov. 5 at Sanders Theatre. “It is true that all of us are having a harder time talking to one another now than we had in the past, and that’s true for colleges as well. What I deny is that colleges or the younger generation are doing worse in that kind of environment.”</p>



<p>Eisgruber’s new book, <a href="https://www.hachettebookgroup.com/titles/christopher-l-eisgruber/terms-of-respect/9781541607453/">“Terms of Respect: How Colleges Get Free Speech Right,”</a><em> </em>reflects on lessons he’s learned as the leader of Princeton for more than a decade, and during rising U.S. polarization. In his talk with Deming, he expanded on themes covered in the book and spoke from experience, some of it sharp-edged, on the challenges of handling campus tensions in real time.</p>



<p>Among them: headline-grabbing student protests of campus speakers at schools across the country. At Princeton, Eisgruber supports faculty and student efforts to welcome speakers who challenge status-quo views. He’s not interested, however, in promoting empty provocations or people who seem interested only in fueling the flames of conflict.</p>



<p>Scholars and students “have tremendous freedom to make those invitations,” he said. “Inviting noted political figures to campus who are very controversial can be a very valuable thing for people to do. Inviting people who are coming as provocateurs, I think, is a much less valuable thing to do.”</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-e18e953a-08fa-42a9-890b-c6a2633ef0bc">
<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p>“Inviting noted political figures to campus who are very controversial can be a very valuable thing for people to do. Inviting people who are coming as provocateurs, I think, is a much less valuable thing to do.”</p>
</blockquote>
</div>



<p>An area where colleges have stumbled is in balancing the right to protest with their obligation to maintain a safe and productive campus environment, Eisgruber said, pointing specifically to a <a href="https://whyy.org/articles/agreement-ends-student-occupation-of-nassau-hall-in-princeton/">student occupation of a Princeton building</a> during a 2015 Black Justice League protest. The university faltered, he said, by allowing the demonstration to interfere with everyday operations.</p>



<p>“I think maintaining that constructive environment where everybody feels like they can be a part of the conversation on equal terms means that when there’s a disruption going on in the university’s business, and you’ve got a set of rules, and your rules say this is the point where you have to leave, you have to enforce them,” he said.</p>



<p>Even so, he argued, campus protests demonstrate a sustained commitment to free speech on the part of universities — not the other way around.</p>



<p>“When you have a really great free speech environment, which I maintain Harvard does, you’re going to get controversy on the campus, which means sometimes you’re going to see disruptions,” he said. “That’s not the right way to count free speech. If I’m an accountant — what do I want to look for? I want to look for engagement. I want to look for argument. I want to look for controversy.”</p>



<p>Engagement, argument, and controversy remain alive and well on U.S. campuses, he said, but are now under pressure and scrutiny that grow more intense as social media and other forces stoke conflict.</p>



<p>“Colleges get free speech right through millions of conversations like this one or ones that take place in dorm rooms or dining hall tables or at public events or classrooms in colleges and universities across the United States every year.</p>



<p>“We are in a time in America when it’s hard to have the conversations that really matter. We’re in a time in America characterized by extreme polarization that leads people to demonize folks who are on the other side of arguments, and we’re at a time in America when social media has created different kinds of incentives that make constructive conversation difficult.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>‘Incredibly serious and unbelievably funny’</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/incredibly-serious-and-unbelievably-funny/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Liz Mineo]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 12 Nov 2025 19:22:21 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Arts & Culture]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Books]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Humanities]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=419517</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Philip Roth biographer, in Harvard talk, digs into novelist’s contradictions, ‘true loves,’ and recurring themes from lust to Jewish life]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-split-screen has-light-background has-colored-background has-media-on-the-right"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/arts-humanities/"
		>
			Arts &amp; Culture		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		‘Incredibly serious and unbelievably funny’	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Philip Roth biographer, in Harvard talk, digs into novelist’s contradictions, ‘true loves,’ and recurring themes from lust to Jewish life		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Liz Mineo	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-12">
			November 12, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Steven J. Zipperstein." class="wp-image-419520" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_02-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Steven J. Zipperstein speaks at Harvard about his new biography, “Philip Roth: Stung by Life.”</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Stephanie Mitchell/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-left is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-12dd3699 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Steven J. Zipperstein was on the verge of starting rabbinical school when he came across an early excerpt from Philip Roth’s trailblazing novel “Portnoy’s Complaint” about a young Jewish bachelor and his obsession with sex.</p>



<p>It was a reading that taught the young Zipperstein, who grew up in a Jewish Orthodox family in Los Angeles and was wrestling with cultural and religious expectations, crucial lessons about both the nature and the intricacies of freedom.</p>



<p>Zipperstein made a reference to the lasting impact of that early reading of “Portnoy’s Complaint” during a recent Harvard talk about his new biography, “<a href="https://www.jewishlives.org/books/philiproth?srsltid=AfmBOooqPl7MYBobknieXbOHeayR0BgInvTY4I1nboiIrYIsdBRwQEgj">Philip Roth: Stung by Life</a>,” with Rebecca Goldstein, a MacArthur-winning writer of novels and nonfiction, and a research associate in the Department of Psychology. The event was co-sponsored by the <a href="https://cjs.fas.harvard.edu/">Center for Jewish Studies</a> at Harvard and the Jewish Cultures and Societies Seminar at the Mahindra Humanities Center.</p>



<p>“What I realized was that the book was not about the happy life of a libertine, but it was about someone with all the freedom in the world who was a neurotic,” said Zipperstein, the Daniel E. Koshland Professor in Jewish Culture and History at Stanford University.</p>



<p>“It taught me something about the world I was yearning to enter into. It taught me that if I was to leave orthodoxy, I was not entering panacea or utopia. His voice remained with me.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignfull size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1920" height="736" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg" alt="&quot;Philip Roth: Stung by Life&quot; talk at Harvard." class="wp-image-419527" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=150,58 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=300,115 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=768,294 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=1024,393 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=1536,589 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=83,32 83w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=167,64 167w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=1488,570 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_Philip_Roth_13-wide.jpg?resize=1680,644 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /></figure>



<p>Zipperstein left orthodoxy and became a historian of Judaism and Jewish culture and a longtime admirer of Roth for his “honesty to confront life,” for being both “an incredibly serious writer and unbelievably funny,” and for his relentless examination of the Jewish American experience. Zipperstein writes of Roth, “He would probe nearly every aspect of contemporary Jewish life: the passions of Jewish childhood, the pleasures and anguish of postwar Jewish suburbia, Israel, diaspora, the Holocaust, circumcision, the interplay between the nice Jewish boy and the turbulent one deep inside.”</p>



<p>A formidable and prolific author who won numerous literary awards, Roth wrote 31 books, among them “Goodbye, Columbus,” “The Ghost Writer,” “The Plot Against America,” “American Pastoral,” “I Married a Communist,” and “The Human Stain.” When Roth died in 2018 at age 85, he was described in a New York Times <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/05/22/obituaries/philip-roth-dead.html">obituary</a> as a “pre-eminent figure in 20th-century literature,” and a “towering novelist who explored lust, Jewish life and America.”</p>



<p>Around the time when Zipperstein’s biography on writer Isaac Rosenfeld came out, Roth reached out to Zipperstein saying that he was on a “quest to find a biographer,” said Zipperstein. In 2021, a biography of Roth authored by Blake Bailey was quickly pulled from publication in the wake of accusations of sexual misconduct against Bailey. After that, there was a need for a new biographical study of Roth, Zipperstein wrote in his book, “all the more so since few contemporary writers have shown themselves to be quite as self-referential as Roth, and few self-referential writers have managed, for so very long, to be quite cagey, so slippery.”</p>



<p>Zipperstein’s book, which published in October, draws from interviews with more than 100 friends and family members, dozens of archival materials, and conversations with Roth himself about his life and work.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-ce670faf-2555-48cd-9b89-c24b561c0f3c">
<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p>“He decided sometime in his 20s that he was going to be as great a writer as Melville, and I think he felt that there wasn’t a whole lot of discretionary time for emotional involvement.”</p>
</blockquote>
</div>



<p>“One of the reasons why he trusted me is that he knew that I really loved his prose, and that I was going to write a book that centered around his prose, because that’s really all he cared about,” said Zipperstein. “I never tired of reading his work.”</p>



<p>Asked about how he came to terms with the accusations of misogyny against Roth, Zipperstein said Roth avoided emotional ties with women and even male friends because he put more time and energy in writing his books than in any human relationship.</p>



<p>“He decided sometime in his 20s that he was going to be as great a writer as Melville, and I think he felt that there wasn’t a whole lot of discretionary time for emotional involvement,” said Zipperstein. “He was involved romantically with several extraordinary women, but as one of them told me, ‘As soon as we began to demonstrate needs, he disappeared.’”</p>



<p>In writing a biography built on the interaction between Roth’s life and his work, Zipperstein said he approached the endeavor without the need to reconcile the writer’s contradictions. &nbsp;</p>



<p>“He was someone who took offense so quickly, and yet he took incredible fictional risks,” said Zipperstein. “I was surprised by the extent to which he extended himself for friends but not to his close family. I ended up being probably closer to first cousins of his than he was. And yet, he built book after book around family. How do I reconcile that with his indifference to most of his relatives? I can’t, but one doesn’t have to reconcile everything in biography.”</p>



<p>In the end, Roth’s true devotion was to his craft, said Zipperstein. As he writes in his biography, “There was nothing of greater importance to Roth than his books, his true loves, and that is the only reason that justifies spending years of one’s own life writing and rewriting a book about him.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Four years, three words	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Stephanie Mitchell	</p>

			Harvard Staff Photographer		</p>
					</address>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-12">
			November 12, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			long read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Seniors encapsulate how they’ve changed since arriving at Harvard		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Gazette photographer Stephanie Mitchell returns for the final edition of the experiment she has been conducting with six College seniors ever since they arrived at Harvard. Inspired by Nora Ephron, who told her Wellesley College graduating in 1996, “You are not going to be you, fixed and immutable you, forever,” Mitchell met with undergrads during the first week of their <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2022/10/harvard-first-years-describe-themselves-in-three-words/">first year</a>, <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2023/10/sophomores-reflect-on-how-theyve-changed-in-a-year/">sophomore year</a>, and then <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2024/11/harvard-juniors-describe-themselves-in-3-words/">junior year</a>, photographing and asking each to describe themselves in three words. Now in their final year, the students participated in the exercise for the last time. Tracing from their first year, the three words they select appear in gray (2022), dark gray (2023), brown (2024), and black (2025).<strong></strong></p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48)"/>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-grey-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-2732ee9fcdfc559727d479edb6882d9a" id="h-free-independent-friendly" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0">Free. Independent. Friendly.</h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-grey-dark-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-b73664b0500e2ba5dd3afd5a13c25824" id="h-independent-free-possibility" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0">Independent. Free. Possibility.</h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-secondary-ochre-dark-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-0e7ce19d5d8c9803377a97cc93c1156b" id="h-free-independent-creativity" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0">Free. Independent. Creativity.</h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-black-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-9719e407e35aa2a665390abf4bab04cc" id="h-independent-free-curious" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0">Independent. Free. Curious.</h2>



<div class="wp-block-columns alignwide is-layout-flex wp-container-core-columns-is-layout-28f84493 wp-block-columns-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1667" height="2500" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg" alt="Sofia Chavez." class="wp-image-348658" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_01.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">First Year.</figcaption></figure>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1667" height="2500" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg" alt="Sofia Chavez is pictured." class="wp-image-364201" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2023/09/090723_Sophomore_Year_03.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Sophomore Year.</figcaption></figure>
</div>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-columns alignwide is-layout-flex wp-container-core-columns-is-layout-28f84493 wp-block-columns-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1666" height="2500" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg" alt="Sofia Chavez." class="wp-image-393602" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg 1666w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=682,1024 682w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=1365,2048 1365w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/10/090524_Junior_Year_222.jpg?resize=1488,2233 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1666px) 100vw, 1666px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Junior Year.</figcaption></figure>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1320" height="1980" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg" alt="Sofia Chavez Pacheco." class="wp-image-417886" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg 1320w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_29.jpg?resize=43,64 43w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1320px) 100vw, 1320px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Senior Year.</figcaption></figure>
</div>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-19e250f3 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-sofia-chavez">Sofia Chavez</h2>



<p>Currier House</p>
</div>



<p>Each year, Sofia Chavez included “free” and “independent” in her descriptors, and this year, she added “curious.” “I’m curious to be out in the world as a student, but more now as part of society, as a citizen. Now, I’m more curious than ever, and that excites me,” she said.</p>



<p>Settling into senior year, Chavez, who is a government concentrator, has been preparing to write her senior thesis about politics and the judiciary system in Mexico. She honed her writing skills in a junior seminar, incrementally writing longer papers and focusing on how to organize and defend ideas — “a transformative experience.” Many of her closest friends are also writing theses and she expects they will bond over the process. “It’s formidable, but I’m really, really excited.”</p>



<p>Reflecting on personal change she has felt since her first year, she said, “I was very worried that I need to be perfect … now I feel mistakes and hard situations build character, and that character is the tool you need to face the world outside.” She respects people who make mistakes and carry on.&nbsp; “It shows resilience, and it shows courage,” she said.</p>



<p>Initially scared to leave her home in Mexico, she now feels a sense of pride in her family values and traditions and carries them wherever she goes. “Paradoxically, being away from home connected me more with home … I only noticed that once I left and it gave me perspective. … It is not something that I can acquire in reading a book or in a text, but only through experience.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48)"/>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-grey-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-c1dea4ea6b7e2273cc94026e24bb27dd" id="h-god-loving-adventurous-passionate" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong>God-loving. Adventurous. Passionate.</strong></h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-grey-dark-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-b9d2fc142c2ce1de6348f0cecf647a95" id="h-god-loving-adventurous-kind" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong>God-loving. Adventurous. Kind.</strong></h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-secondary-ochre-dark-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-54daf442448be0769a4fe5388e43b3ff" id="h-god-loving-grateful-composed" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong>God-loving. Grateful. Composed.</strong></h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-black-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-d4e46fffc6072512c0cae1a02c02b87f" id="h-god-loving-intentional-bold" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong>God-loving. Intentional. Bold.</strong></h2>



<div class="wp-block-columns alignwide is-layout-flex wp-container-core-columns-is-layout-28f84493 wp-block-columns-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1667" height="2500" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg" alt="Bradley Chinhara." class="wp-image-348667" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/082922_First_Year_41.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">First Year.</figcaption></figure>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">

</div>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-19e250f3 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-bradley-chinhara">Bradley Chinhara</h2>



<p>Lowell House</p>
</div>



<p>Zimbabwe native Bradley Chinhara chose “God-loving” as his first word all four years —“That one is never going to change” — and this year added “Intentional” and “Bold.” “I’ve been trying to take more control of my life. Think things through before I do them. Be intentional with the people in my life, my relationships, my faith, and my work. … I’ve been teaching myself to learn to be uncomfortable and to be courageous and do things even though I’m scared or even though I’m anxious,” he said.</p>



<p>Chinhara’s mother died the summer before his junior year. To help with the grieving process, he kept busy the fall semester with academics from his computer science concentration, rugby, and extracurriculars such as Harvard Product Lab and Harvard African Students Association. “It was overwhelming, but it was a good way to keep myself going, because I had so much to do.” His circle of close friends was essential.</p>



<p>Reflecting on his four years, Chinhara shared: “I thought I knew stuff, because most of that stuff was dreams, goals, a lot of excitement, and that’s good. It’s good energy to have for a freshman. Now that I’m a senior, now that I’ve experienced classes, extracurriculars, social relationships, I’ve pursued my faith even more, I’m more composed, more settled into those dreams. I’m more aware of what I need to do in order to succeed, in order to have healthy relationships, in order to take care of my mental health.”</p>



<p>“I’m grateful for how I’ve held onto my values and morals, that stayed as a constant. I always tell my friends, before you go out and try something in college ask yourself, am I growing or changing? If you’re changing, maybe reconsider doing it, if you’re growing, then do it,” he said. “I’ve focused on growing but not changing my values.”</p>



<p>“Freshman year, my mom was still alive, and senior year, my mom’s not here anymore, and she was the most important person in my entire life. I want anything and everything I do from here to be in honor of her. I want to try my hardest to succeed, to be a good role model for my little brother, and to do her proud.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48)"/>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-grey-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-50b32a03117d90b927c24fbf8cd1c9d2" id="h-excited-determined-outgoing" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong><strong>Excited. Determined. Outgoing</strong></strong>.</h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-grey-dark-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-accd10fb10bf4fa2733e87e87a9b61bc" id="h-excited-passionate-ambitious" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong><strong>Excited. Passionate. Ambitious.</strong></strong></h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-secondary-ochre-dark-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-c473d24ef7b9a2c7d3e72e4aad9f05a3" id="h-growth-excited-passionate" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong><strong>Growth. Excited. Passionate.</strong></strong></h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-black-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-0108c327f7dce6db5aabbbc3d7276b10" id="h-grateful-hopeful-proud" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong><strong>Grateful. Hopeful. Proud.</strong></strong></h2>

<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-19e250f3 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-riley-flynn"><strong>Riley Flynn</strong></h2>



<p>Eliot House</p>
</div>



<p>As she rounds the corner to her senior year, Riley Flynn, who comes from Phoenix, feels grateful. “I’ve met all these incredible people that I get to call my best friends from classes, school, softball, and athletics.” She is hopeful for the future and proud of how far she has come since her first year.</p>



<p>Being on the softball team all four years has provided helpful perspective. “I could see our freshmen come in every year and relive those freshman-year feelings, and that has really helped me keep grounded.”</p>



<p>As a human development and regenerative biology concentrator, junior year was “the year of organic chemistry … a defining moment in every STEM concentrators’ years here.” She pushed through the difficult semesters by bonding with her classmates, and in a dynamic stem cell class where she was able to witness all three parts of healthcare (research, doctors, and patients), which solidified her resolve to continue in the field.</p>



<p>She finds herself still working through future plans and being content with this. “I am just focused on being grateful for being here and trust in the process,” she said. “I know where I want to go, and if it’s not, then I will reassess and change.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48)"/>

<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1320" height="1980" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg" alt="Dara Omoloja." class="wp-image-417887" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg 1320w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/090525_Senior_Year_19.jpg?resize=43,64 43w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1320px) 100vw, 1320px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Senior Year.</figcaption></figure>
</div>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-19e250f3 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-dara-omoloja"><strong><strong>Dara Omoloja</strong></strong></h2>



<p>Leverett House</p>
</div>



<p>Over the last year, Dara Omoloja, who calls Milwaukee home, embarked upon a faith journey, became Christian, and at Easter was baptized. “The way God has worked in my life in the past year has been really extreme,” she shared. “I trust in something that’s bigger and stronger than myself.”</p>



<p>Having finished her pre-med requirements and nearly done with her neuroscience concentration requirements, she is planning to complete her academic requirements and also go on as many trips as possible, exploring local museums, Cape Cod, and Six Flags amusement park, as well as taking trips to New York and Mexico.</p>



<p>Considering her years at Harvard, Omoloja said, “I’m the closest ever to knowing who I am … I feel College is really that experience of discovering what makes you, you. … It is a transformative experience, and years that really do shape you for the rest of your life. I would have been a completely different person if I didn’t go here.”</p>



<p>Thinking back to her first days as a first-year, she shared, “I was still a people pleaser, deeply insecure, and I think I changed for the better in that way. I truly embrace my flaws and the things that I am good at. I’m just more authentic, and that brings me a lot more peace.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48)"/>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-grey-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-d0cb247c00bdb4a13426272286be3165" id="h-adventurous-ambitious-authentic" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong>Adventurous. Ambitious. Authentic.</strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-grey-dark-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-2fb9a847a047c73225f0bed8395501f1" id="h-excited-fun-adventurous" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong>Excited. Fun. Adventurous.</strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></h2>

Relaxed. Melancholy.</strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></h2>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-neutral-black-color has-text-color has-link-color wp-elements-b40fbdfacd0089c83d0c419942f512b1" id="h-happy-sad-excited" style="margin-top:0;margin-bottom:0"><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong>Happy. Sad. Excited.</strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></h2>

<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-19e250f3 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-austin-wang"><strong>Austin Wang</strong></h2>



<p>Lowell House</p>
</div>



<p>At the start of his final year, Austin Wang, from Waterloo, Canada, feels mixed emotions — both excited for the possibilities ahead and surprised by how quickly his College years have passed.</p>



<p>He has continued with activities such as ringing the Lowell House bells every Sunday morning and volunteering at the Harvard Square Homeless Shelter, the latter being “really rewarding, definitely the most impactful thing I do.”</p>



<p>Wang, who is studying chemical and physical biology with a secondary in computer science, is preparing a thesis project where he will “image how cells eat stuff,” a process that is not well understood. “It’s a good mix of comp bio and bench work. I’ve always loved microscopy a lot.”</p>



<p>During junior year, he branched out from his concentration and enjoyed a wide variety of studies, including learning how to make books with binding techniques taught at the Weisman Preservation Center and participating in a creative writing workshop with senior lecturer and fiction writer Paul Yoon.</p>



<p>Reflecting on how he’s changed, Wang said: “I don’t think I was super confident that first year. I didn’t really talk a lot … I definitely grew a lot in College, and hopefully that continues. I’ve also stayed the same in some ways … definitely a bit more mature.”</p>

Motivated. Hopeful.</strong></strong></strong></strong></h2>

Grateful. Inspired.</strong></strong></strong></strong></h2>

Capable. Loved.</strong></strong></strong></strong></h2>

</div>

<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-19e250f3 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h2 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-myra-bhathena"><strong><strong><strong>Myra Bhathena</strong></strong></strong></h2>



<p>Currier House</p>
</div>



<p>“I’m a naturally sentimental person,” said Myra Bhathena, a native of Andover, Massachusetts. “As a senior, it’s making everything a little bit more sentimental for me. I’m just trying to soak it all in.”</p>



<p>An economics concentrator with the global health and health policy secondary and on the pre-med track, her junior year classes were intense. “I’ve been building skills throughout my almost four years here, so it has made me capable. I’m a more confident person.” For her senior thesis on healthcare, Bhathena is writing “about non-communicable diseases, specifically diabetes, because of the huge epidemic that America is currently dealing with, which is predicted to get worse.”</p>



<p>She continues to work as a section leader for EC 10, an introductory economics course. “It was a little daunting at first,” she said, “I have learned that I am capable of teaching concepts and connecting with students, and I found a lot of meaning through that role.”</p>



<p>As a first-year, Bhenthena shared, “Building relationships is the most important thing to me. I believe it is important to have empathy and show love for others,” and as she rounds out her senior year, that hasn’t changed. “I am a very loving person, but that’s because I’ve been lucky enough to be loved so much by my family, by my friends in College and out of College. I chose that one [word] as the last one, because I think it’s my favorite quality of mine, that I am lucky to have so many people that love me.”</p>



	<div class="featured-articles is-post-type-post is-style-grid-list"  style="">
						<h2 class="featured-articles__title wp-block-heading">More like this</h2>
				<ul class="featured-articles__list ">
		
		<li class="featured-article ">
						<figure class="featured-article__image">
				<img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1200" height="750" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/083122_First_Day_383_2500.jpg?resize=1200%2C750" class="attachment-large-landscape-desktop size-large-landscape-desktop" alt="Students enter campus gate." srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/083122_First_Day_383_2500.jpg?resize=608,380 608w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/083122_First_Day_383_2500.jpg?resize=784,490 784w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/083122_First_Day_383_2500.jpg?resize=1024,640 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/083122_First_Day_383_2500.jpg?resize=1200,750 1200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/083122_First_Day_383_2500.jpg?resize=1488,930 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2022/09/083122_First_Day_383_2500.jpg?resize=1680,1050 1680w" />							</figure>
			
			<div class="featured-article__content">
						<a class="featured-article__category" href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/campus-community/">
			Campus &amp; Community		</a>
		
				<h3 class="featured-article__title wp-block-heading"><a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2022/10/harvard-first-years-describe-themselves-in-three-words/">Defining moment</a></h3>
									<p class="featured-article__excerpt">
						We asked a group of first-years to tell us about themselves. Read their answers now, before they change. 
					</p>
				
									<figure class="featured-article__series series-badge__header wp-block-heading no-series-logo">

		<item>
		<title>Reese Witherspoon returns to Harvard in a different role</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/reese-witherspoon-returns-to-harvard-in-a-different-role/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 10 Nov 2025 18:39:43 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Work & Economy]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Business]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Humanities]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Visual Arts]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=419438</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[‘Legally Blonde’ star was tired of vying for good parts, so she created her own — and a company worth nearly $1 billion]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below title-above-image centered-image"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/business-economy/"
		>
			Work &amp; Economy		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Reese Witherspoon returns to Harvard in a different role	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Actor and businessperson Reese Witherspoon in Klarman Hall." class="wp-image-419441" height="992" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_1107.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Reese Witherspoon.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Veasey Conway/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Jacob Sweet	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-10">
			November 10, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			‘Legally Blonde’ star was tired of vying for good parts, so she created her own — and a company worth nearly $1 billion		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>“I just wanted to say something,” Reese Witherspoon said as she took her seat on stage at the Business School’s Klarman Hall. “It’s taken me 25 years, but this is my first day at Harvard!”</p>



<p>Those in attendance at the fireside chat, hosted by the Business School’s Women Student’s Association, were treated to a discussion of Witherspoon’s journey as an actor, producer, and entrepreneur. In a conversation that lasted about an hour, Witherspoon discussed how she grew her media company, Hello Sunshine, into a powerful creator of women-centered movies and television in Hollywood — and one whose partial sale earned Witherspoon $900 million.</p>



<p>Witherspoon also gamely recited some of Elle Woods’ most iconic Harvard-related lines from the classic “Legally Blonde” for the pink-hued attendees. “What, like it’s hard?” she said, to a roar from the crowd.</p>



<p>When asked about the throughline across her career, Witherspoon, 49, said that she’s always been a storyteller. “From the time I was 6 or 7 years old, I’d get all the kids on the playground to all act in a story I wanted to tell,” she said. This corralling also required leadership. “I was really good at persuading people to do things they didn’t want to do, and then working toward a common story.”</p>



<p>At the beginning of her career, Witherspoon fought for roles. Facing doubts from leaders at MGM Studios that she was right for the part of Elle Woods after multiple auditions, Witherspoon was asked to visit the MGM building and stay in character the entire time. Getting the part was exciting — and she continues to find inspiration and joy from the character decades later — but as her career continued, she felt underwhelmed by the lack of dynamic roles available to women in Hollywood.</p>



<p>Witherspoon’s solution was to “break systems.” She encouraged those in attendance to think about where they are situated in different systems within their own lives — their work, families, education, friend groups. “I think it’s important to say, ‘Do you feel comfortable where you sit in these systems?’” she asked. “And could you be the one to address the bigger issue?”</p>



<p>When she was 34, Witherspoon said, she worried that if she didn’t tackle the problem of lackluster representation of women’s stories then, she wasn’t going to leave the business any better off than she found it. “And the truth is, I have a daughter,” Witherspoon said. “I wanted to do it for her, because she deserves better storytelling, and her friends deserve better storytelling.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignwide size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?w=1024" alt="Reese Witherspoon and MBA students Jadyn Bryden (center) and Kayla Walsh." class="wp-image-419443" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110425_ReeseWitherspoon_135.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">During the audience&#8217;s Q&amp;A session, Witherspoon responded to the top question: “Does your company have summer internships?” She was joined by Harvard Business School M.B.A. students Kayla Walsh (left) and Jadyn Bryden.</figcaption></figure>



<p>To help remedy that, Witherspoon started a production company, Pacific Standard, which bought books and turned them into movies. The first three purchases were “Gone Girl,” “Wild,” and “Big Little Lies,” all of which became hits — earning three Oscar nominations and eight Emmy wins.</p>



<p>Witherspoon felt on top of the world until she got a call from her accountant. “She goes, ‘You’re breaking even. And I’m not sure you’re going to make payroll unless you go into your own personal finances to pay people,’” Witherspoon recalled. “I was just like, how is that possible? I could not be doing better, picking better winners, getting better budgets.” The trouble, she realized, was that she had the wrong business idea — making money off fees instead of having greater equity in the product.</p>



<p>After she complained about the situation to her friend Seth Rodsky, co-founder of the private equity firm Strand Partners, he said that to create a bigger company with more employees and more impact she should start over and shift the business plan. The transition to a model that emphasized equity, as well as a willingness to work with streaming platforms, pushed her new company into profitability and success.</p>



<p>In 2017, Hello Sunshine became the first company to sell a scripted television show to the new streaming platform Apple TV+. The unwillingness of other studios to do the same, Witherspoon said, helped Hello Sunshine negotiate an equity stake in each piece of content she created for Apple. “They didn’t like it,” she said. “It was precedent-breaking.” But the next time she negotiated, she was able to ask for the same favorable terms. The success accumulated. In 2021, Witherspoon sold a partial stake in the company to Candle Media, a company backed by the investment firm Blackstone, for nearly $1 billion.</p>



<p>The sale, Witherspoon said, helped the company continue to expand past scripted television shows and movies to events, branded social-media content, and Reese’s Book Club — all of which highlight women-centered stories.</p>



<p>Asked about her experience as an early adopter of new technologies and the potential of artificial intelligence, Witherspoon reflected on the opportunities she seized by embracing social media and streaming before other industry leaders. “You can’t fear the future,” Witherspoon said. “You have to jump in and jump in in a messy way.”</p>



<p>When it comes to adopting technologies like AI, perfection is impossible. “There are a lot of perfectionists in this room,” Witherspoon said. “You have to be perfect to get here. You don’t have to be perfect to get out of here.”</p>



<p>The crowd’s enthusiasm was on display during the Q&amp;A session. Among the top questions: “Does your company have summer internships?”</p>



<p>Witherspoon smiled. “It does!”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
					
		
		
		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">419438</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>More free trade or time to give tariffs a chance? It was up for debate.</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/more-free-trade-or-time-to-give-tariffs-a-chance-it-was-up-for-debate/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 10 Nov 2025 17:09:59 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Work & Economy]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Business]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[U.S. Politics]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[World Politics]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=419418</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[More free trade or time to give tariffs a chance? It was up for debate.]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-overlay has-uncropped-image"
	style=" --min-height: 66.69921875vw;"
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/business-economy/"
		>
			Work &amp; Economy		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		More free trade or time to give tariffs a chance? It was up for debate.	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Panel on tariffs debate HBS." class="wp-image-419420" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1980.HBS-Trade.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Moderator Debora Spar (from left) Oren Cass, Katherine Tai, Robert Lawrence, and Lawrence Summers.</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Clea Simon	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Correspondent		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-10">
			November 10, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Economists, experts face off over nation’s experiences with both since post-World War II era		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Would Americans be better off if the nation shifted from its post-World War II free-trade approach to one that favors higher tariffs?</p>



<p>That was the question posed to those attending an Institute for Business in Global Society debate on Wednesday evening, hours after the Supreme Court heard arguments in a highly anticipated case challenging the global tariffs imposed by the Trump administration.</p>



<p>For the audience’s part, 30 percent favored a change while 70 percent did not. The debaters were, by design, more evenly split.</p>



<p>Arguing for “yes” were Oren Cass, founder of the think tank American Compass and author of “The New Conservatives,” and Katherine Tai, J.D. ’01, a lawyer, former U.S. trade representative under President Joe Biden, and currently a fellow at the Institute of Politics at the Harvard Kennedy School.</p>



<p>Taking the opposing side were Robert Lawrence, the Albert L. Williams Professor of International Trade and Investment at the Kennedy School, and Lawrence Summers, the Charles W. Eliot University Professor, former University president, and former U.S. treasury secretary.</p>



<p>Much of the discussion centered on issues of balance and fairness.</p>



<p>Cass called the current system “very imbalanced” and “not best for prosperity.”</p>



<p>Lawrence later pointed out that perfect economic balance is only achieved in a barter economy, where one good is directly traded for another. He concluded: “Nothing is going to guarantee balance.”</p>



<p>Cass went on to argue that the premise that free trade always promotes prosperity is “essentially wrong” and dismissed the idea that trade imbalances are self-correcting. Stressing that some kind of intervention is needed, he said “strategic tariffs are going to be higher tariffs.”</p>



<p>Summers, in response, focused on the unequal impact of tariffs. Pointing out that people with less money are more likely to spend on goods, while those with more spend on services, he argued that tariffs function as a regressive tax, forcing the poor to spend more while the rich reap the benefits.</p>



<p>“Using tariffs as a way to try to correct trade imbalances is using the wrong instrument,” he said.</p>



<p>Speaking as a onetime history major, Tai sought to put the debate in context.</p>



<p>After World War II, she said, the U.S. went from a highly regulated New Deal-influenced economy, which lasted several decades, to a neo-liberal Reagan-era world of free-trade agreements, where the perception is “government is part of the problem.”</p>



<p>Making the case for tariffs, she pointed out that the current free-trade era has seen two major economic crises — one sparked by the subprime mortgages collapse in 2008-2009 and the other, in 2020-2021, by the pandemic supply-chain breakdown.</p>



<p>“Do you feel like the promise of the neoliberal era … is delivering the results it’s supposed to?” she asked.</p>



<p>The opposition was quick to counter claims of damage brought by tariffs.</p>



<p>Bringing up levies on imported steel in response, Summers noted that&nbsp;tariffs may help U.S. steelworkers by making imports more expensive, but it will hurt those employed by companies that make products with steel — and there are far more of them.</p>



<p>Instead, he argued that trade with allies and “good mercantilism” should be goals.</p>



<p>However, Summers’ pivot to discussing trade agreements as a means of cementing alliances triggered a conversation on the case of China.</p>



<p>Cass recalled how China was brought into the World Trade Organization in 2001 — a move <a href="https://www.politico.com/news/2021/12/09/china-wto-20-years-524050">some analysts</a> say helped it become a trading powerhouse, in part due to unfair practices, and resulted in a loss of U.S. manufacturing jobs.</p>



<p>Cass argued that using tariffs now to confront the global giant is “the only rational perspective.”</p>



<p>Speaking specifically of the auto industry, and China’s place atop the electric vehicle market, he said: “There are only two options — allow China to dominate or confront them.”</p>



<p>At stake are real lives, Tai stressed. Acknowledging that she feels uneasy in today’s economy, she cast the problem as widespread.</p>



<p>“When we’re talking about an economy, we’re talking about the people in the economy,” she said. “Consumers spending money at the market are workers, who have to have a job, who have to get paid a wage, who have the right to advocate for themselves and bargain in the marketplace of the workplace to better those wages.”</p>



<p>With free trade, she concluded, “productivity has gone up, but wages have not kept pace.”</p>



<p>The debate turned to how tariffs are currently used. Cass acknowledged the apparent contradiction of raising levies on allies such as Canada, but argued that such moves are simply “a bargaining tool.”</p>



<p>But Lawrence said tariffs are actually ineffectual as policy tools.</p>



<p>&nbsp;“If you want more jobs, it’s more efficient to subsidize production,” he said. “If you want to raise revenue, it’s more efficient to have a broadly based sale tax than to use a tariff.”</p>



<p>Considering what could replace the current system, Tai drew on her experience in the Biden administration to point out how both Biden and Trump saw a use for tariffs. The differences in their approaches are so great, however, that they have colored discussion about the utility of tariffs as part of the nation’s foreign policy.</p>



<p>At the end of the evening, a final vote on the proposal showed at least a few minds had been changed, with a final count of 32 percent in favor and 68 percent opposed.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
					
		
		
		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">419418</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>No one knows the answer, and that’s the point</title>
