		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		No one knows the answer, and that’s the point	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Mansi Srivastava giving a lecture." class="wp-image-419408" height="945" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_022.jpg?resize=1680%2C945" width="1680" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_022.jpg?resize=608,342 608w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_022.jpg?resize=784,441 784w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_022.jpg?resize=1024,576 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_022.jpg?resize=1200,675 1200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_022.jpg?resize=1488,837 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_022.jpg?resize=1680,945 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Mansi Srivastava.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Veasey Conway/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Kermit Pattison	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-10">
			November 10, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			8 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			‘Genuinely Hard Problems’ pilots novel approach to scientific education		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>A few weeks after their arrival at the College, 15 first-year students settled into chairs for an unusual class — one with no answers.</p>



<p>The brainchild of Dean of Science Jeff Lichtman, “Genuinely Hard Problems in Science” explores mysteries of the natural world that have stubbornly resisted the best efforts to crack them. How did life emerge from non-biological matter? How did single cells evolve into complex life? What mechanisms drive the aging process — and can they be reversed? What is the relationship between brain anatomy and mental illness?</p>



<p>In this class, there is no assumption that scientific authorities are right. Rather than training conformists who follow their elders down well-worn routes — and possibly to dead ends — the instructors want out-of-the-box thinkers who can blaze new pathways.</p>



<p>“What we’re trying to do,” explained <a href="https://lichtmanlab.fas.harvard.edu/people/jeff-lichtman">Lichtman</a>, “is transform science, 15 students at a time.”</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-harvard-quote harvard-quote has-purple-color is-style-sand" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);--primary-page-color-bright:var(--color-purple-bright);--primary-page-color-text:var(--color-purple-dark);--primary-page-color-ui:var(--color-purple);--primary-page-color-reverse-background:var(--color-purple);--primary-page-color-reverse-text:var(--color-white);--primary-page-color-reverse-ui:var(--color-white)"><blockquote class="wp-block-quote"><p>&#8220;What we’re trying to do is transform science, 15 students at a time.&#8221;</p><cite>Jeff Lichtman</cite></blockquote></div>



<h5 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-a-new-narrative-for-undergraduate-education">‘A new narrative for undergraduate education’</h5>



<p>“Genuinely Hard Problems,” or GHP as it’s become known in the division, seeks to pilot a new approach to a scientific landscape that has been reshaped by technology.&nbsp;</p>



<p>Traditional scientific education is designed to develop expertise in one discipline. But today anybody with a smartphone can instantly retrieve vast amounts of scientific information. Artificial Intelligence performs some tasks with speed and accuracy no human can match.</p>



<p>“Depth of expertise and knowledge in the traditional sense are far less valuable perhaps than they once were,” said <a href="https://www.physics.harvard.edu/people/facpages/mccarty">Logan McCarty</a>, associate dean of science education, who is co-teaching the class with Lichtman. “If you need to learn a specific task or a specific concept, you now can turn to ChatGPT.”</p>



<p>Indoctrination within one narrow discipline can confine a scientist, McCarty noted — and lead them into blind alleys from which they cannot escape. Indeed, the historian of science and philosopher Thomas Kuhn ’43, Ph.D. ’49, famously described scientific training as “a narrow and rigid education, probably more so than any other except perhaps in orthodox theology.”</p>



<p>“If you spend a 10-year career learning a specific skill and a specific task and becoming an expert in something, you tend to not want to jettison all of that knowledge and training,” said McCarty, a chemist who also teaches physics and applied math. “That’s where the dogma can actually trap you inside a particular way of thinking.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?w=1024" alt="Jeff Lichtman (left) with Logan McCarty." class="wp-image-419563" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg 1680w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?resize=1536,1025 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_335.jpeg?resize=1488,993 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1680px) 100vw, 1680px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Jeff Lichtman (left) with Logan McCarty.</figcaption></figure>



<p>The seminar’s solution: catching first-years before their minds become shaped by orthodoxy. Lichtman and McCarty hope to instill students with a multidisciplinary, expansive outlook — thus emboldening them to peer over the walls of any maze.</p>



<p>“This class is sort of the tip of the iceberg of what we hope is a big reimagining of undergraduate science education,” said McCarty. “In some ways, this is about creating a new narrative for undergraduate education in the sciences. These students are our first cohort.”</p>



<p>About 160 incoming students applied for spots in the seminar. Fifteen were selected for the fall cohort, and another 15 will take it in the spring. Next year, Lichtman and McCarty will offer the class again and add sophomore programming, allowing current students to continue their journeys, possibly with advisers.</p>



<p>Genuinely Hard Problems are challenges that have stumped scientists despite decades of research. Solving them is not simply a matter of continuing to hammer away with current approaches. Instead, solutions require conceptual breakthroughs.</p>



<p>“It’s a very strange education because you’re not teaching kids facts,” Lichtman said. “You’re teaching them things that are not known. There is no textbook about the unanswered questions in science.”</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-harvard-quote harvard-quote has-purple-color is-style-sand" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);--primary-page-color-bright:var(--color-purple-bright);--primary-page-color-text:var(--color-purple-dark);--primary-page-color-ui:var(--color-purple);--primary-page-color-reverse-background:var(--color-purple);--primary-page-color-reverse-text:var(--color-white);--primary-page-color-reverse-ui:var(--color-white)"><blockquote class="wp-block-quote"><p>&#8220;You’re teaching them things that are not known. There is no textbook about the unanswered questions in science.&#8221;</p><cite>Jeff Lichtman</cite></blockquote></div>



<h5 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-learning-to-attack-problems">Learning to attack problems</h5>



<p>Each week, GHP invites guest lecturers to class — Harvard faculty who conduct research into unsolved problems. Michael Segel, assistant professor of stem cell and regenerative biology, outlined the mysteries of why we age. Dan Freed, Shiing-Shen Chern Professor of Mathematics, presented the Millennium Prize Problems, a series of unsolved mathematical challenges. Dimitar Sasselov, Phillips Professor of Astronomy, and Adam Cohen, professor of chemistry, chemical biology and physics, presented the enigma of the origins of life on Earth. At the end of the semester, each student will pick a hard problem to study, possibly for the remainder of their undergraduate years, and instructors will match them with a mentor.</p>



<p>The course description intrigued Adam Stone, a first-year from Charlotte, North Carolina. “I thought that this class was really exciting for couple of reasons,” he said. “One is that you get a taste of all these different fields — biology, physics, math. I think the idea of trying to tackle something that no one knows the answer to is really exciting.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large is-style-default"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?w=1024" alt="" class="wp-image-419409" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/092425_Seminar_360.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Belen Franco de la Matta (center left), Mieti Adi, Lucille Nomaguchi-Long, and Rosa Kooijmans.</figcaption></figure>



<p>Stone also was driven by personal motivation: He has Tourette syndrome, a neurological disorder that causes sudden, involuntarily movements. Like many other brain conditions, it remains poorly understood. “If you understand the framework of how to tackle these unsolved problems, you can apply that to so many things that that can really make a difference in people’s lives,” he said.</p>



<p>Likewise, Belén Franco de la Matta, from Northvale, New Jersey, was drawn by the opportunity to survey a broad array of sciences and work closely with a mentor. “Personally, I’m very interested in physics and mathematics, but at the same time, there’s so many valuable things that we can learn from other topics, such as biology or computer science,” she said. “In class, we’ve been talking about how in order to solve difficult problems, sometimes you can tackle them with the ways of thinking from other fields.”</p>



<h5 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-black-holes-and-black-boxes">Black holes and black boxes</h5>



<p>In late September, <a href="https://galison.scholars.harvard.edu/">Peter Galison</a>, Joseph Pellegrino&nbsp;University Professor of History of Science and of Physics, gave the students a primer on his <a href="https://bhi.fas.harvard.edu/">research into black holes</a>. The discussion explored theoretical questions of how science balances prediction, explanation, and understanding.</p>



<p>“You said, in the beginning of your lecture, that the majority of things in science are a black box,” said Lucille Nomaguchi-Long ’29. “Do you think we’ll ever get to a point where we will be able to uncover all those black boxes, or even, fully understand one black box?”</p>



<p>We can go a long way toward understanding natural phenomena, answered Galison, but we also must resign ourselves that much will remain beyond our comprehension. “There’s almost no branch of science, none, that we can understand completely,” he said.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-harvard-quote harvard-quote has-purple-color is-style-sand" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);--primary-page-color-bright:var(--color-purple-bright);--primary-page-color-text:var(--color-purple-dark);--primary-page-color-ui:var(--color-purple);--primary-page-color-reverse-background:var(--color-purple);--primary-page-color-reverse-text:var(--color-white);--primary-page-color-reverse-ui:var(--color-white)"><blockquote class="wp-block-quote"><p>&#8220;There’s almost no branch of science, none, that we can understand completely.&#8221;</p><cite>Peter Galison</cite></blockquote></div>



<p>Then Lichtman interjected another perspective: Often our understanding is just a “gloss” — a superficial explanation that distracts us from the more important goal of making accurate predictions.</p>



<p>“The ego of a person cares more about understanding than prediction,” said Lichtman, Jeremy R. Knowles Professor of Molecular and Cellular Biology. “But to make the world work, all that matters, I would argue, is prediction.”</p>



<h5 class="wp-block-heading" id="h-finding-joy-in-the-questions">Finding joy in the questions</h5>



<p>A week later, the class took on yet another unsolved problem: How does genetic code translate into biology?</p>



<p><a href="https://www.oeb.harvard.edu/people/mansi-srivastava">Mansi Srivastava</a>, co-chair of the Department of Organismic and Evolutionary Biology, gave the lecture, recounting how a burst of optimism swept through her field when the first genomes were sequenced in the early years of the new millennium. People imagined the genome as a “blueprint” — a plan that could easily be translated into physiology and behavior. Those hopes were dashed when it became clear that the matter was far more complex. “We thought we would be able to solve it 25 years ago,” she said, “and we have been deeply humbled.”</p>



<p>Today, entire genomes can be sequenced, and scientists are beginning to decipher how the expression of genes can be modified by epigenetic effects during the lifetime of the organism. Despite those advances, we remain unable to fully translate genotype into phenotype. “Maybe this idea of a blueprint being some perfect instruction was never true,” Srivastava said. “Maybe we carried the analogy too far in thinking about genetics and development.”</p>



<p>Standing beside the projection screen, the professor posed a question to the class.</p>



<p>“Say I give you all the information — I can tell you how the environment impacts things, I can tell you all the epigenetic marks. Do you think even then we’d be able to predict the phenotype? Or is it hopeless?”</p>



<p>“Maybe some of it, yeah,” answered Rosa Kooijmans. “Not all of it.”</p>



<p>&nbsp;“I like your light of hope,” responded Srivastava. “Because if you don’t have hope, then we might as well go home.”</p>



<p>Her advice to students: Spend time thinking carefully about how to define your questions of interest. The road will be long, so learn to find joy in the questions.</p>



<p>“You find your path, because that’s the big task that I want you to solve in the next four years,” said Srivastava. “Or maybe in the next 30 years of your careers.”</p>



<p><a id="_msocom_1"></a></p>
</div>

					<description><![CDATA[Exhibit peels back layers to reveal raw expression in monochrome]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-overlay has-uncropped-image"
	style=" --min-height: 66.68vw;"
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/arts-humanities/"
		>
			Arts &amp; Culture		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		‘A love letter to drawing’	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="" class="wp-image-419293" height="1667" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?w=2500" width="2500" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg 6000w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=2048,1365 2048w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0051.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 6000px) 100vw, 6000px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Niles Singer/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Anna Lamb	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-10">
			November 10, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Exhibit peels back layers to reveal raw expression in monochrome		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>At Harvard Art Museums, paintings by renowned artists such as Pablo Picasso, John Singer Sargent, Edgar Degas, Piet Mondrian, and Georges Seurat capture viewers’ attention with their rich colors and practiced brush strokes. A fall exhibition strips down the styles that made these artists, and others, famous to expose raw expression and experimentation in the most basic form of composition — drawing.</p>



<p>“<a href="https://harvardartmuseums.org/exhibitions/6465/sketch-shade-smudge-drawing-from-gray-to-black">Sketch, Shade, Smudge: Drawing from Gray to Black</a>” showcases around 120 works spanning the 19th to 21st centuries all done in a combination of drawing mediums — chalk, charcoal, graphite, and crayon.</p>



<p>“It’s a love letter to drawing,” said <a href="https://harvardartmuseums.org/about/staff/66">Penley Knipe</a>, the Philip and Lynn Straus Senior Conservator of Works of Art on Paper. “We have an amazing drawing collection here. It’s one of the best in the country, maybe in the world.”</p>



<p>The exhibition was curated by Knipe, as well as Division of European and American Art curator <a href="https://harvardartmuseums.org/about/staff/32">Miriam Stewart</a>. The pair spent over a year carefully choosing works that would not only show off the museums’ collection of rarely seen works but also demonstrate the versatility of simple tools in the hands of artists.</p>



<p>“Some of our favorite drawings are up in this exhibition,” Knipe said during a recent tour of the gallery. She pointed to a large Degas charcoal drawing last brought out for exhibition in <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2005/04/all-his-pretty-ones/">2005</a>.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-jetpack-image-compare"><div class="juxtapose" data-mode="horizontal"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" id="419295" src="https://i0.wp.com/news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0031.jpg?ssl=1" alt="" width="6000" height="4000" class="image-compare__image-before"/><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" id="419296" src="https://i0.wp.com/news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0032.jpg?ssl=1" alt="" width="6000" height="4000" class="image-compare__image-after"/></div><figcaption>&#8220;After the Bath, Woman Drying Herself&#8221; by Hilaire-Germain-Edgar Degas.</figcaption></figure>



<p>“This drawing by Degas was in perilous condition and lived up in the conservation lab for decades because it’s on tracing paper, which is super thin, and it was then adhered to a thicker board,” Knipe noted. “It needed to come off because it was starting to fracture.”</p>



<p>All the materials in the exhibition, including the charcoal drawings, are fragile. As a conservator, Knipe played a key role in making sure the works on view were viable to be framed and hung. This process, she said, lent itself to better understanding the works — what makes them unique and what makes them alike. She noted charcoal’s ability to be “intensely rich and velvety, or delicately gray and suggestive” while graphite — the material in pencils — is “slippery, shiny, and easy to erase.” Crayon, Knipe said, “is deeply black and waxy, whereas chalk can be crumbly and diffuse.”</p>



<div class="wp-block-columns alignwide is-layout-flex wp-container-core-columns-is-layout-28f84493 wp-block-columns-is-layout-flex">

<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="" class="wp-image-419301" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg 5958w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=2048,1365 2048w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/102325_Sketch_Shade_Smudge_0011.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 5958px) 100vw, 5958px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">A display case of materials that features graphite from the collection. </p></figcaption></figure>
</div></div></div></section>
</div>
</div>



<p>In addition to exploring artists’ materials, “Sketch, Shade, Smudge,”as the name implies, also explores the manipulation of those materials and how artists use them, alter them, and play with them.</p>



<p>The exhibition shows how pressure applied to graphite can bring out its shimmery effect, and scraping away waxy layers of crayon can reveal stark highlights. Beyond the works hanging in the gallery, Knipe and Stewart also tried their hand at some of the altering techniques themselves — recording their attempts. Links to those videos can be found throughout the exhibition, and on the Harvard Art Museums’ <a href="https://www.instagram.com/harvardartmuseums/?hl=en">Instagram account</a>.</p>



<p>For erasing a lighter material like charcoal, graphite, or chalk, Knipe said they found that the historical way to do it — with bread — is actually most effective. “So you take a loaf of French bread, you go into the middle, and you kind of roll it into a stump shape.”</p>



<p>For those wanting to try their hand at some of the techniques seen in the latter half of the exhibition, staff have set up an area with stools, paper, and pencils.</p>



<p>“It’s set up as a 19th-century academic drawing academy where you would come and you would draw from the nude. You would draw from other arts. You would draw from sculpture. You would draw still lives — skulls and feet, plaster casts and things,” Knipe said. “And so one of the things that we hope is that people come, they see all of the drawings, and they realize, ‘I kind of want to try my hand at this.’”</p>



<p>She also noted that it’s best not to bring any bread into the gallery, and instead opt for the rubber erasers provided.</p>



<p><em>“Sketch, Shade, Smudge: Drawing from Gray to Black” is on display in the Special Exhibitions Gallery through Jan. 18. The Harvard Art Museums are free to all and open 10 a.m.-5 p.m. Tuesday through Sunday.</em></p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>‘Exploring everything’ leads to Rhodes</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/exploring-everything-leads-to-rhodes/</link>

										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below has-uncropped-image"
	style=" --min-height: 66.69921875vw;"
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Fajr Khan." class="wp-image-419341" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/110525_Rhodes_Pakistan_0040-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Niles Singer/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/campus-community/"
		>
			Campus &amp; Community		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		‘Exploring everything’ leads to Rhodes	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Kermit Pattison	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-07">
			November 7, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Fajr Khan to represent Pakistan, plans career in clinical psychology		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Fajr Khan has learned the value of small things — giving somebody a meal, providing a safe place to sleep, or being fully engaged during a conversation with a lonely person.</p>



<p>Most her volunteer work with homeless people has been done quietly. But now the senior is trying to grasp a very different experience that is not so anonymous: winning a Rhodes Scholarship.</p>



<p>“It still hasn’t really settled in,” Khan said with a laugh. “It just feels very not real. There’s like this level of disbelief. But it’s very cool and exciting.”</p>



<p>Khan was chosen as one of two Rhodes Scholars representing Pakistan. She will spend two years at Oxford University, where she hopes to complete a master’s degree in clinical neuroscience, psychology, or psychiatry.</p>



<p>Afterward, she hopes to pursue a Ph.D. in clinical psychology and a career that combines research and working with patients.</p>



<p>“There’s a lot of stigma around mental health and a lot of misconceptions,” she said. “A lot of people consider it a rich man’s disease, but the data shows that people in vulnerable situations in poverty are some of the people with the most debilitating conditions and the ones who need mental health care. The fact that this Rhodes committee from Pakistan showed this commitment to taking mental health seriously is absolutely amazing — and very encouraging.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--24);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--24)"><blockquote><p>“I think the biggest way that you can make an immediate, direct impact is through human connection, with human empathy.”</p></blockquote></figure>



<p>Khan calls herself a “classic international kid.” Born in Pakistan, she moved to Amsterdam as a toddler so her mother could begin a Ph.D. program in economics. The family remained in the Netherlands while her mother and father worked in the energy sector.</p>



<p>She attended international schools and grew up speaking English, Urdu, and Dutch. When she was 16, the family moved back to Pakistan, and she enrolled in an international school in Lahore.</p>



<p>At Harvard, she is studying neuroscience with a secondary in philosophy — and many forays into other subjects, such the history of science, creative writing, lyric poetry, and global health.</p>



<p>“If you look at my transcript, you would not be able to tell what my concentration was until my third year,” she said with a chuckle. “Probably because I was very much one of those ‘explore everything!’ people.”</p>



<p>Khan is particularly interested in public health. In 2024, she did a summer internship with Gavi, The Vaccine Alliance in Geneva, Switzerland. Last January, she started working as a research assistant at a clinic for mothers with postpartum traumatic stress disorders at Massachusetts General Hospital.</p>



<p>She has been active in the Phillips Brooks House Association and volunteering at the Harvard Square Homeless Shelter, a student-run operation in the basement of&nbsp;University Lutheran Church. She has served meals, cleaned bathrooms, and worked directly with clients alongside a case manager.</p>



<p>Later she became a supervisor for overnight shifts. Now she serves as the shelter’s policy director.</p>



<p>“A lot of the time, issues can feel so big and all-encompassing, and it can be difficult trying to figure out where to start,” she said. “I think the biggest way that you can make an immediate, direct impact is through human connection, with human empathy. I’ve grown up in so many different places — different languages, different cultures — and one-on-one, human connection is something that I remember the most.”</p>



<p>When Khan made the shortlist of Rhodes finalists, she flew home to Pakistan for two days of social events and interviews.</p>



<p>On the final day, the candidates were called into interviews, one by one. The selection committee deliberated briefly and emerged to announce the two winners. Typically, at least one — and sometimes both — attend universities in Pakistan, so Khan figured the odds were not in her favor.</p>



<p>“They came out with a piece of paper, and they called my name,” she said. “I was just in complete disbelief. I remember I got up from my chair, but then I was like, ‘Wait, I don’t even know why I’m standing!’”</p>



<p>Khan phoned her parents and told them she was done and ready to be picked up — but nothing about winning.</p>



<p>Her parents drove up in front of the grand house where the event was hosted.</p>



<p>“I came downstairs, opened the door, and sat in the car,” she recalled. “Then I said, ‘Guess who’s a Rhodes Scholar!’”</p>

					<description><![CDATA[Brittney Griner, Spike Lee, and other honorees lauded for contributions to Black culture, scholarship, and civic life ]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-overlay"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">

			class="article-header__category"

		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		7 awarded W.E.B. Du Bois Medals	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Henry Louis Gates Jr" class="wp-image-419369" height="945" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Gates.jpg?resize=1680%2C945" width="1680" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Gates.jpg?resize=608,342 608w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Gates.jpg?resize=784,441 784w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Gates.jpg?resize=1024,576 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Gates.jpg?resize=1200,675 1200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Gates.jpg?resize=1488,837 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Gates.jpg?resize=1680,945 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Henry Louis Gates Jr. </p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Niles Singer/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Christy DeSmith	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-07">
			November 7, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Brittney Griner, Spike Lee, and other honorees lauded for contributions to Black culture, scholarship, and civic life		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Ballet dancer Misty Copeland, WNBA star Brittney Griner, and filmmaker Shelton Jackson “Spike” Lee were among the honorees at this year’s W.E.B. Du Bois Medal ceremony. The event, organized annually by the <a href="https://hutchinscenter.fas.harvard.edu/">Hutchins Center for African &amp; African American Research</a>, recognizes changemakers for their lasting contributions to Black culture, scholarship, and civic life.</p>



<p>Also presented with medals were South Carolina Rep. James E. Clyburn, artist Amy Sherald, and entrepreneur George E. Johnson, whose products revolutionized Black hair care in mid-century America. The politician and human rights activist Marielle Franco was honored posthumously. Franco, an elected member of Rio de Janeiro’s City Council who was assassinated in 2018, became the award’s first Brazilian recipient.</p>



<section class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-image-carousel alignfull carousel carousel--images"><div aria-labelledby="heading-d545388a-0528-4c7f-9f1a-3879ec2ad049" class="carousel__wrapper splide"><div class="carousel__track splide__track"><div class="carousel__list splide__list">
<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Misty Copeland " class="wp-image-419371" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Copland_b4d513.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Misty Copeland.</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Brittney Griner." class="wp-image-419372" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Griner_ecf4d7.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Brittney Griner.</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Spike Lee" class="wp-image-419373" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Spike-Lee_f62e20.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Spike Lee.</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Amy Sherald." class="wp-image-419374" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Sherad_190788.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Amy Sherald.</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="James E. Clyburn." class="wp-image-419375" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Clyburn1_d61c43.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">James E. Clyburn.</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="George E. Johnson" class="wp-image-419392" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2500_Du_Bois_0836.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">George E. Johnson.</p></figcaption></figure>
</div></div></div></section>



<p>Honorees are said to follow in the footsteps of Du Bois, a towering scholar, author, and activist who became the first Black person to earn a Harvard Ph.D. in 1895. “Each of our seven esteemed medalists embodies this brilliant and courageous man’s dedication to excellence in the arts, in business, philanthropy, and public service — and his determination to fight racism and ethnic hatred wherever they raise their pernicious, ugly heads,” said <a href="https://hutchinscenter.fas.harvard.edu/henry-louis-gates-jr">Henry Louis Gates Jr.</a>, Alphonse Fletcher University Professor and director of the Hutchins Center.</p>



<p>Celebrating their contributions at Sanders Theatre was an enthusiastic crowd of family, friends, students, and community members. <a href="https://hutchinscenter.fas.harvard.edu/people/glenn-h-hutchins">Glenn H. Hutchins</a>, the donor who also chairs the Hutchins Center’s National Advisory Board, shared that Copeland had met earlier with College students involved with the Harvard dance scene. Griner and Lee spent the afternoon with players from the women’s and men’s basketball teams.</p>



<p>“You are society’s future leaders, culture-shapers, and role models,” Hutchins said to the students in the audience. “We expect some of you will be on the stage in the future receiving one of these awards.”</p>



<p>Some of the ceremony’s most powerful moments called back to the medal’s namesake. Sherald, well-known for her <a href="https://npg.si.edu/learn/classroom-resource/michelle-obama">richly colored portrait</a> of former first lady Michelle Obama, was in graduate school when she discovered Du Bois’ <a href="https://www.loc.gov/pictures/collection/anedub/dubois.html">“Exhibit of American Negroes”</a> for the 1900 Paris Exhibition. The display, awarded its own medal by organizers of the World’s Fair, wielded photography and data visualization against racist stereotypes. &nbsp;</p>



<p>“I recognized something of myself in what he was doing — using images to affirm the beauty, intellect, and complexity of Black life,” Sherald recalled. “In that moment, I understood that the work that I was beginning to make — portraiture rooted in grace, in stillness, in the interior lives of Black people — was part of the same conversation.”</p>



<p>Copeland, the first Black dancer promoted to principal in American Ballet Theater’s 85-year history, also evoked the power of representation. Projected behind her was a photo of her confetti-filled final bow, taken just last month, following 25 years with the New York company.</p>



<p>“Standing in that space, I realized that representation isn’t only about being seen,” Copeland said. “It’s about what seeing makes possible. It’s about transforming a tradition, expanding who’s invited to the table, and keeping that door open wide enough for generations to follow.”</p>



<div class="wp-block-columns alignwide are-vertically-aligned-top is-layout-flex wp-container-core-columns-is-layout-28f84493 wp-block-columns-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-column is-vertically-aligned-top is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?w=1024" alt="Allison Feaster " class="wp-image-419379" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Alison-Fiester.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Allison Feaster.</figcaption></figure>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-column is-vertically-aligned-top is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?w=1024" alt="Glenn H. Hutchins " class="wp-image-419380" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Glenn-Hutchins.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Glenn H. Hutchins.</figcaption></figure>
</div>
</div>



<p>The event doubled as a showcase for the Harvard community’s talent and sense of unity. Hopi Hoekstra, Edgerley Family Dean of the Faculty of Arts and Sciences, took the stage to share a chapter from Du Bois’ “The Souls of Black Folk” (1903). Brandon Terry, John L. Loeb Associate Professor of the Social Sciences, read from Du Bois’ 1909 text “John Brown.” Sarah Lewis, John L. Loeb Associate Professor of the Humanities and Associate Professor of African and African American Studies, excerpted “The Criteria for Negro Art” (1926).</p>



<p>Retired WNBA player and former Crimson basketball <a href="https://gocrimson.com/news/2019/9/9/9_9_2019_12958">MVP</a> Allison Feaster ’98, currently a vice president with the Boston Celtics, got personal when presenting Griner with her medal. Feaster called the 6-foot-9, three-time Olympic gold medalist — whom she had never met before Tuesday — “one of my heroes” for building interest in the WNBA while embracing her role as an activist following a 10-month imprisonment in Russia three years ago.</p>



<p>“Your experience reminded us all: When one of us is in pain, none of us can remain silent,” Feaster said. “Through your partnership with <a href="https://www.bringourfamilieshome.org/">Bring Our Families Home</a>, you are fighting for others who are wrongly detained abroad, and we thank you for that.”</p>



<p>Other event highlights included Lee’s comical exhortations to fellow New York Knicks fans and Clyburn’s one-by-one acknowledgments of longtime staffers and loved ones. Johnson, who launched Johnson Products Co. in 1954, is known for elevating joyful pictures of Black America through his 1970s sponsorship of the hit TV show “Soul Train.” His remarks shined brightest concerning his impact as an employer, offering Chicagoans well-paying jobs and benefits.</p>



<p>“As I look back on my life at 98 years old,” he said, to a burst of applause, “I am grateful.”</p>
</div>

					<description><![CDATA[Visiting professor who served 3 decades with Justice Dept. sees urgent need to protect presumption of regularity]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-classic has-colored-heading has-media-on-the-left"
	style=" "
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Andrew Mergen." class="wp-image-419261" height="2232" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Mergen-3.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="(max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Andrew Mergen.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Niles Singer/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Voice of DOJ experience makes case for ‘deference doctrine’	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Visiting professor who served 3 decades with Justice Dept. sees urgent need to protect presumption of regularity		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Christina Pazzanese	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-06">
			November 6, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-right is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-f1f2ed93 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>When a decision by federal agencies or officials is challenged in court, judges implicitly trust that the government is operating in good faith when it explains how and why it made the decision. It’s a tradition of deference known as the presumption of regularity.</p>



<p>Since January, however, a growing number of federal judges have <a href="https://abcnews.go.com/US/judge-hear-arguments-trump-deportation-flights-defied-court/story?id=120446845">sharply criticized</a> Trump administration attorneys over their conduct and <a href="https://www.npr.org/2025/05/02/nx-s1-5385355/perkins-coie-trump-executive-order-law-firms">questioned</a> their truthfulness in several high-profile <a href="https://www.reuters.com/world/trump-asks-us-supreme-court-allow-illinois-troop-deployment-2025-10-17/">immigration enforcement</a> and <a href="https://www.politico.com/news/2025/10/27/pam-bondi-kristi-noem-comments-kilmar-abrego-garcia-00624342">deportation</a> cases, as well as others involving law firms and federal workers. Most recently, a judge in Virginia <a href="https://apnews.com/article/comey-justice-department-trump-a342c81a84c72917a33e6627afa0b35f">raised concerns</a> about the actions of federal prosecutors in the James Comey case.</p>



<p><a href="https://hls.harvard.edu/faculty/andrew-mergen/">Andrew Mergen</a> spent three decades as an attorney at the Department of Justice and is now a visiting assistant clinical professor of law and faculty director of the Emmett Environmental Law &amp; Policy Clinic at <a href="https://hls.harvard.edu/">Harvard Law School</a>. In this edited conversation, he explains why losing the court’s confidence could significantly harm the DOJ.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p><strong>Where does this presumption of regularity come from?</strong></p>



<p>There’s a common-sense notion that we assume that people in our day-to-day lives are doing what they are supposed to do. And in the government, the idea is that we should assume that government people are discharging their duties in an upright and forthright and legal way. Historically, the presumption began as a way of filling in evidentiary gaps, and it applied to both private officials and to corporate officers. The common example is, if a document was filed from a corporation with a corporate seal on it, we assume that it was filed by a person authorized to submit that document. You could call it a deference doctrine, or you could call it a working principle. We assume that people have discharged their official duties in a forthright, honest way, and what that principle means is that the courts will insist on some meaningful evidentiary showing before entertaining doubts about official acts or documents.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-d40dffb7-ff7a-4e26-b736-6cdd0079e14c">
<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-harvard-quote harvard-quote is-style-colored"><blockquote class="wp-block-quote"><p>“The majority of career civil servants are forthright, candid, honest people. They’re trying to do the best for the American people.”</p></blockquote></div>
</div>



<p><strong>Why do the courts offer it to the government?</strong></p>



<p>Because it makes the government more efficient overall. Mostly what we’re talking about are public law disputes. For example, you might challenge a government decision to award a permit or start a ground-disturbing activity. That decision is typically reviewed on the basis of an administrative record that the government submits to the court. So, if the government says we’re going to build this rocket base, and we’ve looked at all the environmental harms and concluded that this is the best way to do this in a way that complies with environmental statutes, and here’s the record that we submit, no one asks: “Is this really the record?” The presumption is that the record is what the government says it is. To be sure, a person could argue that the record is incomplete, but the presumption requires the challenger to make some showing that the record is incomplete before a court will consider the challenge. This makes government more efficient. The presumption of regularity avoids us getting courts tied up in a lot of minutiae. The government says that the record was submitted and the person in charge of putting that together did all the right things and that is the end of the story.</p>



<p><strong>How unusual is it to see judges openly express skepticism about the government’s intentions and veracity?</strong></p>



<p>There’s a presumption of regularity and then there’s also the special duty that governmental lawyers have as officers of the court. I spent 33 years as a Justice Department attorney. I was in court a lot and I understood that every time I stood before a federal judge I had a responsibility as a member of the bar — all lawyers have that — and then I had a special obligation as a member of the federal government. I knew that I had to be candid. I knew that I had a special obligation to be truthful. I owed the court a real explanation of why the government was doing something different or had changed its mind that the judge was rightfully entitled to probe.</p>



<p>Part of what we’re seeing in, for example, the Kilmar Abrego Garcia situation is a real doubt about whether the government is being truthful or hiding the ball. That’s a little bit different from a presumption of regularity. It’s in the same family, but also a bigger problem. If the judge doesn’t believe you, if a judge thinks you’re lying to them or concealing something, you are in a really, really bad place. Historically, the Justice Department has had a very good reputation for credibility, for forthrightness. Any time you talked to a judge or a clerk in the federal court system, someone would invariably say, “I read the government’s brief first because I expect the government’s brief to not be full of hyperbole and to tell the truth.” You’re obligated to zealously represent your client, but Justice Department attorneys have historically tempered that zealousness with their special responsibility for candor and credibility to the courts.</p>



<p><strong>What danger does this doubt voiced by judges pose in the future?</strong></p>



<p>What’s terrifying about this moment is that it will carry over. One of the things that’s really damaging about the way that the administration is treating the courts is that it is going to hurt the credibility of the Justice Department overall. Most cases in federal courts that the Justice Department defends are not things like extraterritorial extradition. They’re boring. And what we worry about is judges saying, “Can I trust the Justice Department in boring cases?” That is going to harm the government overall. </p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
					
		
		
		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">419255</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>Heavy drinking linked to worse strokes</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/heavy-drinking-linked-to-worse-strokes/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[gazettebeckycoleman]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 06 Nov 2025 19:12:56 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Health]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Addiction]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Brain]]></category>

		<category><![CDATA[Research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=419209</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Study finds larger, deeper brain bleeds at an earlier age for patients who have 3 or more alcoholic drinks a day]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below title-above-image centered-image"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/health/"
		>
			Health		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Heavy drinking linked to worse strokes	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Wine poured in glass." class="wp-image-419210" height="992" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/drinking-strokes-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Getty Images</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Mass General Brigham Communications	</p>
				</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-06">
			November 6, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Study finds larger, deeper brain bleeds at an earlier age for patients who have 3 or more alcoholic drinks a day		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>A new study by investigators from&nbsp;<a href="https://www.massgeneralbrigham.org/">Mass General Brigham</a>&nbsp;suggests that heavy alcohol use may lead to more severe brain bleeds and cause long-term brain vessel damage at a younger age. The team’s results, based on patients treated for brain bleeds at Massachusetts General Hospital, are published online in&nbsp;<a href="https://dx.doi.org/10.1212/WNL.0000000000214348" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Neurology</a><em>,</em>&nbsp;the medical journal of the American Academy of Neurology.</p>



<p>“The brain bleed is one of the most lethal and disabling conditions known to human beings,” said corresponding author&nbsp;<a href="https://researchers.mgh.harvard.edu/profile/3296317/Edip-Gurol">Edip Gurol</a>, a clinician investigator in the Mass General Brigham Department of Neurology and associate professor of neurology at Harvard Medical School. “They come on suddenly, cause severe damage, and often leave patients with life-changing disabilities. It’s one of the most difficult conditions to recover from.”</p>



<p>A brain bleed — also called an intracerebral hemorrhage or hemorrhagic stroke — happens when a blood vessel inside the brain bursts. Up to 50 percent of people with this condition die, and 30 percent are severely disabled. Only 20 percent of people who sustain a brain bleed can take care of themselves independently a full year later, Gurol said.</p>



<p>The study used a cohort of 1,600 patients admitted between 2003 and 2019 to Massachusetts General Hospital who had a brain bleed that was not a result of an injury or trauma. They used CT scans to assess the size and location of the brain bleed. They checked MRI scans for signs of damage to the brain’s small blood vessels.</p>



<p>About 7 percent of the brain-bleed patients reported consuming three or more drinks a day. Compared with those who didn’t drink, the people who drank heavily were younger when the brain bleed happened (average age 64 versus 75) and had bleeds that were about 70 percent bigger. They were twice as likely to have a bleed deep inside the brain or one that spread into the brain’s fluid-filled spaces. The researchers saw that an even lower level of alcohol intake, two drinks per day, was significantly associated with younger age of onset of a brain bleed.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--24);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--24)"><blockquote><p>The researchers hypothesize that heavy alcohol use raises blood pressure, damaging the small blood vessels in the brain, making them weak and more likely to leak or burst.</p></blockquote></figure>



<p>People who drank heavily had lower platelet counts (cell fragments that help blood clot) and higher blood pressure when they arrived at the hospital. They were more likely to show evidence of damage in the tiny blood vessels in the brain, which is linked to dementia, memory loss, and walking problems. It’s also a major risk factor for a brain bleed.</p>



<p>The researchers hypothesize that heavy alcohol use raises blood pressure, damaging the small blood vessels in the brain, making them weak and more likely to leak or burst. On top of that, lowered platelet counts make it harder for the body to stop bleeding. Together, these factors greatly increase the risk of a brain bleed.</p>



<p>Drinking less is a key part of stroke and brain health prevention efforts, the researchers said.</p>



<p>“We now know that heavy drinking leads to larger, earlier brain bleeds,” said Gurol. “Minimizing or stopping alcohol use is an important step to lower that risk. Even for people at relatively low brain-bleeding risk, limiting alcohol consumption to no more than three drinks per week may be an effective measure to protect against all types of stroke and to preserve both brain and cardiovascular health.”</p>



<p>This study has some limitations. Alcohol use was self-reported, the number of people who drank heavily was small, it took place at a single hospital, and the cohort investigated was mostly white patients. It also captured only one point in time, so it cannot show cause and effect. Future research with larger, more diverse groups should explore how different levels of alcohol use affect small vessel disease, brain aging, and the risk of blockage-type strokes.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p>This study was funded by National Institutes of Health.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
