		<item>
		<title>Educating the eye</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/educating-the-eye/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Eileen O'Grady]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 06 Nov 2025 19:06:32 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Arts & Culture]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Harvard History]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Humanities]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Visual Arts]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=419175</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Harvard celebrates 150th anniversary of art history department, the nation’s first]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below centered-image"
	style=" "
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Collage of students taking art history courses at Harvard from 2019 and 1944." class="wp-image-419249" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/haa-anniversary-1920b-2.png?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Harvard art history students through the years.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Harvard file photo; Harvard Art Museums Archives </p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/arts-humanities/"
		>
			Arts &amp; Culture		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Educating the eye	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Eileen O’Grady	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-06">
			November 6, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			7 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Harvard celebrates 150th anniversary of art history department, the nation’s first		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>In 1873, Charles Eliot Norton wrote a letter to his cousin, Harvard President Charles William Eliot, to propose what was then a bold idea: add art history courses to the University’s curriculum.</p>



<p>Education, Norton argued, is incomplete without the study of the fine arts. Norton, who had just returned from five years in Europe, had become inspired by the Arts and Crafts movement after hearing art historian John Ruskin lecture at Oxford and conversing with his friend Ralph Waldo Emerson on the boat back to Boston.</p>



<p>“Architecture, sculpture, and painting have been, next to literature, the most important modes of expression of the sentiments, beliefs and opinions of men,” Norton wrote. “They afford evidence, often in a more striking and direct manner than literature itself, of the moral temper and intellectual culture of the various races by whom they have been practiced and thus become the most effective aids to the proper understanding of history.”</p>



<p>His argument was convincing. Thus began Harvard’s <a href="https://haa.fas.harvard.edu/">Department of History of Art and Architecture</a> (originally called the Fine Arts Department), making Harvard the first U.S. university to develop a professorship and a permanent program for art history curriculum. This year the department will mark its 150th anniversary with a conference slated for Nov. 7-8 called “<a href="https://sites.harvard.edu/haa150">The Education of the Eyes: 150 Years of Art History at Harvard</a>.”</p>



<p>Co-hosted by the <a href="https://harvardartmuseums.org/">Harvard Art Museums</a>, the conference will bring together academics, art critics, and curators from across the U.S. and Europe to reflect on the history and legacy of the department, and on the future of the art history field. <a href="https://sites.harvard.edu/haa150/conference-program/">Speakers</a> include former Museum of Modern Art Director Glenn Lowry, art critic Rosalind Krauss, art historian Svetlana Alpers, and New York Times architecture critic Michael Kimmelman.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1980" height="1320" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg" alt="Felipe Pereda." class="wp-image-419189" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/103025_Felipe_Pereda_01.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">The conference comes at a time “in which the visual has more importance than ever in the production of culture,” says Felipe Pereda, director of graduate studies for the department.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Stephanie Mitchell/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>



<p>“I’m so thrilled and happy about the conference that is going to happen,” said <a href="https://haa.fas.harvard.edu/people/felipe-pereda">Felipe Pereda</a>, Fernando Zóbel de Ayala Professor of Spanish Art and director of graduate studies for the department. “For the graduate students, it presents what the future could be and gives them the opportunity to think about how they can shape where the department is moving to, and where art history is moving to, at a moment in which the visual has more importance than ever in the production of culture.”</p>



<p>The conference will include presentations on topics ranging from the relationship between artist and critic, to art that is included versus excluded in the discipline. Pereda, who has been the driving force behind planning the conference, conducted extensive research to piece together the department’s origin story, with documents found in the archives at the Harvard Art Museums, Houghton Library, the University Archives, and University of London’s Warburg Institute.</p>



<p>The event’s title comes from an idea central to an early ethos of Harvard’s Fine Arts Department: the German concept of Augenerziehung, which translates to “education of the eye.”</p>



<p>“The idea that developed was that to be educated wasn’t only going to be in the realm of reading books and reasoning from them. There was something different and deeply salient about grappling with artifacts, with humanly made things,” explained <a href="https://haa.fas.harvard.edu/people/joseph-koerner">Joseph Koerner</a>, chair of the department and Victor S. Thomas Professor of the History of Art and Architecture.</p>



<section class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-image-carousel alignfull carousel carousel--images"><div aria-labelledby="heading-abf2e460-1e64-490a-81da-ce86685c407a" class="carousel__wrapper splide"><div class="carousel__track splide__track"><div class="carousel__list splide__list">
<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Students inside an art workshop in the Fogg Museum in 1932." class="wp-image-419219" height="1206" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=150,122 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=300,243 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=768,622 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1024,830 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1245 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=39,32 39w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=79,64 79w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1488,1206 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/2-RS83927_obj883-VRS244270_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1362 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">An art course takes place inside the Fogg Museum in 1932, back when the curriculum was more hands-on and less “bookish.”</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Harvard Art Museums Archives (unless otherwise noted)</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Students from 1970s or 1980s taking notes and viewing art." class="wp-image-419222" height="994" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=768,513 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=1024,684 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1026 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=1488,994 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/12-RS87609_ArtifactId-51010-VRS253031_scr-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1122 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Students take notes in Warburg Hall.</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Seated students view Pablo Picasso’s “Guernica.”" class="wp-image-419220" height="1120" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=150,113 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=300,226 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=768,578 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1024,771 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1156 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=43,32 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=85,64 85w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1488,1120 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/3-RS87608_obj6385-LEG256976_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1264 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">When Pablo Picasso’s “Guernica” came to Harvard in 1941, students had the opportunity to study it up close.</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Students engage with David Smith’s sculpture “Fish.”" class="wp-image-419221" height="1850" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg 1544w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?resize=121,150 121w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?resize=241,300 241w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?resize=768,955 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?resize=823,1024 823w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1235,1536 1235w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?resize=26,32 26w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?resize=51,64 51w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/11-RS87606_obj18442-LEG263316_pr-1920.jpg?resize=1488,1850 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1544px) 100vw, 1544px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Students engage with David Smith’s “Fish” in 1981.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">© The Estate of David Smith </p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Archival image of Fogg Museum of Art." class="wp-image-419223" height="1047" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=150,106 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=300,211 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=768,540 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=1024,721 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1081 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=45,32 45w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=91,64 91w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=1488,1047 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/13-Fogg-Museum-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1182 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Hunt Hall, which housed the original Fine Arts Department and the Fogg Museum from 1895 to 1925. The building was demolished in 1973.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Harvard University Archives</p></figcaption></figure>
</div></div></div></section>



<p>From the start, the new department was designed to combine intellectual inquiry with practical training. The first two fine arts courses offered, in the 1874-75 school year, were “The History of the Fine Arts and Their Relation to Literature,” taught by Norton, and “Principles of Design,” a studio course taught by painter <a href="https://harvardartmuseums.org/article/the-beginnings-of-art-history-at-harvard-part-2-charles-herbert-moore-john-ruskin-and-the-teaching-of-drawing">Charles Herbert Moore</a>, in which students learned to draw.</p>



<p>“The program initiated students in the practice of drawing — not to become artists, but to understand artists’ creative process. In other words, to educate their eyes,” Pereda said.</p>



<p>This commitment to studying real objects led the University to begin collecting art. In 1895, Harvard founded the Fogg Museum of Art, making its Art History Department the first in the nation to have its own museum. Housed with the Fine Arts Department in Hunt Hall, which was located where Canaday Hall is today, the museum served as a “laboratory for the eye,” where classes often gathered to examine paintings up close.</p>



<p>“It was a laboratory,” Koerner said. “There were instruments, and people studied how paints were made, how lacquer is produced, how bronze was cast. Science and art history, they’re not that different, both are ways of understanding how things came about, how facts are produced.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--24);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--24)"><blockquote><p>“It was not just about learning the difference between a Rembrandt and one of his followers, or about appreciating beautiful things.”</p><cite>Joseph Koerner, department chair</cite></blockquote></figure>



<p>Over time, the emphasis on practicing artmaking dwindled as the field became more “bookish,” Koerner said. The art history department stopped offering drawing courses in the 1960s. By the time Koerner was completing his graduate work and beginning to teach in the 1980s, it was “inconceivable” that a scholar might try learning skills like etching, engraving, or ink-making as part of the research process, he recalled.</p>



<p>But now, those ideas are coming back around.</p>



<p>“Fast-forward to today, the thoughts of 150 years ago seem to me to align again with what people now think the study of art should about: understanding how material objects are made by learning the processes by which they were made and by looking very carefully, using whatever methods best suit your object,” Koerner said. “That’s what a lot of the younger art historians are doing, even to the point of apprenticing themselves to weavers and printmakers.”</p>



<p>Koerner added that training the eye is even more important today, as visual images impact how we understand politics, especially in the new era of AI-produced images and digital falsification. Many HAA courses today, such as “<a href="https://haa.fas.harvard.edu/class/human-20-colloquium-visual-arts">A Colloquium in the Visual Arts</a>,” have “looking labs,” opportunities for classes to go to the Harvard Art Museums to examine objects and discuss them.</p>



<p>Both Pereda and Koerner hope the conference will serve to “rekindle” the close relationship between the department and the Harvard Art Museums and provide a space to celebrate and critically examine the field and its history.</p>



<p>“It’s inspiring to see that 150 years ago, people conceived of a future history of art&nbsp;in such a deep, foundational way,” Koerner said. “It was not just about learning the difference between a Rembrandt and one of his followers, or about appreciating beautiful things. It was really about what education should be ideally; about what it means, say, for undergraduates to learn to look closely at artworks and so do with advanced research ongoing at the University.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Setti Warren." class="wp-image-419086" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111909_mayors_017-1920-warren.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Harvard file photo</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/campus-community/"
		>
			Campus &amp; Community		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Setti Warren honored as lifelong public servant, remembered as bridge builder	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Institute of Politics director, first elected Black mayor in Massachusetts ‘had superpower of knowing how to lift people up’ 		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Christina Pazzanese	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-05">
			November 5, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			6 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-right is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-f1f2ed93 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Setti Warren’s job as director of the Institute of Politics at Harvard Kennedy School involved working with political luminaries and important scholars.</p>



<p>But those who knew him said he was the kind of person who made everyone he met feel like they mattered, especially his students.</p>



<p>“He wanted us to feel like we were valued, and heard, and cared for,” said Summer Tan ’26, IOP vice president. “I remember even when he was still interim director, he had taken the time to know my name and would greet me every time we passed in the hallway, no matter how busy he looked or how important the person he was walking with was.”</p>



<p>Friends, students, faculty, and members of the Harvard community remember the popular IOP director and the first elected Black mayor in Massachusetts as an optimistic bridge builder who did politics the right way and inspired young people with his lifelong love of public service.</p>



<p>Warren died at the age of 55 at his Newton home on Sunday.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-dc3df48c-9c17-4eba-bb31-e15def5cd032">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1920" height="1280" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg" alt="Setti Warren." class="wp-image-419082" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-hks-1.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">&#8220;Setti made public service seem less like a sacrifice than a secular sacrament, a calling that pulls people to be their best selves,&#8221; said Nancy Gibbs, director of the Shorenstein Center.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photo courtesy of Harvard Kennedy School</p></figcaption></figure>
</div>



<p>“Setti had the superpower of knowing how to lift people up and create a shared sense of mission that was joyful.&nbsp;He listened hard, and he understood how to unlock the best in everybody,” said former U.S. Senator John Kerry, a longtime ally.</p>



<p>Warren worked on Kerry’s 2004 presidential campaign and later joined the Massachusetts Democrat’s Senate staff. Kerry, who served as U.S. secretary of state during the Obama administration, called Warren a rare role model in this pugnacious political era.</p>



<p>“I hope that a big part of his legacy will be that he inspired a lot more Setti Warrens to carry on that tradition which our democracy needs so much right now. America needs more people with Setti’s moral compass who do the right thing for the right reasons.”&nbsp;</p>



<p>In an announcement to the Harvard community, Kennedy School Dean Jeremy Weinstein and David Deming, dean of Harvard College, called the news “heartbreaking” and recounted Warren’s nearly four decades of public service, from the military to politics to academia, as “an example to us all.” Colleagues say he was a man of strongly held principles and deep faith who kept a curious and open mind.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--24);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--24)"><blockquote><p>“America needs more people with Setti’s moral compass who do the right thing for the right reasons.”</p><cite>John Kerry</cite></blockquote></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignwide size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1920" height="1280" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg" alt="Notes and flowers outside Setti Warren's Harvard office." class="wp-image-419081" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/setti-warren-door-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Tributes of notes and flowers collect outside of Setti Warren’s office at the Kennedy School.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photo by Nikki Rojas</p></figcaption></figure>



<p>“Setti lived and modeled the values that infuse this School: excellence, enterprise, collegiality and a muscular commitment to service,” said Nancy Gibbs, director of the Shorenstein Center on Media, Politics and Public Policy, where Warren served as executive director in 2018 before joining the IOP in 2022.</p>



<p>“Setti made public service seem less like a sacrifice than a secular sacrament, a calling that pulls people to be their best selves,” she said.&nbsp;</p>



<p>An Iraq War veteran, Warren entered politics in 1995, campaigning for President Bill Clinton&#8217;s re-election. He then went to work in the Clinton administration from 1996 to 2000.</p>



<p>In 2010, Warren, a Democrat and lifelong Newton native, was elected that city’s mayor, serving two terms. He later made two briefs runs at higher office. In 2011, he entered the race for U.S. Senate, a seat won by then-Harvard Law School professor Elizabeth Warren. In 2017, he ran for governor of Massachusetts before coming to Harvard in 2018.</p>



<p>Many recall Warren’s unwavering dedication to his family, his students, and his constituents, along with his generosity of time and spirit.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-71cb63d6-3201-4833-a116-d0b9336a9707">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?w=1024" alt="Aaron Goldman and Setti Warren in Newton City Hall in 2014." class="wp-image-419080" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/AG_Inauguration-day-warren-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Aaron Goldman, now director of the JFK Jr. Forum, with Warren in Newton City Hall in 2014.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photo by Russ Campbell</p></figcaption></figure>
</div>



<p>“He was focused; he was energetic; he was always thinking about what was next and what else [needed] to be done, but he also had so much fun in the various roles that he held,” said Aaron Goldman, director of the JFK Jr. Forum, who began working with Warren in 2005 and held several top leadership roles during Warren’s eight years in Newton City Hall.</p>



<p>“He always wanted to hear about people’s days and hear about people’s lives and always seemed to find a common connection with somebody and something to reminisce about. Even in stressful, challenging moments, which there were plenty of, he always seemed to find the silver lining or the good in whatever we were dealing with,” Goldman said.</p>



<p>Warren is survived by his wife, Tassy Warren, who is <a href="https://developingchild.harvard.edu/people/tassy-warren/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">co-executive director and chief strategy officer</a> at Harvard’s Center on the Developing Child. The couple have two children, Abigail and John. “To have him as a friend and colleague was to have a true champion in your corner,” said Liz Schwartz, the Shorenstein Center’s communications director.</p>



<p>For students, who left flowers and posted notes on his office door, Warren was much more than some school administrator. His office was a warm, welcoming refuge whose door never seemed to close.</p>



<p>Senior Ethan Kelly, a former IOP vice president, felt that Warren was looking out for him and credits Warren with sparking his pursuit of a career in politics.</p>



<p>“He was more than a director to me — and many others. He was a friend, an ally, a parental figure, a mentor, a public servant. He was everything someone ought to be if they choose to pursue a career in public service,” said Kelly.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignwide size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1920" height="1280" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg" alt="Setti Warren." class="wp-image-419085" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/111021_Vets_1818-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Warren, an Iraq War veteran, speaks during a Harvard Veterans Day event in 2021.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Harvard file photo</p></figcaption></figure>



<p>Weinstein said, “Setti always encouraged students from across the political spectrum to run toward politics rather than run away from them, just like he did throughout his life. His students loved him, and he will live on through their legacies of service.”</p>



<p>Longtime friends say whether guiding young people or trying to solve constituents’ problems, Warren really was who he seemed to be.</p>



<p>“Setti cared deeply about others and was genuinely motivated to make the world a better place,” said Brigid O’Rourke, IOP director of communications, who has known Warren for almost 20 years. “That’s not something I say lightly. That’s who he was at his core. A kind and thoughtful man who was invested in his community and wanted the best for everyone, not just those who agreed with him.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
