					<description><![CDATA[Findings could explain why some older adults at risk for the disease decline faster than others]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-split-screen has-light-background has-colored-background has-media-on-the-left"
	style=" "
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Older adults walk briskly in the park." class="wp-image-418971" height="945" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/alzheimers-exercise-1920.jpg?resize=1680%2C945" width="1680" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/alzheimers-exercise-1920.jpg?resize=608,342 608w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/alzheimers-exercise-1920.jpg?resize=784,441 784w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/alzheimers-exercise-1920.jpg?resize=1024,576 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/alzheimers-exercise-1920.jpg?resize=1200,675 1200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/alzheimers-exercise-1920.jpg?resize=1488,837 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/alzheimers-exercise-1920.jpg?resize=1680,945 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/health/"
		>
			Health		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Walking 3,000-5,000 steps a day may delay Alzheimer’s	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Findings could explain why some older adults at risk for the disease decline faster than others		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Mass General Brigham Communications	</p>
				</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-04">
			November 4, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-right is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-f1f2ed93 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Increasing your steps by even a little bit may help slow down the progression of Alzheimer’s disease among people at heightened risk, according to a new study.</p>



<p>In a paper published in&nbsp;<a href="https://www.nature.com/articles/s41591-025-03955-6" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Nature Medicine</a>, Mass General Brigham researchers&nbsp;found that physical activity was associated with slower rates of cognitive decline in older adults with elevated levels of amyloid-beta, a protein associated with Alzheimer’s.</p>



<p>Cognitive decline was delayed by three years on average for people who walked just 3,000-5,000 steps per day, and by seven years in people who walked 5,000-7,500 steps per day. Sedentary individuals had a significantly faster buildup of tau proteins in the brain and more rapid declines in cognition and daily functioning.</p>



<p>“This sheds light on why some people who appear to be on an Alzheimer’s disease trajectory don’t decline as quickly as others,” said senior author&nbsp;<a href="https://www.massgeneral.org/doctors/19278/jasmeer-chhatwal?cmp=nwsref">Jasmeer Chhatwal</a> of the Mass General Brigham&nbsp;Department of Neurology. “Lifestyle factors appear to impact the earliest stages of Alzheimer’s disease, suggesting that lifestyle changes may slow the emergence of cognitive symptoms if we act early.”</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-c80a0083-4224-4090-8e06-d7303e61eb99">
<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p>“Lifestyle changes may slow the emergence of cognitive symptoms if we act early.”</p>
</blockquote>
</div>



<p>The researchers analyzed data from 296 participants aged 50-90 years old in the&nbsp;<a href="https://habs.mgh.harvard.edu/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Harvard Aging Brain Study</a>&nbsp;who were all cognitively unimpaired at the beginning of the study. They used PET brain scans to measure baseline levels of amyloid-beta in plaques and tau in tangles and assessed the participants’ physical activity using waistband pedometers. The participants received annual follow-up cognitive assessments for between two and 14 years (average, 9.3 years), and a subset received repeated PET scans to track changes in tau.</p>



<p>Higher step counts were linked to slower rates of cognitive decline and a slower buildup of tau proteins in participants with elevated baseline levels of amyloid-beta. The researchers’ statistical modeling suggested that most of the physical activity benefits associated with slowing cognitive decline were driven by slower tau buildup. By contrast, in people with low baseline levels of amyloid-beta, there was very little cognitive decline or accumulation of tau proteins over time and no significant associations with physical activity.</p>



<p class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-stats hg-stats has-text-align-undefined" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48)">
<span class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-stats-text hg-stats-text is-style-colored-text has-sanomat-font-family has-stats-text-x-large-font-size">3 years</span>







<span class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-stats-text hg-stats-text has-neue-haas-grotesk-text-font-family has-stats-text-small-font-size">Of delayed cognitive decline on average for those who walk 3,000-5,000 steps daily (7 years for those who get 5,000-7,500 steps)</span>
</p>



<p>“We are thrilled that data from the Harvard Aging Brain Study has helped the field better understand the importance of physical activity for maintaining brain health,” said co-author&nbsp;<a href="https://habs.mgh.harvard.edu/our-team/reisa-sperling-md/" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Reisa Sperling</a>, a neurologist in the Mass General Brigham Department of Neurology and co-principal investigator of the Harvard Aging Brain Study. “These findings show us that it’s possible to build cognitive resilience and resistance to tau pathology in the setting of preclinical Alzheimer’s disease. This is particularly encouraging for our quest to ultimately prevent Alzheimer’s disease dementia, as well as to decrease dementia due to multiple contributing factors.”</p>



<p>Looking ahead, the researchers plan to dive deeper into which aspects of physical activity may be most important, for example exercise intensity and longitudinal activity patterns. They also plan to investigate the biological mechanisms linking physical activity, tau buildup, and cognitive health. Critically, the authors believe that this work may help design future clinical trials that test exercise interventions to slow late-life cognitive decline, especially in individuals who are at heightened risk due to preclinical Alzheimer’s disease.</p>



<p>“We want to empower people to protect their brain and cognitive health by keeping physically active,” said first author&nbsp;<a href="https://www.massgeneral.org/doctors/23351/wai-ying-yau?cmp=nwsref">Wai-Ying Wendy Yau</a>, a cognitive neurologist in the Mass General Brigham Department of Neurology. “Every step counts — and even small increases in daily activities can build over time to create sustained changes in habit and health.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p>This study was funded in part by the National Institutes of Health.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Cold War arms-control pioneers perhaps weren’t peacemakers we thought they were </title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/cold-war-arms-control-pioneers-perhaps-werent-peacemakers-we-thought-they-were/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 04 Nov 2025 18:10:36 +0000</pubDate>

					<description><![CDATA[Nuclear-age historian argues scientists who backed arsenals as deterrent aided military-industrial complex, hampered disarmament]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-classic has-colored-heading has-media-on-the-left"
	style=" "
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Benjamin Wilson." class="wp-image-418989" height="2232" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/1667_Stability_0114.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="(max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Benjamin Wilson. </p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Niles Singer/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Cold War arms-control pioneers perhaps weren’t peacemakers we thought they were 	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Nuclear-age historian argues scientists who backed arsenals as deterrent aided military-industrial complex, hampered disarmament		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Sy Boles	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-04">
			November 4, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			7 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-right is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-f1f2ed93 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Keeping the peace in the Cold War was a matter of MAD, or mutually assured destruction, with both the U.S. and Soviet Union racing to develop and amass ever more deadly weapons to keep the other at bay and maintain an uneasy status quo.</p>



<p>The problem, according to Benjamin Wilson, was the chief proponents of that early brand of arms control, an elite group of science advisers, “wore a progressive face” but ended up<strong> “</strong>protecting existing structures and domestic arrangements, foreclosing the possibility of more radical transformations.”</p>



<p>That’s the argument <a href="https://histsci.fas.harvard.edu/people/benjamin-wilson">Wilson</a>, an associate professor of the history of science, makes in his new book, “<a href="https://www.hup.harvard.edu/books/9780674976085">Strange Stability: How Cold War Scientists Set Out to Control the Arms Race and Ended Up Serving the Military-Industrial Complex</a>.”</p>



<p>In this edited interview, Wilson discusses the doctrine of “strategic stability” and the downside of the American cultural myth of the independent scientist who saves society from itself.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p><strong>The book takes a look at the narrative that grew up around the arms control movement. Can you describe it?</strong></p>



<p>In the 1950s, the U.S. and the Soviet Union started building these big arsenals of intercontinental bombers and ballistic missiles that could carry out devastating attacks very quickly.</p>



<p>But strategists realized that there could be a political or military crisis where tensions are extremely high, and one side gets convinced the other side might attack, so it seems prudent to attack first. Or there could be a mishap, like a blip on a radar screen that looks like an attack.&nbsp;</p>



<p>Strategic stability is a condition where neither side would ever strike first, even if it might ordinarily be tempting to attack. Stability theory says you should put your missiles in hardened silos and submarines where the enemy can’t find them, so even if there is a crisis or an accident, both sides will know that they can retaliate even if they’re attacked first.&nbsp;</p>



<p>The standard narrative from the Cold War says that strategic stability was a natural and inevitable idea and was dictated by the logic of strategy.&nbsp;</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-b6179342-4a1a-4ef6-aca0-3ef58a475319">
<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p>“Many of the arrangements that I uncovered and analyzed in my book were hidden from the public, so it could take a future historian to build an accurate understanding of whatever is going on right now.”</p>
</blockquote>
</div>



<p>The second part of the narrative is about the people who discovered and promoted this idea of strategic stability. These were social and natural scientists who advised and consulted for the government. They created a new field organized around stability that they called arms control.</p>



<p>The narrative says that these arms controllers were independent thinkers whose advice to policymakers was geared toward promoting stability and peace.</p>



<p>In effect, they were a restraint on what President [Dwight] Eisenhower famously called the military-industrial complex, and they were able to serve as a restraint precisely because they were independent scientists and intellectuals.&nbsp;</p>



<p><strong>But you found that’s not the case. What’s missing from that standard story?&nbsp;</strong></p>



<p>What I found in researching my book was that both parts of that Cold War narrative are wrong, or at least incomplete.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-bc545ed8-7a17-43d9-affe-efc9d02be56b">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large is-style-drop-shadow"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="1024" width="683" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?w=683" alt="Book Cover. Stability." class="wp-image-418993" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/11/Stability.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /></figure>
</div>



<p>The first part, the origins of stability, is incomplete.</p>



<p>American strategists were talking about protecting retaliatory weapons before they talked about strategic stability, so it wasn’t the case that they discovered the theory of stability and then used it to formulate concrete strategic policy.</p>



<p>It turns out that throughout the Cold War, strategists applied stability concepts in flexible and sometimes inconsistent ways, often as a justification for policies they preferred for other reasons.&nbsp;</p>



<p>So then, you can ask, what other reasons?</p>



<p>Well, it turns out those scientists were not the independent thinkers we thought they were. They were very much attached. They represented powerful institutions of the national security state, including extremely successful weapons contractors, and the interests of those institutions had a way of sculpting the kinds of stability arguments that the thinkers I studied put forward.</p>



<p>What they always argued for was more research and development to make better weapons. In the book I refer to such figures as R&amp;D elites.</p>



<p><strong>What was the alternative vision if not arms control and strategic stability?&nbsp;</strong></p>



<p>The alternative was disarmament — getting rid of weapons, getting rid of the means of developing and making weapons.</p>



<p>Cold War scientists and arms controllers spent a huge amount of energy arguing that disarmament was unrealistic and utopian because it would be impossible to verify, and it would be destabilizing.</p>



<p>They argued you could never erase the knowledge of the nuclear age, and that under a hypothetical condition of disarmament, the first side that decided to rearm would have a big advantage, and that was destabilizing.</p>



<p>I would simply point out that that argument is also very convenient for the military research and development organizations they represented.&nbsp;</p>



<p><strong>In the book you discuss the popular American trope of the independent scientist restraining the establishment for its own good. Why do you think that image has had such staying power culturally?&nbsp;</strong></p>



<p>I think the myth is a consoling one. It responds to an anxiety that people feel about the state and other big institutions that shape our lives.&nbsp;</p>



<p>During World War II and the Cold War, the U.S. created an enormous national security state that reached deeply into other institutions, including private corporations and universities.</p>



<p>The myth became solidified with the surge of anti-militarism around the Vietnam War. That war profoundly damaged the faith that many Americans had in the institutions that purported to guarantee their security, and I think many wanted to believe that there was someone on the inside who was trying to keep things under control, who was working to check the government’s worst impulses.&nbsp;</p>



<p>Into that space arrived the image of the scientist, someone who seemed to have a kind of innate independence, who could transcend institutions and restrain them from the inside.</p>



<p>And here were these powerful and prestigious scientists claiming to be doing exactly that, and so the myth was endorsed both by these scientists and by many people on the outside who looked to them with a kind of sincere hope.&nbsp;</p>



<p><strong>In what ways has this dynamic evolved from the Cold War, and in what ways has it remained the same?&nbsp;</strong></p>



<p>Many of the arrangements that I uncovered and analyzed in my book were hidden from the public, so it could take a future historian to build an accurate understanding of whatever is going on right now.</p>



<p>&nbsp;I do have a hunch that the traditional military complex still has an important influence on policy debates today. But I suspect that in addition to missile companies, it’s Silicon Valley that’s furnishing some of the most influential R&amp;D elites.&nbsp;</p>



<p><strong>What lesson can today’s scientists take about their role in guiding national policy and in speaking to the public?&nbsp;</strong></p>



<p>I have not written an advice book, and I don’t pretend to tell scientists how they should behave today.</p>



<p>But I do think my book presents a kind of cautionary tale. Here were some of the greatest and most prestigious scientists of the 20th century, and in my view, they ended up serving forces they claimed to control and even to oppose, sometimes without realizing they were doing it.&nbsp;</p>



<p>Any advice I have applies as much to myself as anyone else: Be clear about what you value. Try to comprehend the forces that you serve and understand that they’re much larger than you.</p>



<p>And beware the temptation to believe that you are not responsible, ultimately, for the role that you play.&nbsp;</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Salamanders can regrow limbs. Could humans someday?</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/salamanders-can-regrow-limbs-could-humans-someday/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Elizabeth Zonarich]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 04 Nov 2025 17:48:54 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Science & Tech]]></category>

		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418586</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Findings on adrenaline’s role in process raise new possibilities for regenerative medicine]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-classic has-colored-heading has-media-on-the-left"
	style=" "
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Jessica Whited (right), an associate professor of Stem Cell and Regenerative Biology, and Duygu Payzin-Dogru, a postdoctoral fellow, are seen in a portrait outside their lab, in the Sherman Fairchild building. The two are part of a team researching how axolotl salamanders regrow limbs. Their work was recently published in the journal Cell. Veasey Conway/Harvard Staff Photographer" class="wp-image-418601" height="930" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Axolotl.png" width="621" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Axolotl.png 621w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Axolotl.png?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Axolotl.png?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Axolotl.png?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Axolotl.png?resize=43,64 43w" sizes="(max-width: 621px) 100vw, 621px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Duygu Payzin-Dogru (left) and Jessica Whited. </p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Veasey Conway/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/science-technology/"
		>
			Science &amp; Tech		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Salamanders can regrow limbs. Could humans someday?	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Findings on adrenaline’s role in process raise new possibilities for regenerative medicine		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Kermit Pattison	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer 		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-04">
			November 4, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-right is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-f1f2ed93 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Biologists long have been fascinated by the ability of salamanders to regrow entire limbs. Now Harvard researchers have solved part of the mystery of how they accomplish this feat — by activating stem cells throughout the body, not just at the injury site.</p>



<p>In a new <a href="https://doi.org/10.1016/j.cell.2025.09.025">paper</a> published in the journal Cell, researchers documented how this bodywide response in axolotl salamanders is triggered by the sympathetic nervous system, or the “fight or flight” network. The study raises the possibility that these mechanisms might one day be manipulated to regenerate human limbs and organs.</p>



<p>“We’ve shown the importance of the adrenaline stress signaling hormone in getting cells ready for regeneration,” said <a href="https://hscrb.harvard.edu/people/duygu-payzin/">Duygu Payzin-Dogru</a>, lead author of the study and a postdoctoral researcher in the Department of Stem Cell and Regenerative Biology. “Because adrenaline exists in humans, this tells us we can co-opt some of the things we found in the axolotl to perhaps improve regenerative outcomes in humans. We have some of the same components and just have to figure out the right way to implement them.”</p>



<p>The new study culminates several years of research by the <a href="https://hscrb.harvard.edu/labs/whited-lab/">lab of Jessica Whited</a>, associate professor in the Department of Stem Cell and Regenerative Biology, who studies limb regeneration in axolotls, a species native to Mexico. Axolotls are often examined as model organisms of limb regeneration because they are among the fastest-breeding species of salamanders.</p>



<p>Some invertebrates, such as planarian flatworms, can regrow entire bodies from small bits of tissue. But salamanders are the only vertebrates that can regenerate full limbs.</p>



<p>When an appendage is severed, salamanders sprout a blastema — a lump that contains the precursor cells that become increasingly specialized to form a new arm, leg, or tail.</p>



<p>This remarkable ability has long intrigued biologists because it may provide insights into regenerative medicine. Some researchers suspect that the ancient common ancestor of all tetrapods (the group of four-limbed vertebrates that includes amphibians, birds, and mammals) was able to regenerate limbs, but this ability was subsequently lost in most evolutionary lineages.</p>



<p>In 2018, the Whited team <a href="https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0012160617301951?via%3Dihub">reported</a> that limb amputation triggered a proliferation of cells throughout the body — even in limbs and organs that remained unharmed — but it remained unclear what mechanisms governed this response. The team spent more than six years deciphering those processes — an investigation that ultimately involved 38 co-authors.</p>



<p>They discovered that the systematic response was coordinated by the adrenergic signaling network, part of the sympathetic nervous system that also controls involuntary responses such as heart rate, breathing, and blood flow during times of extreme stress. (This system became well-known due to the pioneering studies of Harvard physiologist <a href="https://hms.harvard.edu/education-admissions/md-program/advising-academic-support/academic-societies/cannon-society">Walter Bradford Cannon</a>, who coined the term “fight or flight response” more than a century ago). Adrenergic signaling also involves the hormones noradrenaline and adrenaline, both of which also can act as neurotransmitters.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-395e8fd9-ea34-49ac-a6ef-f25b36c25f4f">
<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-harvard-quote harvard-quote is-style-sand" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--48)"><blockquote class="wp-block-quote"><p>&#8220;There is something that senses the injury and kind of goes into ‘getting ready’ mode for a subsequent injury so it can respond faster.&#8221;</p><cite>Duygu Payzin-Dogru</cite></blockquote></div>
</div>



<p>The systemic activation of stem cells and other progenitor cells “primes” the other uninjured limbs to regenerate more quickly — an ability that may help salamanders survive in the wild because they often lose multiple limbs to predators or cannibalism. The researchers discovered that the activated cells reconfigured their DNA architecture to make some genes easier to turn on — thus readying them for future regeneration.&nbsp;</p>



<p>“The animal seems to form a short-term memory of the injury, bodywide,” said Payzin-Dogru. “There is something that senses the injury and kind of goes into ‘getting ready’ mode for a subsequent injury so it can respond faster.”</p>



<p>But the priming was short-lived: The researchers found that systemic activation persisted only a few cell cycles, perhaps because the high metabolic costs could only be sustained for brief periods. After four weeks, there was no difference in the speed of limb regeneration.</p>



<p>The study parsed the roles of different elements of this system: One pathway, known as alpha-adrenergic signaling, is required to prime distant cells for limb regeneration, while another, known as beta-adrenergic signaling, promotes regrowth at the amputation site. The adrenergic signaling also triggered cascades of downstream processes essential for limb regeneration.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-02a6d6a0-ca6f-4eea-9f44-7c6d2735e5d6">
<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-harvard-quote harvard-quote is-style-sand"><blockquote class="wp-block-quote"><p>&#8220;I think it’s going to inspire a lot of future work to try to figure out not just how this works in an axolotl, but also how it works in other systems.&#8221;</p><cite>Jessica Whited</cite></blockquote></div>
</div>



<p>For two centuries, scientists have known that nerve supply was necessary to regenerate limbs, but many suspected the process involved sensory or motor nerves. “I heard very few people talking about sympathetic nerves,” said Whited.</p>



<p>Until now, many biologists have viewed limb regeneration as a local phenomenon at the injury site. But Whited said growing evidence suggests that it should be viewed as a whole-body event.</p>



<p>“I think it’s paradigm-shifting,” she said of the new study. “I think it’s going to inspire a lot of future work to try to figure out not just how this works in an axolotl, but also how it works in other systems.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p>This research was funded in part by the National Institutes of Health.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		‘Designed to be different’: Harvard unveils David Rubenstein Treehouse	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-video">
<iframe loading="lazy" title="The David Rubenstein Treehouse at Harvard" width="500" height="281" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/M3XFCY-lvgY?feature=oembed" frameborder="0" allow="accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture; web-share" referrerpolicy="strict-origin-when-cross-origin" allowfullscreen></iframe>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Amy Kamosa	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Correspondent		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-03">
			November 3, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			‘Visual connections,’ sustainability are key features of first University-wide conference center		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>With its facade of Alaskan yellow cedar, its bird-safe glass, and its branch-like mass timber beams, the David Rubenstein Treehouse, Harvard’s first University-wide conference center, has been designed to create a unique visual experience for visitors and to facilitate collaboration and social connection.&nbsp;The building, which began hosting private events this month, will serve as the “front door” to the adjacent <a href="https://seas.harvard.edu/about-us/visit-us/allston/enterprise-research-campus">Enterprise Research Campus</a> (ERC) in Allston. It was made possible by a gift from David Rubenstein, whose history of giving and service to Harvard extends over the past several decades.</p>



<p>“Since planning began on the ERC, one of our primary goals was to create a convening space at the University that would serve not only as a hub for sharing and innovating across disciplines but also for wider collaboration among academic, industry, and public and community leaders,” said Harvard Executive Vice President Meredith Weenick. “The Rubenstein Treehouse will provide space for exciting convenings and it will facilitate partnerships and foster connections.”</p>



<section class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-image-carousel alignfull carousel carousel--images"><div aria-labelledby="heading-9d4596de-785f-4459-be81-0192bfb4c3f9" class="carousel__wrapper splide"><div class="carousel__track splide__track"><div class="carousel__list splide__list">

2500px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Resiliency is embedded in both the building and landscape design by deploying nature-based water management strategies and using climate-adaptive planting.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Jason O’Rear/Studio Gang</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Interior space." class="wp-image-418766" height="2232" construction methods.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Jason O’Rear/Studio Gang</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Interior shot of Treehouse." class="wp-image-418767" height="1376" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/250002_Studio-Gang_David-Rubenstein-Treehouse_Atrium_cJason-ORear.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">The interior finishes align with Harvard Healthier Building Academy guidelines for removing harmful chemicals from the supply chain.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Jason O’Rear/Studio Gang</p></figcaption></figure>
</div></div></div></section>



<p>The building’s proximity to both Harvard Business School and Harvard’s Science and Engineering Complex supports its role as a convening space, and its design features are intended to facilitate collaboration and connection.&nbsp;</p>



<p>“Conference facilities are often closed-off spaces that lack connections to the communities and environment around them, but this building is designed to be different,” said the building’s design architect, Jeanne Gang, M.Arch. ’93, founding partner of Studio Gang and Kajima Professor in Practice of Architecture at the Harvard Graduate School of Design. “The idea was to create a place that felt more open and welcoming to everyone. Our design features visual connections across levels, so people can see each other and feel part of the activity happening throughout the building. Lots of natural light and framed views of the surrounding campus also help create a pleasant and unique experience for visitors.”</p>



<p>According to Gang, the building’s treehouse-like design is critical to the visitor experience.&nbsp; “We wanted visitors to feel the wonder and excitement of climbing up into a treehouse,” she said. The mass timber beams form branching structures that expand outward through the building’s three floors, culminating with the main 600-person conference room, called Canopy Hall, on the top floor.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?w=1024" alt="Jeanne Gang." class="wp-image-418768" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg 2500w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=2048,1366 2048w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/2500.102225_Rubenstein_Treehouse_0004.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 2500px) 100vw, 2500px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Jeanne Gang.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Niles Singer/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>



<p>The Rubenstein Treehouse is Harvard’s first mass timber building, an important factor in making it the University’s most sustainable structure.</p>



<p>“The David Rubenstein Treehouse is a model for the University’s leading-edge, multi-faceted approach to sustainability on campus,” Weenick said. “The mass timber construction makes the building visually stunning and creates a warm, welcoming, and memorable environment for visitors from near and far.”</p>



<p>The choice of mass timber over steel significantly reduces the building’s embodied carbon. Other key features include concrete made with ground glass pozzolan as an additive, rather than the commonly used coal fly ash, and interior fabrics and finishes that meet Harvard Healthier Building Academy guidelines for avoiding chemicals of concern in the supply chain, such as PFAS. Sustainability and overall environmental health were at the forefront of decision-making throughout the construction process. In some cases, the design and sustainability goals worked synergistically, as in the case of the in-floor ventilation system.</p>



<p>“We wanted to make sure the mass timber beams and columns were not obstructed by ducting and other building systems, so we moved most of those components under the floor,” said Gang. Not only did that create clean lines and an improved visual experience, it enabled the installation of a floor-based ventilation system that provides 75 percent more fresh air than standard HVAC and boosts energy efficiency. &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>



<p>The Rubenstein Treehouse is <a href="https://rubensteintreehouse.harvard.edu/inquire">now open for private scheduled events</a>. The first floor, with a cafe in the atrium, is expected to open to the public in January. Other elements of the ERC, including the Atlas Hotel and laboratory spaces, are expected to open in early 2026 as well, according to developer Tishman Speyer.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Can revenge be addictive?</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/11/can-revenge-be-addictive/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[gazettebeckycoleman]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 03 Nov 2025 19:49:25 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Health]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Addiction]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Books]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Brain]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Psychology]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[social media]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418864</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Psychiatrist explains how humans are hardwired to crave payback and why forgiveness is the ultimate antidote]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below title-above-image centered-image"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/health/"
		>
			Health		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Can revenge be addictive?	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Illustration of fire in shape of a brain." class="wp-image-418865" height="1052" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=150,106 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=300,212 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=768,543 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=1024,724 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=1536,1086 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=45,32 45w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=90,64 90w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=1488,1052 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/revenge-neuroscience.jpg?resize=1680,1188 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Getty Images</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Max Larkin	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-11-03">
			November 3, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Psychiatrist explains how humans are hardwired to crave payback and why forgiveness is the ultimate antidote		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Alongside painkillers, food, and technology, one psychiatrist worries about the addictive potential of getting back at people — or even thinking about it.</p>



<p>James Kimmel knows the danger well.</p>



<p>Now a psychiatrist at the Yale School of Medicine, he once was moments from being a teenage murderer. In a lecture given Wednesday, Kimmel explained how he wielded a loaded gun against school bullies after years of worsening torment —&nbsp;and why he didn’t fire.</p>



<p>“At the last second, I had this split second of insight,” Kimmel told his audience at the remote Community Conversations event hosted by the Harvard Graduate School of Design. “If I went ahead with this, I’d have, for the rest of my life, to identify as a murderer — and that was not an identity that was acceptable to me.”</p>



<p>It was just enough time to break the spell of “sweet revenge” —&nbsp;a psychological phenomenon that, Kimmel argued, works very much like any other drug.</p>



<p>When people are harboring a grievance, no matter its validity, Kimmel said, “It’s a very real pain. And your brain really, really doesn’t want pain —&nbsp;and so it instantly scrambles to rebalance that pain with pleasure.”</p>



<p>Decades of functional MRI data show the result: As subjects fantasize about settling scores, the brain’s reward and pleasure centers flood with dopamine — just as they would from gambling or tobacco.</p>



<p>And, just as with gambling or tobacco, the high doesn’t last. “It spurs us to go further, fantasize even more heavily, or go and take a physical act of revenge —&nbsp;to get that hit again and again,” Kimmel said.</p>



<p>Viewed from outside, the psychological cycle of resentment and revenge has an obvious grim futility. And yet in population studies, almost 100 percent of subjects will confess to having had at least one revenge fantasy in their lives.</p>



<p>“It’s thought to be part of an adaptive strategy from the Ice Age: to stop people from stealing your mate, stealing the food you need for the coming winter,” Kimmel said. “But now, of course, we seek revenge for threats that are just to our egos, that don’t have life-or-death consequences.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32)"><blockquote><p>If revenge is a cheap thrill, Kimmel argued, “Forgiveness is something of a wonder drug.”&nbsp;</p></blockquote></figure>



<p>Kimmel argued that the ancient brain chemistry lies at the root of most contemporary conflict, from ongoing wars and rancorous political divisions to social media spats.</p>



<p>What has changed, he said, is the technology: Notably, algorithms that have learned to exploit the druglike potential of contesting with an enemy.</p>



<p>“Now, when political leaders express a grievance, they can instantly ‘infect’ millions of people with that same grievance,” Kimmel said.</p>



<p>Online platforms don’t just act as vectors for rapid spread. They also offer a means of instant relief: to “fire back” violent or cruel rhetoric at the perceived enemy.</p>



<p>For that reason, Kimmel believes those algorithms should be regulated just as if they were herbicides or pesticides —&nbsp;hazardous to human health.</p>



<p>But he does also see avenues for hope.</p>



<p>Looking back at his harrowing teenage experience, Kimmel sees that moment of insight as having given his prefrontal cortex —&nbsp;momentarily swamped by the operation of rage in other parts of the brain —&nbsp;a chance to recover and do its protective work.</p>



<p>And near-misses are common: While revenge fantasies are nearly universal experiences, only about one in five say they have acted on those fantasies.</p>



<p>What’s more, once we come to see revenge in his terms, Kimmel said, we gain tools against it: namely, cognitive-behavioral therapy, peer-support programs like Alcoholics Anonymous, and —&nbsp;potentially —&nbsp;GLP-1 drugs that have shown promise in <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/dry-january-helped-drive-drinking-rates-to-96-year-low/">reducing other kinds of cravings</a>.</p>



<p>In a <a href="https://www.amazon.com/Science-Revenge-Understanding-Deadliest-Addiction/dp/0593796519/ref=sr_1_1?crid=3R0VE1S8M8VMB&amp;dib=eyJ2IjoiMSJ9.jG7Gsqs1yr2sv4sA_nTGQg.r6W20rZ85FjPOEt4QOdoeurFSzcJdxNI759wABO9o5Y&amp;dib_tag=se&amp;keywords=kimmel+science+of+revenge&amp;qid=1726254825&amp;sprefix=kimmel+science+of+revenge%2Caps%2C70&amp;sr=8-1">book</a> published this spring and a <a href="https://www.miraclecourt.com">free app</a>, Kimmel offers his own intervention: a kind of imaginary courtroom where perceived wrongs can be considered, judged, and —&nbsp;eventually —&nbsp;released.</p>



<p>If revenge is a cheap thrill, Kimmel argued, “Forgiveness is something of a wonder drug.” The same brain scans show that after a harm, forgiveness “actually deactivates the anterior insula, the pain network inside your head. So instead of covering it up with a dopamine rush, you’re actually getting pain <em>stoppage</em>.”</p>



<p>Easier said than done, of course. Kimmel calls his imaginary courtroom the “non-justice system” for a reason&nbsp;— it aims not to settle accounts but to offer liberation from a destructive pattern.</p>



<p>After hearing both sides and weighing a sentence, “In the final judgment, you’re given an opportunity to <em>imagine</em> what it would feel like if you decided to forgive — you don’t even have to commit” to going that route, Kimmel said.</p>



<p>Even at the notion of breaking the cycle, Kimmel said, participants report they feel an instantaneous sense of relief — evidence that, despite its pleasures, “revenge-seeking is hard work.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Where were you the first time you heard ‘Hamilton’? The actors remember.</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/where-were-you-the-first-time-you-heard-hamilton-the-actors-remember/</link>

					<description><![CDATA[Touring cast visits to offer students insights into theater and representation, gain some into U.S. history around campus]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-overlay has-uncropped-image"
	style=" --min-height: 66.69921875vw;"
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/arts-humanities/"
		>
			Arts &amp; Culture		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Where were you the first time you heard ‘Hamilton’? The actors remember.	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="" class="wp-image-418878" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0318.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Curator John Overholt (left) shows historical documents to members of the cast of “Hamilton.&#8221; </p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Veasey Conway/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Eileen O’Grady	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-31">
			October 31, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			6 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Touring cast visits to offer students insights into theater and representation, gain some into U.S. history around campus		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Both A.D. Weaver and Tyler Fauntleroy remember exactly where they were in 2015, the first time they heard the “Hamilton” soundtrack.</p>



<p>Weaver was working a shift at Best Buy. He wasn’t supposed to be listening to music on the sales floor, but he’d snuck a headphone into one ear instead of his work-issued walkie-talkie. Fauntleroy was in his college dorm. By the time the soundtrack got to “Helpless,” the love song in the first act, he was lying down, completely floored.</p>



<p>“I remember ‘My Shot’ had me like, ‘OK, this is something else,’” Fauntleroy told a group of students over a campus lunch on Tuesday. “By ‘Wait for It’ I just was like, ‘I have surrendered to this album.’ It was one of those times where you’re like, ‘I’m engaging with something that’s going to change my life.’”</p>



<p>Weaver now plays George Washington and Fauntleroy plays Alexander Hamilton in the <a href="https://hamiltonmusical.com/us-tour/">North American touring cast of “Hamilton</a>.” They were joined by fellow actors Marja Harmon (Angelica Schuyler), Lauren Mariasoosay (Eliza Hamilton), and Christian Magby (Thomas Jefferson and Marquis de Lafayette) at the <a href="https://ofa.fas.harvard.edu/artsbites">ArtsBites Luncheon</a> hosted by the <a href="https://ofa.fas.harvard.edu/">Office for the Arts at Harvard</a>.<br></p>



<figure class="wp-block-embed is-type-video is-provider-vimeo wp-block-embed-vimeo wp-embed-aspect-16-9 wp-has-aspect-ratio"><div class="wp-block-embed__wrapper">
<iframe loading="lazy" title="The Harvard Opportunes perform for the cast of Hamilton" src="https://player.vimeo.com/video/1131437675?h=60c10dea16&amp;dnt=1&amp;app_id=122963" width="500" height="281" frameborder="0" allow="autoplay; fullscreen; picture-in-picture; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; web-share" referrerpolicy="strict-origin-when-cross-origin"></iframe>
</div></figure>



<p>The cast members told students about their journeys from being fans of the musical to getting the offer to play in it. For Fauntleroy, who first auditioned in 2015 and was cast in 2023, playing Hamilton feels like a hard-won victory.</p>



<p>“It was a long, long, long road of ‘You did everything great, nothing you could do differently,’” Fauntleroy told students, describing the audition process. “For whatever reason, they go the way that they go. Now there’s something about [performing] the end of ‘Yorktown’ and seeing the feeling of victory wash over all of us. The awe of, ‘Oh, my God, we’re here. We did it.’”</p>



<p>The cast of the Tony-award winning musical has been performing at the Citizens Opera House since September as part of the 25/26 Broadway in Boston Season.&nbsp; Their last local show is Nov. 2.</p>



<p>Earlier in the day, the actors visited <a href="https://library.harvard.edu/libraries/houghton">Houghton Library</a>, where they were welcomed by <a href="https://history.fas.harvard.edu/people/drew-faust">Drew Gilpin Faust</a>, president emerita and Arthur Kingsley Porter University Research Professor.</p>



<p>“It really matters so much that you all are carrying on the tradition and that you’re here to see some of the original materials on which the whole story is based and have been passed down to our generation of Americans,” Faust said. “Thank you for bringing these ideas and these principles to wider audiences, because we need desperately to understand where we come from and who we are, and what our values are as a nation.”</p>



<p>At&nbsp;<a href="https://library.harvard.edu/libraries/houghton">Houghton Library</a>,&nbsp;<a href="https://library.harvard.edu/staff/john-overholt">John Overholt</a>, curator of the&nbsp;<a href="https://library.harvard.edu/collections/donald-and-mary-hyde-collection-dr-samuel-johnson">Donald and Mary Hyde Collection of Dr. Samuel Johnson</a>&nbsp;and Early Books and Manuscripts, showed the cast an array of rare documents dating back to the Revolutionary War.</p>



<section class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-image-carousel alignfull carousel carousel--show-numbers carousel--images"><div aria-labelledby="heading-9a1fa3b4-f9ca-4950-8490-2ed2d7e7f2f9" class="carousel__wrapper splide"><div class="carousel__track splide__track"><div class="carousel__list splide__list">
<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="" class="wp-image-418882" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1410.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Lauren Mariasoosay (center), who plays Eliza Hamilton.</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="" class="wp-image-418879" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0780.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Marja Harmon, who plays Angelica Schuyler (from left), and Tyler Fauntleroy (who plays Alexander Hamilton).</p></figcaption></figure>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-carousel-slide carousel__slide splide__slide wp-block-image wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="George Washington’s handwritten rebuttals in the margins." class="wp-image-418877" height="992" loading="lazy" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_0049.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">George Washington’s handwritten rebuttals to James Monroe are seen in the margins of Monroe’s book detailing his time as ambassador to France.</p></figcaption></figure>
</div></div></div></section>



<p>Among them were an original Boston printing of the Declaration of Independence, a first edition of the Federalist Papers, and a “Reynolds Pamphlet.” Hamilton wrote the pamphlet, which figures famously in the musical, to defend himself from embezzlement accusations by confessing to an extramarital affair with Maria Reynolds, whose husband was a blackmailer and scam artist.</p>



<p>The actors clustered around the document<strong>,</strong> turning its 250-year-old cotton fiber pages and scanning the text. “And he wrote it down right there,” Magby exclaimed, quoting from the musical.</p>



<p>The cast took turns reading aloud an English translation of a Latin honorary degree — the first Harvard ever bestowed — presented to George Washington in 1776.</p>



<p>Faust and Marc Goodheart, vice president and senior adviser to the president and provost, led the cast members on a tour of some of the historic sites in Harvard Yard, including Massachusetts Hall, which once housed founding fathers Samuel Adams, John Adams, John Hancock, and Elbridge Gerry as students, as well as Hollis Hall and Holden Chapel, which (like Mass Hall) housed Continental Army soldiers during the Revolutionary War. &nbsp;</p>



<p>At the Office for the Arts, the cast answered student questions about favorite moments from the show, navigating an uncertain theater job market, and representation.</p>



<p>Harmon recalled the first time she saw actress Renée Goldsberry playing Hamilton’s sister-in-law Angelica Schuyler and thinking, “This is the first time I’ve ever seen myself on stage, represented,” Harmon said.</p>



<p>“What auditioning looked like pre-‘Hamilton’ and what it looks like now, even though it still has a long way to go, it’s night and day,” she said. “The difference is the opportunity and trusting that brown and Black people can lead shows and be successful.”</p>



<p>Each cast member shared a different approach to handling the stress of nightly shows on tour. Weaver said getting enough sleep is key. Fauntleroy said sitting in silence for at least 15 minutes every day helps him handle some of the fast-paced show’s more exhausting numbers, including “My Shot,” which he said feels like a “flash grenade.”</p>



<div class="wp-block-columns alignwide is-layout-flex wp-container-core-columns-is-layout-28f84493 wp-block-columns-is-layout-flex">
<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?w=1024" alt="" class="wp-image-418885" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_1932.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Harvard Opportunes singer Kaylor Toronto ’27 (right) performs a solo.</figcaption></figure>
</div>



<div class="wp-block-column is-layout-flow wp-block-column-is-layout-flow">
<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?w=1024" alt="" class="wp-image-418886" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102825_Hamilton_2015.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">A.D. Weaver, who plays George Washington (from left), and Fauntleroy applaud the surprise performance by the Harvard singing group.</figcaption></figure>
</div>
</div>



<p>“The show is just super stimulating. You have to pace your brain throughout the day,” Fauntleroy said. “I’ve learned if I’ve already overstimulated myself during the day, of course it’s gonna be crazy, it’s gonna feel more intense than it is. If I’ve grounded myself then I have even more to give and more places to go.”</p>



<p>At the historic Wadsworth House, the cast were delighted by a surprise a capella performance of “Wait for It,” by the <a href="https://www.harvardopportunes.com/">Harvard Opportunes</a>.</p>



<p>“I think for lot of us — at least for me at 14 — our lives were changed by ‘Hamilton,’” Opportunes soprano Gabby Medina ’26 told the cast as they hugged and posed for photos after the performance.&nbsp;“We don’t really get to sing a lot of musical theater like this, so this is really healing.”</p>



<p>“It’s healing for us too,” Mariasoosay said.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/health/"
		>
			Health		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		COVID in pregnancy raises child’s risk for developmental disorders	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Infection poses greatest threat during third trimester, according to study analyzing more than 18,000 births during pandemic peak		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Mass General Brigham Communication	</p>
				</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-31">
			October 31, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			2 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Pregnant woman wearing medical mask." class="wp-image-418846" height="993" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1025 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=1488,993 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/pregnancy-covid-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1121 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Getty Images</p></figcaption></figure>

	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-left is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-12dd3699 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Children born to mothers who had COVID-19 while pregnant face an elevated risk of developmental disorders by the time they turn 3 years old, including speech delays, autism, motor disorders, and other developmental delays, according to new research by investigators at&nbsp;<a href="https://www.massgeneralbrigham.org/">Mass General Brigham</a>. The findings are published in&nbsp;<a href="http://doi.org/10.1097/AOG.0000000000006112" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Obstetrics &amp; Gynecology</a>.</p>



<p>“These findings highlight that COVID-19, like many other infections in pregnancy, may pose risks not only to the mother, but to fetal brain development,” said senior author&nbsp;<a href="https://www.massgeneral.org/obgyn/vcrb/research/edlow-lab?cmp=nwsref" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Andrea Edlow</a>, a maternal-fetal medicine specialist in the&nbsp;Department of Obstetrics and Gynecology&nbsp;at Mass General Brigham. “They also support the importance of trying to prevent COVID-19 infection in pregnancy and are particularly relevant when public trust in vaccines — including the COVID-19 vaccine — is being eroded.”</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-6ad74409-c841-43b6-9f8e-245f90777ac6">
<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p>“These findings highlight that COVID-19, like many other infections in pregnancy, may pose risks not only to the mother, but to fetal brain development.” </p>
</blockquote>
</div>



<p>Other maternal infections during pregnancy have been linked to risk for a range of neurodevelopmental diseases during childhood, and animal studies have shown that immune activation during pregnancy disrupts normal fetal brain development and offspring behavior. To assess the effects of SARS-CoV-2 infection during pregnancy, investigators analyzed data on 18,124 live births at Mass General Brigham within the peak COVID-19 window of March 2020 to May 2021.</p>



<p>The investigators studied 18,124 mother-child pairs. Among the 861 children whose mothers were SARS-CoV-2–positive during pregnancy, 140 (16.3 percent) received a neurodevelopmental diagnosis by age 3, compared with 1,680 (9.7 percent) of the 17,263 remaining children from SARS-CoV-2–negative pregnancies. After adjusting for other influencing factors, SARS-CoV-2 infection during pregnancy was associated with 29 percent higher odds of a neurodevelopmental condition in children.</p>



<p>The investigators also found that males were at higher risk than females. Risk was greatest when infection occurred during the third trimester of pregnancy.</p>



<p>While reducing risk is important, co-senior author&nbsp;<a href="https://www.massgeneral.org/doctors/17173/roy-perlis?cmp=nwsref" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Roy Perlis</a> of the Mass General Brigham&nbsp;Department of Psychiatry noted, “The overall risk of adverse neurodevelopmental outcomes in exposed children likely remains low.”</p>



<p>First author and maternal-fetal medicine specialist&nbsp;<a href="https://www.massgeneral.org/doctors/22603/lydia-shook?cmp=nwsref" target="_blank" rel="noreferrer noopener">Lydia Shook</a> added, “Parental awareness of the potential for adverse child neurodevelopmental outcomes after COVID-19 in pregnancy is key. By understanding the risks, parents can appropriately advocate for their children to have proper evaluation and support.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p>This study was supported by the National Institute of Mental Health and the National Institute of Child Health and Human Development.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Leading FAS in period of major challenges, opportunity for change</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/leading-fas-in-period-of-major-challenges-opportunity-for-change/</link>

					<description><![CDATA[Hopi Hoekstra details what she’s learned in first two years as dean, her moves to strengthen funding, academics, admissions, and expand aid]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-classic has-colored-heading has-media-on-the-left"
	style=" "
