		<title>Pritzker sees an institution meeting the moment</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/pritzker-sees-an-institution-meeting-the-moment/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 30 Oct 2025 18:00:00 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Campus & Community]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Education]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Leadership News]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Q&A]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[U.S. Politics]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418609</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Senior fellow stresses core principles, Corporation engagement, constructive dialogue as University navigates ‘period of severe challenge’]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-classic has-colored-heading has-media-on-the-left"
	style=" "
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Penny Pritzker '81." class="wp-image-418704" height="2232" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667_Pritzker_Penny_02.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="(max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">File photo by Stephanie Mitchell/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/campus-community/"
		>
			Campus &amp; Community		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Pritzker sees an institution meeting the moment	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Senior fellow stresses core principles, Corporation engagement, constructive dialogue as University navigates ‘period of severe challenge’		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
							</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-30">
			October 30, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			long read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-right is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-f1f2ed93 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>As senior fellow of the Harvard Corporation, Penny Pritzker ’81 has been deeply engaged in supporting and advising President Alan Garber during a tumultuous period for the University. The adversity Harvard has faced in recent years is “unprecedented,” she said, and has tested the University to “stand by the guiding principles of our community.”</p>



<p>In this edited interview, Pritzker, a private sector leader and public servant, reflects on events of recent months, the progress Harvard is making, the University’s financial position, the work of the Corporation, and the experience of becoming a Harvard student once again.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p><strong>This has clearly been a difficult year for the University. I guess the obvious starting point is what’s on your mind as you think about Harvard at this moment?</strong></p>



<p>Look, I am a realist and we can’t sugarcoat what has been a period of severe challenge for Harvard and for higher education. Our approach to the situation under President Garber’s leadership and as a Corporation has been to ground ourselves in Harvard’s values. That has led us to do three things simultaneously, and they aren’t mutually exclusive.</p>



<p>First, we’ve articulated and regrounded ourselves in our core research and teaching mission. From new research on AI and quantum science to potentially life-saving advances in the study of Alzheimer’s, cancer, and gene editing, the research mission continues to demonstrate the power of universities to contribute to the national interest and to making countless lives better. And, of course, we remain focused on helping students grow into citizen-leaders who will make a positive impact in the world.</p>



<p>Second, we are working hard and with a lot of humility to address the legitimate challenges on our campus. We’ve taken important steps to tackle discrimination and bias, building on the exceptional work of the task forces on <a href="https://www.harvard.edu/task-force-on-antisemitism/">antisemitism and anti-Israeli bias</a> and on <a href="https://www.harvard.edu/task-force-on-anti-muslim-and-anti-arab-bias/">anti-Muslim, anti-Arab, and anti-Palestinian&nbsp;bias</a>. As an institution we’ve made good progress on the implementation of the recommendations of those task forces, and that work must continue.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-ea4987e3-6e36-43b4-9672-ecd94cede177">
<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p>“This has been the touchstone in every single conversation among the Corporation about the crisis: How do we advance the mission and stand by the guiding principles of our community?”</p>
</blockquote>
</div>



<p>We have introduced Harvard’s <a href="https://provost.harvard.edu/institutional_voice_principles_FAQs">institutional voice policy</a>, and we are making real progress on reaffirming a culture of constructive dialogue and open inquiry. We are all aware of the problem — reports of students and faculty feeling unable to speak out in class, to express what they may feel is a minority view. The work led by Tomiko Brown-Nagin, Eric Beerbohm, and the <a href="https://provost.harvard.edu/report-open-inquiry-and-constructive-dialogue">Open Inquiry Working Group</a> is helping us craft real solutions to a problem that is not unique to Harvard, but is shared across higher education. In every School at Harvard there are meaningful efforts to address these issues, working to build skills that will ensure graduates can be open to changing their minds and can hear their assumptions challenged.</p>



<p>Finally, we are seeking to address a wide range of unprecedented threats to our core principles, loss of research funding, challenges for international students, and the dozens of investigations and inquiries into various aspects of our work.</p>



<p>In every instance, my number one focus and that of the Corporation is to provide advice and make decisions that support Harvard’s fundamental mission of excellence in teaching, learning, and research. This has been the touchstone in every single conversation among the Corporation about the crisis: How do we advance the mission and stand by the guiding principles of our community? We have tried to ask hard questions and act with humility and curiosity in answering them.</p>



<p><strong>You mentioned constructive dialogue as a key area of focus. You took the course that first-years take on this issue. Tell us about that.</strong></p>



<p>I did! I took the same online module that all first-year students take when they get to Harvard. This was a custom module developed for the College by the Constructive Dialogue Institute. The module is a powerful tool that has already had an impact on our incoming students. It challenges us to approach dialogue with empathy and understanding, to ask ourselves, “What are the values underlying this view that I disagree with?” “How can I approach disagreements with curiosity rather than defensiveness?” — to let go of winning and to find what is shared. I loved the program and have shared it with a lot of people beyond Harvard. These are skills not just lacking at universities; they are being lost across our society.</p>



<p>On the broader issue of dialogue and speech, the feedback we’re receiving from students and faculty is anecdotal but already very positive.</p>



<p><strong>Another area of concern has been the financial headwinds facing the University. Harvard’s </strong><a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/harvard-reports-operating-deficit-amid-federal-funding-cuts/"><strong>financial report</strong></a><strong> came out earlier this month. How do you think about our financial standing?</strong></p>



<p>There is good news and bad news. Our supporters leaned in and made this a strong year for fundraising and our endowment performed well, but cuts to federal research funding and our efforts to fill that gap led to a deficit. Before we talk further about our finances, I want to say thank you to those who have and are supporting the University. It is important that folks know how deeply grateful we are for their trust and their generosity.</p>



<p>As for our finances, it is sobering and more important than ever to bring a multiyear perspective to the situation. We have to assume the model for federal-university partnership will not return to where it has been. There are measures in the administration’s budget and policies that would drastically shrink federal support for scientific research and would seek to direct funds away from institutions like Harvard. In addition, Congress passed a significant increase in the endowment tax that targets Harvard and a handful of other similar institutions. Plus, of course, there continues to be legal uncertainty — investigations and appeals that could directly target our funding.</p>



<p>Unfortunately, this has meant painful decisions like layoffs and a hiring freeze. People have lost their jobs, and that weighs heavily on me and on all of us. We are making these decisions always in service to our core mission of teaching, learning, and research. Through all of this, I have been profoundly grateful for everything our community is doing to rally around the University and keep it moving forward.</p>



<p><strong>The Corporation itself has seen important changes over the past year. Can you talk about some of those?</strong></p>



<p>I should start by noting that the idea of reflection and renewal are constants among the Corporation. We always ask ourselves some key questions: How can we best serve the institution not just for the present but also for the long term? Do we have the right blend of experience and expertise? Where can we best engage with the wider community? I would point to a few changes in that context.</p>



<p>First, we have some new members of the Corporation — Joe Bae, Ken Frazier, Rick Lifton, Kannon Shanmugam — all of whom make unique and vital contributions to our deliberations, in part through embracing how the Corporation works: as a collegial, apolitical, consensus-focused group that brings disparate points of view and is not reluctant to express them. They also love Harvard deeply. As a group we are focused on discharging our duties as fiduciaries, but also as individuals, and as a body, we are deeply devoted to the purpose and progress of Harvard.</p>



<p>Second, we have undertaken a serious review of how we approach the presidential search process. Led by a small committee that includes members of the Corporation as well as alumni with deep commitment to the University, we are looking at how we can ensure the most thoughtful, thorough, and effective search at the appropriate time. This is a core role for the Fellows and one we are committed to getting right.</p>



<p>The third area I want to highlight is one that the Corporation takes very seriously: We have recommitted to being more intentionally engaged with members of the Harvard community. For example, we meet regularly with the deans of the Schools, participate in faculty town halls and meetings with faculty groups on a range of issues, engage with students to better understand their experience, and also meet with alumni and supporters of the University to hear their ideas and concerns. We’ve really sought to increase the volume and depth of engagement over the last couple of years. I know that it enhances our deliberations as a Corporation, and we will continue that work.</p>



<div class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-supporting-content alignleft supporting-content" id="supporting-content-1e3fa56d-043e-415c-a826-4ad56a41e575">
<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow">
<p>“People in our community and frankly others with no direct relationship to the University are dedicated to the ideal of what Harvard and higher education can do for our country and the world. That gives me hope.”</p>
</blockquote>
</div>



<p><strong>As you pursue this engagement strategy, what are some of the key things you are hearing?</strong></p>



<p>We’re hearing a lot of support for President Garber and how he has led the University during an extraordinarily challenging time. He’s been very clear and proactive in conveying the principles that we stand for and in foregrounding the vital importance and work of research universities. He’s a person of deep integrity and impact — introducing much-needed reforms to strengthen our community, tackling bias in all its forms, and creating a culture of constructive dialogue. So there’s a great deal of appreciation for his purpose-driven leadership. Not everyone agrees with every step we are taking as a university, but there’s a widespread belief, I think, that Alan and the entire leadership team have the best interests of Harvard in mind as they make decisions.</p>



<p>Also, we hear a wide range of perspectives on the challenges the University is facing and the course we should be pursuing on behalf of Harvard and higher education. We welcome those perspectives. Whether those concerns are about our finances or research funding or outstanding legal matters, the input always comes from a place of goodwill for Harvard. People in our community and frankly others with no direct relationship to the University are dedicated to the ideal of what Harvard and higher education can do for our country and the world. That gives me hope.</p>



<p><strong>It might be natural, at a moment like this, to focus just on the challenges, but positive things have been happening on campus. What’s been most exciting to you and the Corporation?</strong></p>



<p>As I mentioned earlier, there are so many great things happening at Harvard. Our researchers making groundbreaking progress in diabetes, cancer, Alzheimer’s, and other diseases are an inspiration, especially at a moment when scientific research is under duress.</p>



<p>Across the University there is exciting, innovative work underway. The new <a href="https://artsandhumanities.fas.harvard.edu/public-culture-project">Public Culture Project</a> at the FAS will bring the critical thinking of the humanities to some of the biggest questions of our time, such as the evolution of AI and the moral principles underpinning economic life. At the Graduate School of Education, a team of researchers is <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/as-states-take-lead-in-fixing-u-s-schools-harvard-will-serve-as-a-hub/">undertaking a nine-state effort</a> to assess, measure, and share what actually drives student outcomes. And the <a href="https://www.hks.harvard.edu/american-service-fellowship">American Service Fellowship</a> at the Kennedy School will provide full scholarships for public servants and military veterans to enroll for a fully funded master’s degree. These are just a few examples and there are many more across all of our Schools.</p>



<p>I also recently toured Allston, where I saw the <a href="https://americanrepertorytheater.org/a-new-home-in-allston/">new A.R.T</a>., the new <a href="https://rubensteintreehouse.harvard.edu/">Rubenstein Treehouse</a>, and the emerging <a href="https://seas.harvard.edu/about-us/visit-us/allston/enterprise-research-campus">Enterprise Research Campus</a>. What’s happening across that growing section of campus is game-changing for the University and the Greater Boston region. The bottom line is that there are many significant and impactful things happening at Harvard, and we should not lose sight of that fact.</p>



<p><strong>We’re in the mid-semester period. What is your message to the community?</strong></p>



<p>I know that there is a lot of swirl and noise around Harvard and higher education. We have taken seriously the criticisms we’re hearing while defending the principles that underpin this great institution. I certainly don’t minimize the challenges we face.</p>



<p>But I want to shine a light on the powerful things happening on campus and the people making them happen: our students, who are an inspiration to me and all of us on the Corporation; the faculty and researchers who are advancing knowledge and making a difference in people’s lives; and the staff who come to work every day to enable our mission.</p>



<p>Harvard is not perfect, nor do we ever claim to be. This community gives so much to so many, including me. I came here at 18 at a very difficult time in my life and when I needed that support and love the most. Harvard changed my life and it does that for so many people. Our strength is in how we evolve to push new frontiers and to meet the moment we find ourselves in. I’m confident that this institution and its people will rise to the occasion together.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

<div class="wp-block-group alignfull has-neutral-white-color has-neutral-black-background-color has-text-color has-background has-link-color wp-elements-de147e5df7bba7fe1487d7bf6a4a808c has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<div style="height:100px" aria-hidden="true" class="wp-block-spacer"></div>



<p>Lurking in the galleries and archives of the Harvard Art Museums you’ll find works that inspire, delight, and, sometimes, unnerve. The following quiz shines a light on the darker corners of these hallowed halls.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-squares"/>

       } else {            triggerPostRender();        }    } );} );
</script>



<div style="height:100px" aria-hidden="true" class="wp-block-spacer"></div>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Tracking climate change through nature’s ‘breaths’</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/tracking-climate-change-through-natures-breaths/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[David Trilling]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 30 Oct 2025 15:17:04 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Science & Tech]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Climate]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418331</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[New research tower monitoring Harvard Forest’s carbon intake, outtake continues data collection that started in 1989 ]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-overlay has-uncropped-image"
	style=" --min-height: 66.69921875vw;"
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/science-technology/"
		>
			Science &amp; Tech		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Tracking climate change through nature’s ‘breaths’	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Mark VanScoy climbs the new environmental monitoring tower in Harvard Forest." class="wp-image-418332" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1175-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by David Trilling
</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		David Trilling	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Salata Institute Communications		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-30">
			October 30, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			6 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			New research tower monitoring Harvard Forest’s carbon intake, outtake continues data collection that started in 1989 		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>You might call it the case of the missing carbon.</p>



<p>It was the 1980s. Scientists knew roughly how much carbon dioxide (CO₂) was being emitted globally by humans burning fossil fuels and by natural processes such as volcanos. They also knew how much CO₂ was in the atmosphere. These data showed that about half of the fossil fuel CO₂ was disappearing each year. But where was it going?</p>



<p>It was clear that forests absorb a lot: Plants fix carbon, through photosynthesis, into wood and other vegetation. But forests also emit. Microbes in the soil digest rotting leaves and dead wood, respiring carbon back into the sky.</p>



<p>Understanding which of those two processes dominates — forests’ absorption of carbon or their emission of it — is essential to predict the future of the carbon budget and climate change.</p>



<p>Atmospheric chemist <a href="https://salatainstitute.harvard.edu/people/steven-c-wofsy/">Steven Wofsy</a> saw in this question an engineering challenge. By measuring CO₂ in the updrafts and downdrafts swirling around the forest canopy, over years, he realized he could account for the role of forests in the carbon cycle.</p>



<p>He’s been measuring ever since.</p>



<p>Eight times a second, every minute, every day since October 1989, a 100-foot lattice tower poking above the canopy at Harvard Forest has been analyzing the carbon exchange. As tiny eddies of air shuttle gases up and down, an infrared spectrometer measures concentrations of CO₂ and water vapor, while a 3D sonic anemometer logs vertical and horizontal wind speed. Over time, the covariance shows how much carbon the forest absorbed or released — the net exchange with the atmosphere, also known as the eddy flux.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-immersive alignfull immersive" data-fade-first-slide="false" data-speed="75" data-transition-type="fade"><div class="immersive--scroll-content">
<div style="background-image:url(&#039;https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1139-1920.jpg&#039;);background-size:cover;" class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-immersive-slide immersive--scroll-content-item is-layout-constrained has-background">
<p class="has-text-align-center has-neutral-white-background-color has-background has-neue-haas-grotesk-text-font-family has-x-large-font-size"><strong>The original tower is crowded with instruments.</strong></p>
</div>



<div style="background-image:url(&#039;https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1202-1920.jpg&#039;);background-position:39% 41%;background-size:cover;" class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-immersive-slide immersive--scroll-content-item is-layout-constrained has-background">
<p class="has-text-align-center has-neutral-white-background-color has-background has-x-large-font-size"><strong>Research assistant Mark VanScoy climbs the new tower.</strong></p>
</div>



<div style="background-image:url(&#039;https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1237-1920.jpg&#039;);background-size:cover;" class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-immersive-slide immersive--scroll-content-item is-layout-constrained has-background">
<p class="has-text-align-center has-neutral-white-background-color has-background has-x-large-font-size"><strong>Data from the new tower is recorded on computers in a shed below.</strong></p>
</div>
</div></figure>



<p>The early data shocked Wofsy and his team: The oaks and maples and scattered conifers around the tower were absorbing roughly 2 tons of carbon per hectare per year. The data proved that Harvard Forest — 16 square kilometers in central Massachusetts where farms and pastures had been abandoned in the 19th century — was not yet at steady state; it was still a net carbon sink. They had found the missing carbon — and, more important, the way to measure it.</p>



<p>“Most people thought these forests had fully regenerated after being farmland and were already full of carbon,” said Wofsy, the Abbott Lawrence Rotch Professor of Atmospheric and Environmental Science.</p>



<p>The findings inspired a network of towers, now hundreds strong, around the world, from Korea to Congo. These inform policy and land-use decisions. Progress on climate change requires hard choices — keep a forest standing or clear it for a solar farm — and the flux towers underwrite the rigorous carbon accounting that confirms which options truly reduce atmospheric CO₂.</p>



<p>Now in its fourth decade, and source of the world’s longest-running flux data set, the Environmental Measurement Station (EMS) tower at Harvard Forest is reaching the end of its lifespan. With support from the Salata Institute for Climate and Sustainability and the Faculty of Arts and Sciences, EMS 2.0 was installed this summer. It will carry on collecting data as the changing climate becomes harder to ignore.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1920" height="1280" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg" alt="Maxwell Lutz pointing to a tree in Harvard Forest." class="wp-image-418365" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1251-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Field technician Maxwell Lutz demonstrates how measurements of wood mass validate the findings of the EMS instruments.</figcaption></figure>



<h5 class="wp-block-heading has-neue-haas-grotesk-text-font-family" id="h-deposits-and-withdrawals"><strong>Deposits and withdrawals</strong></h5>



<p>To understand carbon flux, picture a simple accounting ledger, explains <a href="https://seas.harvard.edu/person/bill-munger">William Munger</a>, senior research fellow in atmospheric chemistry at the Harvard John A. Paulson School of Engineering and Applied Sciences, who has worked on the EMS project from the start.</p>



<p>Deposits are photosynthesis; that’s carbon uptake, or fixed carbon. Withdrawals are respiration, when decomposing plants release CO₂. In this metaphor, the temperate forests in Massachusetts are posting steady deposits into the “carbon bank” — for now.</p>



<p>“The EMS data show very clearly that winters are not what they used to be,” said Munger. “Annual variability jumped in the 2000s.” Like the rest of southern New England, Harvard Forest now gets less snow — meaning less spring meltwater to encourage growth. Snowpack once insulated roots; without it, more trees die during a deep freeze. Less water and more dead trees both cut the forest’s carbon uptake.</p>



<p>“A few years after a dry year, you see a dip in carbon respiration — when you’d expect those leaves to be decomposing.”</p>



<p>After monitoring a decade of steady deposits, the EMS tower saw carbon uptake double for a few years before falling, for one year, to zero. These oscillations show a system highly responsive to disturbance and climate variability.</p>



<p>“The tower lets us observe the way the forest responds to major seasonal and annual weather fluctuations, and to track the carbon cycle,” said Wofsy. &nbsp;</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32)"><blockquote><p>“Plants, animals, and soil microbes take in 25 to 30 percent of our emitted carbon dioxide. Should it stop, we’re in trouble.”</p><cite>Michele Holbrook</cite></blockquote></figure>



<p>Flux towers are also keeping an eye on some of the world’s largest carbon deposits.</p>



<p>In the Amazon, rapid photosynthesis by mammoth trees is offset by equally intense respiration from warm, wet soil and tireless decomposers, yielding near-zero net annual change. In the boreal forests of the north, the soil contains millennia of peat and cold-stabilized organic matter — vast carbon stores accumulated since the ice retreated. Disturbances such as fire, drought, or thaw trigger fast, <a href="https://salatainstitute.harvard.edu/canada-used-to-send-clean-cool-air-harvard-scientist-explains-the-new-reality-of-wildfire-season/">whopping withdrawals</a> that may be pushing them toward tipping points.</p>



<p>“We need to know what these forests are doing, and long-term research is the only way,” said <a href="https://salatainstitute.harvard.edu/people/n-michele-holbrook/">Michele Holbrook</a>, the Charles Bullard Professor of Forestry. “It’s hard to detect change when there’s so much year-to-year variation. You need decadal records. That’s what makes the tower, and the data it has collected, so important. In the global carbon cycle, the terrestrial biosphere — plants, animals, and soil microbes — takes in 25 to 30 percent of our emitted CO₂. Should it stop, we’re in trouble.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image alignfull size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1920" height="1280" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg" alt="Trees." class="wp-image-418373" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Trilling_Salata-1260-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">As Harvard Forest matures, and other species are felled by invasive species, red oaks are coming to dominate.</figcaption></figure>



<h5 class="wp-block-heading has-neue-haas-grotesk-text-font-family" id="h-biodiversity-in-flux"><strong>Biodiversity in flux</strong></h5>



<p>To a casual observer, Harvard Forest looks to be thriving. In the fall, the brush is so thick it can be hard to see the new tower from 50 feet away — perfect cover for cedar waxwings, great horned owls, and the occasional bobcat. A new shed houses the recording instruments and computers. Porcupines had gnawed into the old wooden building.</p>



<p>But the forest is changing. The native hemlock, ash, and beech are fading under blights spread by human movement and trade.</p>



<p>EMS 2.0 will measure the forest’s breathing well into the future, offering insights as the planet warms and the global community struggles to mitigate carbon emissions. It cannot see the slow turnover of species that will shape those numbers, but the tower will show how the system’s carbon balance shifts as the climate warms and invasive pests restructure the canopy.</p>



<p>“There aren’t that many species in this forest,” said Wofsy. “You’re really watching the forest be transformed by climate change and exotic species. That’s what lies ahead.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Harvard appoints four University Professors</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/harvard-appoints-four-university-professors/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Wed, 29 Oct 2025 17:00:00 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Campus & Community]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Awards & Honors]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Education]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Faculty]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Harvard History]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418611</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Dulac, Feldman, Goldin, and Vafa honored with highest faculty distinction]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below title-above-image centered-image"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/campus-community/"
		>
			Campus &amp; Community		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Harvard appoints four University Professors	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Noah Feldman (clockwise from upper left), Cumrun Vafa, Claudia Goldin, and Catherine Dulac." class="wp-image-418635" height="821" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=150,83 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=300,165 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=768,424 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=1024,565 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=1536,847 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=58,32 58w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=116,64 116w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=1488,821 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/UP1_1092.jpg?resize=1680,927 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Mark James Dunn, Harvard file photos/photo illustration by Liz Zonarich/Harvard Staff</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
							</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-29">
			October 29, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			9 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Dulac, Feldman, Goldin, and Vafa honored with highest faculty distinction		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Catherine Dulac, Noah Feldman, Claudia Goldin, and Cumrun Vafa were recognized with appointments as University Professors, the highest distinction a faculty member can receive at the University. The <a href="https://www.harvard.edu/about/university-professorships/">title</a> was created in 1935 to honor individuals whose groundbreaking work crosses the boundaries of multiple disciplines, allowing them to pursue research at any of Harvard’s Schools.</p>



<p>“University Professors embody the academic excellence that Harvard seeks to nurture in every generation,” said President Alan M. Garber. “Catherine, Claudia, Noah, and Cumrun are outstanding colleagues and superb University citizens. They represent not only the potential of individual scholars but also the timelessness of our mission to expand the frontiers of knowledge for the benefit of humanity. It is an honor to acknowledge their achievements and to celebrate their contributions to academia and to our community.”</p>



<ul class="wp-block-list">
<li><a href="https://www.mcb.harvard.edu/directory/catherine-dulac/">Dulac</a>, a Howard Hughes Medical Institute investigator who was most recently the <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2022/11/harvard-names-three-new-university-professors/">Samuel W. Morris University Professor</a>, will now be the Xander University Professor. The professorship was created for “an eminent scholar whose work focuses on new or emerging areas of scientific inquiry, especially areas at the intersection or interstices of existing scientific disciplines and fields.” Dulac succeeds <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/06/sons-diabetes-diagnosis-sent-scientist-on-quest-for-cure/">Douglas Melton</a>, who remains at Harvard as Catalyst Professor.</li>



<li><a href="https://hls.harvard.edu/faculty/noah-r-feldman/">Feldman</a>, the Felix Frankfurter Professor of Law, chair of the Society of Fellows, and founding director of the Julis-Rabinowitz Program on Jewish and Israeli Law, has been named the Arthur Kingsley Porter University Professor. The appointment honors a scholar in the humanities.</li>



<li><a href="https://www.economics.harvard.edu/people/claudia-goldin">Goldin</a>, the Henry Lee Professor of Economics and the Lee and Ezpeleta Professor of Arts and Sciences, has been appointed the Samuel W. Morris University Professor. The professorship was established to support a faculty member in the Faculty of Arts and Sciences.</li>



<li><a href="https://www.physics.harvard.edu/people/facpages/vafa">Vafa</a>, the Hollis Professor of Mathematicks and Natural Philosophy and chair of the Department of Physics, has been appointed the Timken University Professor. The appointment was established to honor a distinguished scholar in any field of science.</li>
</ul>



<p>“These four appointments represent the extraordinary breadth of excellence across our University,” said Harvard University Provost John F. Manning. “Not only are they great scholars and teachers, they are also extraordinary institutional contributors.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-large is-resized is-style-drop-shadow"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="1024" width="683" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?w=683" alt="Catherine Dulac." class="wp-image-418641" style="width:327px;height:auto" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Dulac_.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /></figure>



<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h3 class="wp-block-heading has-text-align-center" id="h-catherine-dulac">Catherine Dulac</h3>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-text-align-center has-h-5-title-font-size" id="h-xander-university-professor" style="margin-top:0;margin-right:0;margin-bottom:0;margin-left:0;padding-top:0;padding-right:0;padding-bottom:0;padding-left:0">Xander University Professor</h2>
</div>



<p>“It is a great honor to be named the Xander University Professor, and I wish to express my deep gratitude for this distinction and for the recognition of the research in my group,” Dulac said. “I feel extremely fortunate to be a faculty at Harvard and have the chance to interact daily with so many highly talented students, amazing lab members, and inspiring staff and colleagues, while fulfilling our scientific and teaching mission.”</p>



<p>Dulac has spent nearly two decades at Harvard working to expand the understanding of the neural processes underlying instinctive social behaviors, a set of brain functions that are typically impaired in mental illness. These functions include parental care and the brain’s responses to sickness and social isolation.</p>



<p>She has long been driven by a deep fascination with the interplay between social interactions and the human brain. In 2021, Dulac was awarded the prestigious <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2020/09/catherine-dulac-rewarded-for-study-of-parenting-behavior/">Breakthrough Prize in Life Sciences</a> for her research, which identified the neuronal circuits that control these behaviors and questioned the long-held belief that different circuits develop in male and female brains.</p>



<p>Dulac also started investigating social isolation and its effects on the brain in a new project that became particularly relevant during the pandemic. Her research indicates that the <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/02/is-social-connection-a-basic-need-like-food-water/">need for social connection</a> has similar neural circuitry as physiological needs, such as those for food, water, and sleep.</p>



<p>In addition to the Breakthrough Prize, Dulac has received the 2006 Richard Lounsbery Award and the 2015 National Academy of Sciences’ <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2015/04/three-faculty-members-receive-nas-awards/">Pradel Research Award</a>.</p>



<p>She is an elected member of the U.S. National Academy of Sciences, the French Academie des Sciences, and the American Philosophical Society. In addition, she is an officer of the French Legion d’Honneur.</p>



<p>Dulac graduated from École Normale Supérieure in Paris and received her Ph.D. from the University of Paris VI at the Institute of Molecular and Cellular Embryology of the College de France.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-large is-resized is-style-drop-shadow"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="1024" width="683" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?w=683" alt="Noah Feldman." class="wp-image-418638" style="width:327px;height:auto" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667-Feldman.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /></figure>



<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h3 class="wp-block-heading has-text-align-center" id="h-noah-feldman">Noah Feldman</h3>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-text-align-center has-h-5-title-font-size" id="h-arthur-kingsley-porter-university-professor" style="margin-top:0;margin-right:0;margin-bottom:0;margin-left:0;padding-top:0;padding-right:0;padding-bottom:0;padding-left:0">Arthur Kingsley Porter University Professor</h2>
</div>



<p>Feldman is a constitutional scholar whose work uses history, philosophy, literature, and religious studies to shed light on how we form and sustain communities in keeping with our values.</p>



<p>A prolific author, Feldman has written 10 books, including an ongoing series that traces the history of American constitutional ideas through the major figures who shaped them and another sequence on the trajectory of contemporary Islamic political thought.</p>



<p>“I’m deeply moved by this honor,” Feldman said. “I’m working on a book about the importance of being human in an age of artificial intelligence, so it is especially meaningful to hold a chair associated with humanistic study.”</p>



<p>Since 2020, Feldman has chaired Harvard’s Society of Fellows, the oldest and most eminent postdoctoral program in the U.S., which draws scholars from all disciplines. He was a junior fellow of the Society from 1998 to 2001 and became a senior fellow in 2010.</p>



<p>At Harvard Law School, Feldman is founding director of the <a href="https://pjil.law.harvard.edu/">Julis-Rabinowitz Program</a>, which strives “to present the full range of opinion and critique, internal and external, consistent always with the academic values of civility, openness, and mutual respect.”</p>



<p>In 2024, Feldman co-chaired the University’s Institutional Voice Working Group, which issued a report on official University statements about social and political matters.</p>



<p>“The main point of the report is that the University’s leadership can and should speak out on anything relevant to the core function of the University, which is creating an environment suitable for free, open inquiry, teaching, and research,” Feldman said at the time of the report’s release. “At the same time, the University as an institution should not make official statements on issues outside its core function.”</p>



<p>Feldman received his A.B. from Harvard summa cum laude, finishing first in his class. He earned his D.Phil. from Oxford University, where he studied as a Rhodes Scholar, and J.D. from Yale Law School, after which he clerked for Justice David H. Souter of the U.S. Supreme Court.</p>



<p>He is a fellow of the American Academy of Arts and Sciences.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-large is-resized is-style-drop-shadow"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="1024" width="683" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?w=683" alt="Claudia Goldin." class="wp-image-418639" style="width:327px;height:auto" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Goldin_Claudia.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /></figure>



<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h3 class="wp-block-heading has-text-align-center" id="h-claudia-goldin">Claudia Goldin</h3>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-text-align-center has-h-5-title-font-size" id="h-samuel-w-morris-university-professor" style="margin-top:0;margin-right:0;margin-bottom:0;margin-left:0;padding-top:0;padding-right:0;padding-bottom:0;padding-left:0">Samuel W. Morris University Professor</h2>
</div>



<p>A pioneer in the field of economic history, Goldin has explored women’s job market outcomes, their experiences balancing career and family, educational change, and the gender earnings gap across U.S. history.</p>



<p>In 2023, Goldin was <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2023/10/harvard-claudia-goldin-recognized-with-nobel-in-economic-sciences/">awarded the Sveriges Riksbank Prize in Economic Sciences in Memory of Alfred Nobel</a> “for having advanced our understanding of women’s labour market outcomes.”</p>



<p>After winning the prize, Goldin explained why she chose her area of study.</p>



<p>“When I first started working on this subject, I realized that most economic historians were studying child labor, or they were studying the labor of men,” she said. “But they didn’t really know what women were doing. And so that’s what I worked on.”</p>



<p>Goldin is known for her dedication to her students. The day after her Nobel win, she kept previously scheduled meetings, staying late at the office in Littauer Center with her “muses.”</p>



<p>Goldin is currently co-director of the National Bureau of Economic Research’s Gender in the Economy group and is a member of various academies and societies, including the U.S. National Academy of Sciences, the American Philosophical Society, and the Society of Labor Economists.</p>



<p>She received the 2019 BBVA Frontiers in Knowledge Award, the IZA Prize in Labor Economics in 2016, and the Mincer Prize in Labor Economics in 2009.</p>



<p>“The Race between Education and Technology,” a book by Goldin and Lawrence Katz, the Elisabeth Allison Professor of Economics, was awarded the 2008 R.R. Hawkins Award. Goldin’s most recent book, “Career and Family: Women’s Century-Long Journey Toward Equity,” has been translated into more than a dozen languages.</p>



<p>Goldin received her B.A. from Cornell University and her Ph.D. from the University of Chicago.</p>



<p>As a University Professor, Goldin will explore the expansion of women’s rights, the tensions between traditionalists and feminists, and why rights in the U.S. have increased but caregiving benefits have not kept up.</p>



<p>“I am deeply honored and thank President Garber for this unique opportunity,” Goldin said. “I look forward to creating a course to introduce our undergraduates to the cross-disciplinary insights of economic history that will reflect the true meaning of being a University Professor.”</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-large is-resized is-style-drop-shadow"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="1024" width="683" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?w=683" alt="Cumrun Vafa." class="wp-image-418640" style="width:327px;height:auto" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg 1667w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=768,1152 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=1024,1536 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=1366,2048 1366w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=43,64 43w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1667.Vafa_Cumrun.jpg?resize=1488,2232 1488w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1667px) 100vw, 1667px" /></figure>



<div class="wp-block-group has-global-padding is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<h3 class="wp-block-heading has-text-align-center" id="h-cumrun-vafa">Cumrun Vafa</h3>



<h2 class="wp-block-heading has-text-align-center has-h-5-title-font-size" id="h-timken-university-professor" style="margin-top:0;margin-right:0;margin-bottom:0;margin-left:0;padding-top:0;padding-right:0;padding-bottom:0;padding-left:0">Timken University Professor</h2>
</div>



<p>A prominent theoretical physicist, Vafa’s mathematical creativity has redefined modern theoretical physics and deeply influenced the fields of string theory, quantum gravity, and mathematical physics. His extensive achievements include developing topological string theory, uncovering holographic aspects of black holes, and formulating observable implications of string theory in cosmology and particle physics.</p>



<p>Vafa has also deepened the understanding of duality — showing how distinct theories can describe the same phenomena — through seminal work that yielded the Strominger-Vafa explanation of black hole entropy and the Gopakumar-Vafa invariants linking physics and enumerative geometry.</p>



<p>More recently, his leadership in the <a href="https://swampland-initiative.physics.harvard.edu/">Swampland Initiative</a> yielded predictive models for dark energy and dark matter, offering one of the most coherent theoretical frameworks consistent with the latest cosmological observations.</p>



<p>“It is truly humbling to receive this news,” said Vafa. “Every day at Harvard, I’m reminded that I am simply one faculty member among so many extraordinary colleagues, remarkable students, and dedicated staff.”</p>



<p>Vafa earned his B.S. in math and physics at MIT and his Ph.D. in theoretical physics at Princeton. He joined Harvard as a junior fellow in the Society of Fellows and became a faculty member in 1988. Vafa has been honored with the <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2016/12/recognition-for-discoveries/">2017 Breakthrough Prize</a> in Fundamental Physics and the 2013 <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/newsplus/two-recognized-for-physics-research/">Physics Frontiers Prize.</a></p>



<p>“I was born in Iran, the home of many eminent scientists and mathematicians. In my pursuit of science, I was inspired by their legacy,” Vafa said when accepting the Frontiers Prize. “I view science as a timeless, borderless adventure where everyone can participate, and it can bring out the best in humanity.”</p>



<p>Vafa also received the Dirac Medal of International Centre for Theoretical Physics in 2008, the 2016 Dannie Heineman Prize for Mathematical Physics, and an <a href="https://hetg.physics.harvard.edu/news/professor-cumrun-vafa-awarded-honorary-doctors-appointment-uppsala-university">Honorary Doctors Appointment at Uppsala University in 2019</a>. In addition, he was honored with the <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/newsplus/vafa-receives-ellis-island-medal-of-honor/">2017 Ellis Island Medal of Honor</a>, which “celebrates inspiring Americans who are selflessly working for the betterment of our country and its citizens.”</p>



<p>Vafa is an elected member of the U.S. National Academy of Sciences and the American Academy of Arts and Sciences.</p>
</div>

		<item>
		<title>‘Kids want to read harder stuff’</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/kids-want-to-read-harder-stuff/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[gazettebeckycoleman]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 28 Oct 2025 20:26:11 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Nation & World]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Books]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Education]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418659</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Are outdated teaching methods to blame for declining U.S. reading scores?]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-fixed-background has-overlay"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		‘Kids want to read harder stuff’	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><figure class="wp-block-image--fixed"><img decoding="async" alt="Child stands on tip-toes to reach for a book off a shelf." class="wp-image-418669" height="945" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/reading-scores-1920.jpg?resize=1680%2C945" width="1680" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/reading-scores-1920.jpg?resize=608,342 608w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/reading-scores-1920.jpg?resize=784,441 784w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/reading-scores-1920.jpg?resize=1024,576 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/reading-scores-1920.jpg?resize=1200,675 1200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/reading-scores-1920.jpg?resize=1488,837 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/reading-scores-1920.jpg?resize=1680,945 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /></figure></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Clea Simon	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Correspondent		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-28">
			October 28, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Are outdated teaching methods to blame for declining U.S. reading scores?		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Average reading scores for fourth-grade students in 2024 dropped two points since 2022 and five points since 2019, as measured by the so-called <a href="https://www.nationsreportcard.gov/reports/reading/2024/g4_8/">nation’s report card</a>. But according to a recent talk at Harvard’s Graduate School of Education, the trend of American children reading below their grade levels stretches back decades before the pandemic — and teaching methods that fail to adequately challenge students, counterintuitive as it may seem, could be the culprit.</p>



<p>“Look at data all the way back, starting in 1969. We’ve never improved,” said educator <a href="https://www.shanahanonliteracy.com/">Timothy Shanahan</a>, author of “<a href="https://hep.gse.harvard.edu/9798895570036/leveled-reading-leveled-lives/">Leveled Reading, Leveled Lives: How Students’ Reading Achievement Has Been Held Back and What We Can Do About It</a>.” “While the need for literacy has been increasing over the last five decades, kids’ literacy has not improved in the United States.”</p>



<p>The problem, said Shanahan, who joined Ed School Dean <a href="https://www.gse.harvard.edu/directory/faculty/nonie-lesaux">Nonie K. Lesaux</a> for the <a href="https://www.gse.harvard.edu/community/library/book-talks">Gutman Library Virtual Book Talk</a>, is rooted in an outdated premise. “There’s been a theory in reading education for about 100 years that if we’re going to be successful, we have to teach students at their level.” After years of testing ideas about “what ‘their level’ meant,” he said, educators arrived at a kind of consensus: Students are asked to read supposed grade-level passages and are then tested on their comprehension of them.</p>



<p>The problem with that approach is that it lacks challenge. “There’s nothing for the kids to learn,” said Shanahan, distinguished professor emeritus at the University of Illinois at Chi­cago, where he is founding di­rector of the UIC Center for Literacy.</p>



<p>The solution Shanahan outlined is to challenge students — but give them the right tools and support. For example, he proposed stopping the common practice of “pre-teaching” the vocabulary of a text before students try to read it. “We’re teaching words that are defined in the text. We’re teaching words you could figure out in context.” The result is that “kids are not seeing the text as something to be solved.”</p>



<p>“I don’t think kids enjoy it very much, and they’re not learning reading,” said Shanahan. “They’re just accumulating words.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32)"><blockquote><p>“We assume they’re de-motivated by difficulty. But research shows they’re de-motivated by the reading instruction.”</p></blockquote></figure>



<p>Questioning past assumptions is key. “We assume they’re de-motivated by difficulty,” he said. “But research shows they’re de-motivated by the reading instruction,” which, he stressed, can come across as “pretty babyish.”</p>



<p>Shanahan identified four basic concepts students learn in order to read: words and parts of words (morphology); fluency, which he defined as “where to pause and where to put emphasis” while reading; comprehension, or a grasp of an entire text; and writing, the ability to create texts on their own.</p>



<p>To master these, students must tackle texts that “pose linguistic or cognitive challenges,” he said.</p>



<p>“That might mean figuring out unknown words or figuring out sentences of complexity. What do you do with pronouns? What if there are too many pronouns? You want texts that are going to pose problems for kids.”</p>



<p>Instead of simply focusing on reading ability when choosing texts, teachers must look for content that will be interesting or engaging, or that has some social connection.</p>



<p>“Kids want to read harder stuff,” Shanahan said. Looking back on his beginners, he noted, “Even the lowest readers took on ‘Harry Potter,’” which mattered to their peer group. “Everybody wanted to be part of it.”</p>



<p>“We shouldn’t be trying to limit what kids take on,” he said. Instead, teachers and librarians should encourage readers to try new books, and offer them alternatives if what they first attempted proves too hard. “There’s nothing shameful in that,” he said. “We don’t want them afraid.”</p>



<p>Throughout the talk, Shanahan referred to the theories of Soviet Russian psychologist Lev Vygotsky, which emphasize learning through social interaction and making connections between prior knowledge and new information. Vygotsky, who studied children’s educational development, championed the idea of “scaffolding,” basically supporting learners so they can reach for a higher level on their own. The theory works for adults too, Shanahan said, as he discovered when he joined a group to read James Joyce’s “Ulysses.”</p>



<p>“I’d read it twice on my own. The first time I got nothing. The second time I felt I got a theme — one important but tiny theme in the book,” said Shanahan. With the group, he got much more out of this difficult text. “I needed a lot of support to read that book. It was a problem to be solved.”</p>

									<p class="featured-article__excerpt">
						In podcast, experts discuss why learning to love to read again may be key to reversing trend					</p>

					<description><![CDATA[Times columnist Michelle Goldberg discusses Israel, social conservatism, immigration, and where free speech becomes something else]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below title-above-image centered-image"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Finding boundaries of debate	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Derek Penslar (left) and Michelle Goldberg." class="wp-image-418576" height="992" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg 5812w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=2048,1365 2048w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101625_Goldberg_0344_2b4284.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 5812px) 100vw, 5812px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Derek Penslar and Michelle Goldberg.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Niles Singer/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Christy DeSmith	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-28">
			October 28, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Times columnist Michelle Goldberg discusses Israel, social conservatism, immigration, and where free speech becomes something else		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Anti-Zionism doesn’t top Michelle Goldberg’s list of fears.</p>



<p>“When I think about my future in the country, my family’s future in this country, I am so much more threatened by people who are opposed to pluralism,” said The New York Times columnist who describes herself as a secular Jew. “Our traditions of liberal pluralism are why Jews have thrived in the United States.”</p>



<p>Goldberg was on campus earlier this month for the 2025 Doft Lecture, hosted annually by the <a href="https://cjs.fas.harvard.edu/">Center for Jewish Studies.</a> Her wide-ranging conversation with center Director <a href="https://history.fas.harvard.edu/people/derek-penslar">Derek J. Penslar</a>, William Lee Frost Professor of Jewish History, covered everything from free speech to hate speech at an event titled “Authoritarianism, Antisemitism &amp; the Future of America.”</p>



<p>Goldberg explained that she doesn’t necessarily equate anti-Zionism with antisemitism.</p>



<p>As she has <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2024/03/11/opinion/antisemitism-vs-anti-zionism.html">noted in her work,</a> trying to force Jewish Americans, with their deep cultural and religious attachments to Israel, to disavow the movement for a Jewish nation is unquestionably antisemitic. But some Israel critics like New York City mayoral candidate Zohran Mamdani have been unfairly tarred as antisemites, she said.</p>



<p>They can’t reconcile the Jewish state with their commitments to pluralism, Goldberg said. “They basically say every state should be a state for all of its citizens. You can argue with that. You can say it’s naïve. But I fundamentally do not find it threatening.”</p>



<p>Penslar observed how adept partisans are at identifying antisemitism in their political opponents. “Do you honestly believe that antisemitism is primarily on one side or the other?” he asked.</p>



<p>“I deeply, honestly believe it’s worse on the right,” Goldberg replied, citing <a href="https://www.politico.com/news/2025/10/14/private-chat-among-young-gop-club-members-00592146">Politico’s recent report</a> on a leaked chat of Young Republicans leaders praising Hitler.</p>



<p>Goldberg later amended that. “I just think the antisemites on the left have dramatically less power,” she said.</p>



<p>Penslar asked where Goldberg draws the line between embracing viewpoint diversity and disavowing reprehensible ideas.</p>



<p>The Buffalo, N.Y., native whose political awakening came with pro-choice activism in the 1990s, spoke to the dangers of “artificially narrowing the boundaries of debate.” She cited <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2023/06/30/opinion/robert-f-kennedy-jr-coalition-supporters.html">her reporting</a> on how experts’ refusal to engage with concerns about COVID vaccines turned everyday skeptics into hard-line opponents.</p>



<p>At the other extreme, she sees the right embracing an ethos of “just asking questions” to jump the old guardrails on political speech and advance prejudice and untruths.&nbsp;</p>



<p>“I don’t know that anybody can say, ‘Here’s where the boundary should be,” she said.</p>



<p>Noting the rise of support among some Hispanic Americans for Donald Trump in 2024, Penslar wondered whether the values of pluralism mandate political consideration for the social conservatism of that segment of the voting bloc. Goldberg suggested that certain policy goals, such as banning same-sex marriage or outlawing abortion, may be incompatible with democracy.</p>



<p>The issue of immigration is next-level tricky. “I think most Democrats probably see America giving asylum to refugees as something sacred and fundamental,” she said.</p>



<p>Maintaining that access, while addressing some citizens’ concerns with immigration system abuses, would require increased infrastructure and investments.</p>



<p>“It’s much easier for Republicans, who don’t care about those values in the first place, to just say, ‘We’re just going to shut it down,’” Goldberg said.</p>



<p>In the Q&amp;A portion of the event, audience members and students solicited Goldberg’s thoughts on podcasting, polarized media, and where she sees distinctly American patterns in 21st-century attitudes toward Jewish populations.</p>



<p>“We are the country with the most Jews outside of Israel,” Goldberg said. There’s a “particular insult,” she continued, in attempts to dismantle institutions where Jewish Americans have historically thrived, like universities, “in the name of fighting antisemitism.”</p>



<p>Another audience member asked how much credit Trump deserves for brokering a ceasefire, however fragile, in Gaza, with the return of all living Israeli hostages. “A lot of credit,” she replied, but “not in the way certain people on the right are saying.”</p>



<p>This was essentially <a href="https://www.bbc.com/news/articles/cj3yke64vp6o">“the same formula”</a> that was on the table in January, when President Joe Biden was still in office, she argued. Trump was able to land a deal by “force of personality, by not being bound to certain norms of American diplomacy.”</p>



<p>Today’s Republican Party also gives Trump enormous leeway to act as he pleases, Goldberg added. “I actually think if Biden had forced [Israeli Prime Minister Benjamin] Netanyahu to accept the exact same deal, the Republicans would be saying he had sold out Israel.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
