		<item>
		<title>One idea for equalizing higher education: admissions lotteries</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/universities-need-to-vindicate-our-public-purpose-sandel-says/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Tue, 28 Oct 2025 15:54:37 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Nation & World]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Civil Discourse]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Democracy]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Education]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418589</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[David Deming and Randall Kennedy discuss — and debate — good, bad of meritocracy with 'Justice' philosopher]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-overlay has-uncropped-image"
	style=" --min-height: 66.69921875vw;"
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Universities need to ‘vindicate our public purpose,’ Sandel says	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="A lecture at Harvard Radcliffe Institute. " class="wp-image-418595" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_730.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Veasey Conway/Harvard Staff Photographer

</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Christina Pazzanese	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-28">
			October 28, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			4 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			David Deming and Randall Kennedy discuss — and debate — good, bad of meritocracy with &#039;Justice&#039; philosopher		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Higher education may be an important source of economic and social mobility, but it doesn’t do nearly enough to meaningfully reduce inequality in the U.S., according to political philosopher Michael Sandel.</p>



<p>And the prestige that elite schools enjoy reinforces privilege, and often leads graduates to see themselves as society’s rightful winners, he added. </p>



<p>“[That] leads to hubris among the winners, leads them to inhale too deeply of their own success, to forget the luck and good fortune that helped them on their way, to forget their indebtedness to those who made their achievements possible,” Sandel said during a lecture last Thursday at <a href="https://www.radcliffe.harvard.edu/">Harvard Radcliffe Institute</a>. “And it leads to humiliation to those left behind, those less fortunate than themselves.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?w=1024" alt="Michael Sandel." class="wp-image-418596" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_275.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Michael Sandel.</figcaption></figure>



<p>The lecture, titled “Does Meritocracy Undermine Democracy?” and moderated by Harvard Radcliffe Dean Tomiko Brown-Nagin, was followed by a panel discussion with economist and Harvard College Dean <a href="https://www.daviddeming.com/">David Deming</a> and Harvard Law School Professor <a href="https://hls.harvard.edu/faculty/randall-l-kennedy/">Randall Kennedy</a>. It was the first in a new series that’s part of a five-year <a href="https://www.radcliffe.harvard.edu/about-the-institute/our-work/academic-freedom">initiative</a> launched last fall at Radcliffe to promote civil discourse and academic freedom.</p>



<p>The current populist backlash against higher ed is driven in large part by a view among many working people “that credentialed elites look down on them,” said Sandel, the Anne T. and Robert M. Bass Professor of Government. It’s “a legitimate complaint” given that 62 percent of Americans don’t have a four-year college degree, and it deepens cultural divisions.<strong> &nbsp;</strong></p>



<p>Top colleges and universities can begin to counteract that perception by using a lottery system to select from among the thousands of worthy and qualified students who apply each year.</p>



<p>A lottery would minimize the significant advantages wealthy applicants still retain in the “meritocratic tournament of college admissions” and relieve high school students of the relentless pressure to compete with each other, argued Sandel, who has written widely on the topics of justice and inequity, including 2020’s “The Tyranny of Merit.”</p>



<p>Deming, an economist who studies education, supports the lottery idea and the principle of greater transparency that’s behind it, but disagreed with Sandel that higher education is undermining democracy. The country should make greater investments in higher education so more people can attend and thrive, he said.</p>



<p>“We’ve gone from a world where probably only one in 10 or one in eight people need to go to college to a world where almost everyone does, and yet we don’t spend enough money as a society on providing the resources to make that possible,” said Deming.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-large"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" height="683" width="1024" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?w=1024" alt="Panel moderator Tomiko Brown-Nagin (from left), Michael Sandel, Randall Kennedy, and David Deming.
" class="wp-image-418594" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102325_Sandel_578.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption">Tomiko Brown-Nagin (from left), Michael Sandel, Randall Kennedy, and David Deming.</figcaption></figure>



<p>“The fundamental source of inequality is that so many young people who have talent but are not ready to take full advantage of those talents are not able to achieve their potential because they do not have the resources devoted to them that a place like Harvard or other universities provide,” said Deming, who noted half of the Class of 2029 pays no tuition.</p>



<p>Kennedy said while he shared Sandel’s criticism of meritocratic hubris, eliminating all competition between students is not an effective solution to rising inequality.</p>



<p>One driver of populist complaints about higher education that gets overlooked is the “aggressive, anti-meritocratic envy and resentment” of those attacking Harvard and other colleges and universities, said Kennedy, a scholar of racial equality. There’s also been “a concerted campaign to vilify the universities and undercut the public’s trust” by political partisans in the media, he said. </p>



<p>“The subtext of some of your remarks was that we have brought it on ourselves and deserve to be brought low,” Kennedy said to Sandel. “I don’t think so. I think at this moment, we face the perennial adversaries of excellence, and we need to be willing to protect legitimate meritocracy.”</p>



<p>To win back and be worthy of public trust, higher education needs to “vindicate our public purpose,” said Sandel, who was <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/newsplus/justice-professor-michael-sandel-wins-1m-philosophy-prize/">awarded</a> the Berggruen Prize for Philosophy and Culture earlier this month.</p>



<p>“We have to show that in addition to launching our students on successful careers, we are institutions who serve the public good, and I think we’ve strayed from that mission,” he said.</p>



<p>“I think we can and should do more to show that that our mission is not to promote private privilege, but to contribute to the public good.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>Rising birth rates no longer tied to economic prosperity</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/rising-birth-rates-no-longer-tied-to-economic-prosperity/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 27 Oct 2025 19:52:48 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Nation & World]]></category>

		<category><![CDATA[Gender & Sexuality]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Health Care]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[History]]></category>

		<category><![CDATA[Research]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Women's Health]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418516</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[New research by Claudia Goldin extends her work on how, why cultural changes around gender are driving down fertility in U.S., elsewhere]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-overlay"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Rising birth rates no longer tied to economic prosperity	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Single infant in nursery at hospital." class="wp-image-418539" height="945" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Single-infant-in-newborn-suite.jpg?resize=1680%2C945" width="1680" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Single-infant-in-newborn-suite.jpg?resize=608,342 608w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Single-infant-in-newborn-suite.jpg?resize=784,441 784w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Single-infant-in-newborn-suite.jpg?resize=1024,576 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Single-infant-in-newborn-suite.jpg?resize=1200,675 1200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Single-infant-in-newborn-suite.jpg?resize=1488,837 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Single-infant-in-newborn-suite.jpg?resize=1680,945 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Sean Gallup/Getty Images</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Clea Simon	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Correspondent		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-27">
			October 27, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			New research by Claudia Goldin extends her work on how, why cultural changes around gender are driving down fertility in U.S., elsewhere		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Fertility rates began falling in most of the world starting in the last century. By the 1970s, the U.S. had dipped under the replacement rate of 2.1 children per woman, a trend that has continued on a declining slope.</p>



<p>In her new working paper, “<a href="https://www.nber.org/papers/w34268?utm_campaign=ntwh&amp;utm_medium=email&amp;utm_source=ntwg5">The Downside of Fertility</a>,” Claudia Goldin, the Henry Lee Professor of Economics, takes a deeper dive into the cultural changes around gender that are driving down fertility rates.</p>



<p>The economic historian and <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2023/10/harvard-claudia-goldin-recognized-with-nobel-in-economic-sciences/">2023 Nobel laureate</a> introduced <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/01/need-to-boost-population-encourage-dads-to-step-up-at-home/">a model</a> in a previous publication that links low fertility rates to higher levels of friction between the sides around gender and generation. This paper, she said, tackles a seeming contradiction in the data.</p>



<p>Across the 18th and 19th centuries increases in the birth rate were seen during periods of prosperity or at the end of economic downturns, as evidenced by the post-World War II baby boom. However, since the later part of the 20th century, that link no longer holds true.</p>



<p>“One of the great demographic surprises” of this time, Goldin writes, “has been the negative relationship between per capita income levels and fertility.”</p>



<p>Although fertility rates fell earlier and faster in developed countries, defined by Goldin as members of the <a href="https://www.oecd.org/en.html">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a>, they have now gone down even in sub-Saharan Africa.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-pullquote" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32);margin-right:0;margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32);margin-left:0;padding-top:0;padding-right:0;padding-bottom:0;padding-left:0"><blockquote><p>“When you look at what these birth rates were for just the previous generation, it’s astronomical change.”</p></blockquote></figure>



<p>“The fertility decline is just about everywhere,” said Goldin, who is also the Lee and Ezpeleta Professor of Arts and Sciences.</p>



<p>The wealthier Middle Eastern countries had birth rates around six or seven children per woman, but the United Arab Emirates “has a birth rate now that is lower or about the same as baby-scarce Japan,” dipping “to about 1.2,” Goldin said, while the birth rate in Saudi Arabia is 2.3.</p>



<p>“When you look at what these birth rates were for just the previous generation, it’s astronomical change,” she said.</p>



<p>The global shift, she continued, can be traced to one common cause: the increased agency of women, who during the last century became increasingly able to opt out of traditional family roles to pursue other options, such as education and careers.</p>



<p>“Wherever you get increased agency, you get reduction in the birth rate,” said Goldin. “The central cause arises when women have autonomy and have the ability to be educated, to have occupations and careers.”</p>



<p>This can lead to conflict with men, who are more comfortable with traditional roles. “Men don’t want to give up what they had in the past,” Goldin said.</p>



<p>Goldin’s close read on the historical patterns revealed fascinating subtleties.</p>



<p>Fertility rates were declining at a slower pace in the U.S. until the onset of the “Great Recession” in 2007. In Northern Europe, rates started declining faster in 2010.</p>



<p>However, the decreases were “substantial” among college graduates, who delayed or avoided having children despite, on average, enjoying higher earnings. This avoidance is “testament to the mismatch factors” that Goldin has brought to light in <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2023/10/excerpt-from-career-and-family-by-claudia-goldin/">previous work</a>.</p>



<p>In other words, men want to start families, but they don’t face the same obstacles combining employment and parenthood. And women, by and large, want time for both work and children.</p>



<p>By now, birth rates have been in decline, at least in rich nations, for 50 years. So Goldin can’t help but wonder: “Why are we talking about this now?”</p>



<p>She referred to the current, often-heated public discourse around ways to increase birth rates in the U.S. Until we, as a society, address the real underlying causes, Goldin said, “you really can’t address what policies can address the declining fertility rate.”</p>



<p>And the issues, she notes, are not just a concern of the left but bridge the political divide.</p>



<p>“One of the things that is important to know is that traditional women today, evangelical women today still want respect in the workplace,” she said. “They still want to know that they’re being promoted appropriately, that they’re being paid the correct amount. It’s not as if women are eschewing all of the rights that they have gained over time.”</p>



<p>The issue of falling birth rates underlies everything, argued Goldin, who is currently working on a book about women’s rights.</p>



<p>“I’ve written on various aspects of demography,” she said. “About the first <a href="https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w21263/revisions/w21263.rev0.pdf">declines in infant and child mortality</a> in America and the role of public health and the cleaning up of the nation’s water supply and the separation of the sewage from clean water. I’ve written about why <a href="https://www.nber.org/system/files/working_papers/w24716/w24716.pdf">women live longer than men</a> and whether that’s always been the case.” (The answer is no.)</p>



<p>“The issues concerning the change in population, about families and children, are absolutely fundamental to everything that I do,” concluded Goldin. “I can’t talk about the labor force unless I talk about population.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>The gum disease, diabetes connection</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/the-gum-disease-diabetes-connection/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 27 Oct 2025 19:44:15 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Health]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Health Care]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Q&A]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418564</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Periodontist explains how the relationship between the two diseases shapes patient care and prevention]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-overlay"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/health/"
		>
			Health		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		The gum disease, diabetes connection	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Illustration of tooth decay with inflammation and gum disease." class="wp-image-418567" height="945" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Gum-disease.jpg?resize=1680%2C945" width="1680" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Gum-disease.jpg?resize=608,342 608w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Gum-disease.jpg?resize=784,441 784w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Gum-disease.jpg?resize=1024,576 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Gum-disease.jpg?resize=1200,675 1200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Gum-disease.jpg?resize=1488,837 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Gum-disease.jpg?resize=1680,945 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Kathleen Refior	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			HSDM Communications		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-27">
			October 27, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			3 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Periodontist explains how the relationship between the two diseases shapes patient care and prevention		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>For people living with diabetes, caring for their gums can be just as important as managing blood sugar. Research has shown that there is a two-way relationship between periodontal disease — more commonly known as gum disease — and diabetes.</p>



<p>People with periodontitis have a higher risk of diabetes, and patients with diabetes are three times more likely to develop periodontal disease. About 50 percent of adults worldwide are affected by gum disease, contributing to the more than 3.5 billion people impacted by oral diseases each year, according to the World Health Organization (WHO).</p>



<p>To shed light on this connection, Harvard School of Dental Medicine spoke with David Wu, an instructor in the School’s Department of Oral Medicine, Infection, and Immunity, and program director of the Advanced Graduate Education Program in Periodontology.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-narrow-single-line"/>



<p><strong>Can you explain why people with diabetes are at higher risk for gum disease?</strong></p>



<p>Individuals with diabetes are at increased risk for gum disease due to persistently elevated blood sugar levels, which can impair the body’s ability to fight infection and promote chronic inflammation in the mouth. This weakened immune response combined with higher amount of glucose in your saliva allows harmful oral bacteria to flourish and plaque to build up, increasing the likelihood of gum tissue breakdown and periodontal infection.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1980" height="1320" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg" alt="David Wu." class="wp-image-418570" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/1980.David-Wu.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /></figure>



<p><strong>What early signs of gum disease should someone with diabetes be aware of?</strong></p>



<p>Early symptoms of gum disease may include gums that are red, swollen, or bleed easily, especially during brushing or flossing. Persistent bad breath, gum tenderness, receding gum lines, and loose teeth can also signal the onset of periodontal problems. These early signs are often painless, but should not be ignored and warrant timely evaluation by a dental professional.</p>



<p><strong>Can treating gum disease help improve diabetes management?</strong></p>



<p>Yes, effective management of gum disease may lead to improvements in blood sugar control. Treating gum disease reduces chronic inflammation in the body, which can enhance the body’s response to insulin and stabilize metabolic health. The key take away is that integrating routine dental care with diabetes management is key to achieving better overall outcomes for patients. Prevention, early diagnosis, and intervention can lead to best clinical outcomes.</p>



<p><strong>Are there any recent research findings that have changed how we understand the link between diabetes and oral health?</strong></p>



<p>Recent scientific advances underscore the bidirectional relationship between diabetes and gum disease. New findings indicate that inflammation from periodontal disease can contribute to worsening blood sugar control, while uncontrolled diabetes may fuel oral infection. Studies are also exploring how genetics and the oral microbiome may influence this complex interplay, heralding a new era of collaborative care between dental and medical professionals.</p>



<p><strong>What daily habits or preventive measures are most effective for protecting gum health in people with diabetes?</strong></p>



<p>Adhering to a thorough oral hygiene routine, including twice-daily brushing, daily flossing, and regular dental checkups, is essential to overall health. Optimal blood sugar control, a balanced diet, and avoidance of tobacco products further support gum health.</p>



<p>Consultation with a periodontist (gum specialist), and professional dental hygiene therapy at least twice a year are strongly recommended, as is prompt attention to any oral changes. For patients with diabetes, the periodontist may recommend a maintenance visit every three months to adequately monitor disease status and halt progression.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>Pursuit of justice borne of personal experience with injustice</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/pursuit-of-justice-borne-of-personal-experience-with-injustice/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[gazettebeckycoleman]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 27 Oct 2025 18:26:30 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Nation & World]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Faculty]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Law]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[World Politics]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418533</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Rosalie Abella, the first Jewish woman on Canada’s Supreme Court, was shaped by her parents’ resilience after Holocaust]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below has-uncropped-image"
	style=" --min-height: 66.69921875vw;"
>
	
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Rosalie Abella." class="wp-image-418534" height="683" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg" width="1024" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1025 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=1488,993 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_083-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1121 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Photos by Veasey Conway/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		Pursuit of justice borne of personal experience with injustice	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Jacob Sweet	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-27">
			October 27, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			9 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Rosalie Abella, the first Jewish woman on Canada’s Supreme Court, was shaped by her parents’ resilience after Holocaust		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignfull has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
	<div class="series-badge" style="">
		<h2 class="series-badge__header wp-block-heading has-series-logo">
			<a class="series-badge__logo" href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/series/life-work/">
	
		<img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="214" height="32" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/2023_03_13_Gazette_Life-Work_Series_logo_AA.jpg?w=214" class="attachment-series-logo size-series-logo" alt="Life | Work series" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/2023_03_13_Gazette_Life-Work_Series_logo_AA.jpg 723w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/2023_03_13_Gazette_Life-Work_Series_logo_AA.jpg?resize=150,22 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/2023_03_13_Gazette_Life-Work_Series_logo_AA.jpg?resize=300,45 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/2023_03_13_Gazette_Life-Work_Series_logo_AA.jpg?resize=214,32 214w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2024/01/2023_03_13_Gazette_Life-Work_Series_logo_AA.jpg?resize=428,64 428w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 723px) 100vw, 723px" />			</a>
	
	</h2>					<p class="series-badge__description">
				A series focused on the personal side of Harvard research and teaching.			</p>
			</div>

	


<p>Rosalie Abella was 4 years old when she learned that her father hadn’t been allowed to practice law in Canada.</p>



<p>“I had no idea what that even meant,” said Abella, the first Jewish woman on the Supreme Court of Canada. “But it galvanized me and committed me to the idea that I would be what he couldn’t be.”</p>



<p>An émigré and child of death camp survivors, Abella faced daunting challenges in becoming one of Canada’s most influential jurists. Now the Samuel and Judith Pisar Visiting Professor of Law at Harvard Law School, she lifted barriers for others through her work in labor and disability rights, constitutional law, and human rights jurisprudence.</p>



<p>Abella’s father, Jacob Silberman, was born in Sienno, Poland, and entered Krakow’s Jagiellonian law school in 1930 as one of the few Jewish students in the program.</p>



<p>By the time he graduated, practicing in his home country was no longer an option. The Nazis had taken over, and he and his wife were sent to concentration camps. They both survived, but Silberman’s parents, three younger brothers, and first-born son — Abella’s brother — were killed.</p>



<p>Abella was born in 1946 in a displaced person’s camp in Stuttgart, Germany. Her father taught himself English and was briefly able to practice law alongside American lawyers, helping to develop a system of legal services for displaced people.</p>



<p>The family got the chance to move to Canada in 1950. Silberman assumed he’d be able to practice there, too. He brought letters of reference from the American judges and lawyers he’d worked with, who wrote glowingly about his abilities.</p>



<p>Their endorsements proved meaningless.</p>



<p>“He came home and said, ‘I can’t do it because I’m not a citizen,’” Abella said. The process to get citizenship would take half a decade, and Silberman had a family to support. He became an insurance agent instead.</p>



<p>In the decades that followed, Abella never heard her parents complain about the injustices they had experienced. Instead, they carried a level of optimism and positivity that was sometimes hard for her to comprehend.</p>



<p>“I don’t think I ever saw them angry about anything that happened to them,” Abella said. “It’s amazing when I think about it. They were so grateful for what they had found in Canada that they didn’t dwell on what they had lost.”</p>



<p>They raised their children to carry themselves as if they could achieve anything with enough hard work and determination.</p>



<p>“It was a rigorously programmed life in terms of the unstated expectation that we would do the best we could possibly do,” Abella said. “I never heard them say, ‘Why can’t you do it as well as so-and-so?’ It was always measuring me against my abilities and stretching them as far as they could go.”</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image aligncenter size-full is-resized is-style-drop-shadow"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1281" height="1920" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg" alt="Rosalie Abella." class="wp-image-418543" style="width:400px" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg 1281w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg?resize=100,150 100w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg?resize=200,300 200w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg?resize=768,1151 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg?resize=683,1024 683w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg?resize=1025,1536 1025w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg?resize=21,32 21w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/101425_RosalieAbellaWorkLife_114-1920.jpg?resize=43,64 43w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1281px) 100vw, 1281px" /></figure>



<p>Abella liked working hard and asked for extra homework because her parents taught her that education was important. She saw school as the “last meritocracy,” where nobody could hold you back based on race, color, and religion as long as you put in the work.</p>



<p>By the time she graduated from law school, she’d become aware of the barriers for women in the professional world, but she wasn’t daunted. “It was too late because I was already formed,” she said, “and I was formed as someone who was not afraid.”</p>



<p>Abella saw herself as an outsider. She knew she didn’t dress or think like many of her peers in classes and later in courtrooms, being both Jewish and a woman. But she proceeded with her studies as if the differences wouldn’t matter.</p>



<p>“This was Canada, the land of opportunities,” she said, “and if people were willing to give them to me, I was going to grab them — even if people around me were saying, ‘Wait a minute, that isn’t what women are supposed to do.’”</p>



<p>She fell in love with a history professor — Irving Abella — who, like her parents, also didn’t see any reason why a woman shouldn’t pursue the career she wanted.</p>



<p>“The transfer of confidence from my parents believing in me to a husband who believed in me was total luck — kind of, as Bette Midler would say, ‘the wind beneath my wings.’”</p>



<p>Abella didn’t have any end goal in sight, except to be a lawyer — a good one. She was particularly hungry for chances to learn, willing to bypass higher salary and prestige for a job that would broaden her experience.</p>



<p>After starting her career in private practice, she accepted an appointment — when she was pregnant with her second child at 29 — to the Ontario Family Court, becoming the youngest person and the first pregnant woman ever appointed to the bench in Canada.</p>



<p>There she handled cases involving issues such as child protection and young offender proceedings. At times, she would have to determine whether people could keep their children — as she herself thought of her two young sons at home.</p>



<p>“It taught me what the world was for most people,” Abella said, “and it taught me to be open-minded, not to look at court cases as a judge from the top down, and to actually listen to what they were telling you.”</p>



<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--32)">
<p class="has-neutral-white-color has-secondary-green-background-color has-text-color has-background has-link-color wp-elements-ae9fce282bbed0588985a550ad6851d6">Abella created a definition of equity in employment that would have far-reaching effects in the Canadian justice system: “Equality means accommodating and acknowledging people’s differences so they can be treated as equals.”</p>
</blockquote>



<p>She had another chance to listen as the sole commissioner of the Royal Commission on Equality in Employment. Abella held sessions around the country, talking to women, Indigenous people, people with disabilities, and others from under-represented groups about employment barriers. Then she met with business and labor leaders to figure out practical ways to solve the issues.</p>



<p>Her time on the Family Court, and her own upbringing, guided her.</p>



<p>“I was always interested in other people’s stories because mine is so different from anybody else’s that I knew growing up,” she said. “I heard what it felt like to have a different color, to have a different gender. I learned the tenacity of a barrier when you’re disabled. I heard about racism. I heard about things that I had not personally experienced, and that’s what being a judge is: learning to listen and not hearing your own story, but actually hearing their story. For me, it was about fairness”</p>



<p>In the commission’s final report, Abella created a definition of equity in employment that would have far-reaching effects in the Canadian justice system: “Equality means accommodating and acknowledging people’s differences so they can be treated as equals.”</p>



<p>“I just felt it growing up. I felt how unfair it had been that 6 million people who were Jewish were exterminated because they were Jewish,” she said. Her time on the Family Court and working in private practice informed her thinking about balancing group rights, which was about human rights, and the civil libertarian theory of the individual. “I wasn’t aware of any of that in law school,” she said. “It was only my clients and then the family court that made me understand.”</p>



<p>Five years later, in its first decision on equality rights, the Supreme Court of Canada struck down the citizenship requirement for lawyers using Abella’s definition. Her father, who died a month before she finished law school, wasn’t able to see her complete the circle, but her mother, Fanny, was.</p>



<p>“I felt so grateful for the fact that she was there on their behalf to see what had happened to the career he launched by not being able to be a lawyer in Canada,” Abella said, “a career that she and my husband nurtured and encouraged.”</p>



<p>Abella brought her ideas on equality to the Ontario Court of Appeals, where she began in 1992, and then to the Supreme Court in 2004.</p>



<p>Though she learned to listen differently — to lawyers instead of from clients directly as she had during Family Court — she made decisions with personal ramifications in mind.</p>



<p>“Every case to me was a novel,” Abella said. “It didn’t matter whether it was copyright or tax or equality or freedom of expression. It was always about how the law affected people.”</p>



<p>Taking this approach meant sometimes finding that the opinion she had formed after days studying the case was wrong. “You have to actually hear what parties are arguing and saying,” she said. “And if you can’t do it with an open mind, you shouldn’t be there — because there’s always a chance that you’re wrong.”</p>



<p>Her willingness to go out on a limb to protect or expand rights — and the jurisprudential status quo — often drew criticism. In 2020’s Fraser v. Canada, Abella’s majority opinion found that the Royal Canadian Mounted Police officers’ pension plan discriminated against women who needed to reduce hours to take care of children. Some justices and academics disagreed, calling it judicial overreach.</p>



<p>Abella’s attention to the law’s treatment of minority groups drove many similar decisions<strong>.</strong> She wrote the first appellate gay rights rulings and paved the way for marriage equality in Canada, protected workers’ bargaining rights, invented employment equity, and advanced fairness for women and minorities in others.</p>



<p>“There were so many things that caused the public to say, ‘Wait a minute, are we ready for this?’” Abella said. “And it was my view that that’s what you have judges for.” If judges couldn’t make decisions that protected the public interest as opposed to public opinion, “then I don’t think we’re doing our job, and we’re mixing up what elected bodies do with what independent bodies, like the judiciary, do.”</p>



<p>She also credits her husband, Irving, who died in 2022, for encouraging her to ignore the controversies and do what she thought was right. He was excited about Abella’s opportunity to teach at Harvard, as were her two adult sons, both lawyers. “It’s great to have professional satisfaction and respect,” Abella said, “but the core is family.”</p>



<p>All the way, she channeled the lessons she learned from her parents. “They were extraordinary people whose core commitment to integrity was unwavering,” Abella said. “It was important to them that whatever I do, I do with dignity and respect.”</p>



<p>In taking up the career her father was denied, Abella built a legacy all her own.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
					
		
		
		<post-id xmlns="com-wordpress:feed-additions:1">418533</post-id>	</item>
		<item>
		<title>‘We don’t need zombies to do ourselves in as a species’</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/we-dont-need-zombies-to-do-ourselves-in-as-a-species/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Liz Mineo]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Mon, 27 Oct 2025 16:31:17 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Arts & Culture]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Books]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Faculty]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Psychology]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418383</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[How a fake medical paper sparked novel once optioned by director of ‘Night of the Living Dead’  ]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-fullscreen has-purple-color has-overlay"
	style="
		--primary-page-color-bright: var(--color-purple-bright);
		--primary-page-color-text: var(--color-purple-dark);
		--primary-page-color-ui: var(--color-purple);
		--primary-page-color-reverse-background: var(--color-purple);
		--primary-page-color-reverse-text: var(--color-white);
		--primary-page-color-reverse-ui: var(--color-white);
	 "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/arts-humanities/"
		>
			Arts &amp; Culture		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		‘We don’t need zombies to do ourselves in as a species’	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-video wp-block-video--ambient"><video autoplay loop muted playsinline src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/Untitled-design-5.mp4"></video><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Illustration by Liz Zonarich/Harvard Staff</p></figcaption><button aria-label="Pause ambient video" class="video-ambient-controls pause"></button></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Liz Mineo	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-27">
			October 27, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			7 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			How a fake medical paper sparked novel once optioned by director of ‘Night of the Living Dead’  		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Steven Schlozman was 11 years old when he watched “Salem’s Lot,” a made-for-TV movie based on the Stephen King book about a Maine town invaded by vampires. He was bitten. After experiencing a “fun kind of scare,” he became a horror fan.</p>



<p>A former assistant professor of psychiatry at Harvard Medical School, Schlozman is the author of “The Zombie Autopsies: Secret Notebooks from the Apocalypse” (2011), whose film rights at one time were optioned by the late George Romero, director of the archetypal zombie horror film “<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=o7xO_ciIEeA">Night of the Living Dead</a>” (1968).</p>



<p>In this interview, which has been edited for clarity and length, Schlozman talks about why he believes zombies are the scariest horror trope.</p>



<hr class="wp-block-separator has-alpha-channel-opacity is-style-squares"/>



<p><strong>Can you explain the psychology behind the attraction to horror?</strong></p>



<p>In terms of psychology, there are several theories. There’s the idea that watching a horror movie is experiencing fear in a safe environment, similar to riding a roller coaster. A cognitive explanation is that some people like horror films because they like to figure out what makes them scared. It’s a process of metacognition; it’s thinking about what you’re thinking. Because at the end of the day, you’re sitting in a movie theater, or at home, and there’s nothing really to be afraid of.</p>



<p>There’s also a whole literature on the thrill-seeking temperament. These are folks who get a serotonin or a dopamine rush with the thrill. You don’t really think your life is in danger for something that’s happening on a 2D screen, but the thrill of it makes you feel euphoric. There is also the catharsis theory, which allows us a release of emotions. Lots of studies say that people actually feel calmer and even less sad after watching a horror film.</p>



<p><strong>In a famous </strong><a href="https://www.drmarkwomack.com/pdfs/why_we_crave_horror_movies.pdf"><strong>essay</strong></a><strong>, Stephen King talks about the need for horror movies as a kind of release valve for our worst impulses. What is your take?</strong></p>



<p>We’ve been telling scary stories since we could tell stories. A lot of stories we read in our childhood are scary stories, and who has not enjoyed listening to a scary story around the campfire?</p>



<blockquote class="wp-block-quote is-layout-flow wp-block-quote-is-layout-flow" style="margin-top:var(--wp--preset--spacing--64);margin-bottom:var(--wp--preset--spacing--64);padding-top:0;padding-bottom:0">
<p>“We need horror stories because they help us confront our fears, and they allow us to have hard conversations about things that we have a hard time talking about, except in the displacement of a movie. ”</p>
</blockquote>



<p>We need horror stories because they help us confront our fears, and they allow us to have hard conversations about things that we have a hard time talking about, except in the displacement of a movie. “Rosemary’s Baby” (1968) deals with the plights of motherhood, “Night of the Living Dead” (1968) and Get Out” (2017) with racism, and “The Babadook” (2014) with trauma and the challenges of having a child who can be difficult to manage. There is also the fear of death, and of the unknown. We fear death because we don’t know what happens after we die. Horror films allow you to deal with fears of what is unknowable.</p>



<p><strong>Let’s talk about zombies. Why are zombies more terrifying than vampires, werewolves, or ghosts?</strong></p>



<p>What’s scary about zombies is that they look so much like us; they were humans before they were infected, but they’re not humans anymore. Even if a zombie looks like your grandmother, when she gets close to you, she’ll lunge and bite you, and it’s a done deal. But the really terrifying thing about zombies isn’t that they will bite you and turn you into a zombie; it’s the idea that humans can get robbed of themselves, and that’s really scary. The idea of a person not being themselves, but still recognizable as a person, is a terrifying notion. It’s also what scares us about sick people because if someone is very ill, they stop looking like themselves, or at least they look like a sick version of themselves.</p>



<p><strong>In your novel, you explained in a scientific way what’s going in the brains and bodies of zombies. Can you tell us more?</strong></p>



<p>I wrote a fake medical paper about zombies, and that was the origin of my book. It’s an epistolary novel, which is a series of diary entries or letters, combining real references with fake references. I wanted it to feel real. I wanted it to feel possible. I came up with a fake name for the zombie disease based on its symptoms. I called it Ataxic Neurodegenerative Satiety Deficiency Syndrome (ANSD). Ataxic means loss of balance; neurodegenerative refers to the brain’s degradation; satiety deficiency means that the satiety regions of the stomach are no longer functioning, so you just keep eating. And syndrome is just a fancy word in medicine for “We don’t actually know what causes this.”</p>



<p>I explain zombie neurology by pointing at different impaired regions of the zombie brain. When you look at the stumbling ghouls in Romero’s “Night of the Living Dead,” you notice that they have lost their sense of balance, which is located in the basal ganglia. They can’t think very well or even open a window, so there must be something wrong with the frontal lobe. They’re hungry all the time, so their satiety regions are not functioning well. When I wrote the novel, I made the zombie disease to be a result of a manufactured pathogen by people who bet against the market when it plunged. I wrote the book in 2011, and the financial crash of 2008 was still on my mind. In most zombie movies, the bad guy is never the zombies.</p>



<p><strong>George Romero, the film director, praised your novel, and the two of you were working on a screenplay. What happened?</strong></p>



<p>George optioned the film rights for my book. He and I worked on the screenplay together, but no one bought it. Someday, somebody will hopefully buy it. It’s been optioned two more times since then, and it’s never been made. George was a lovely person; we enjoyed spending time with each other. He was a little melancholic about what had happened to zombies, that they have become a video game thing. He wanted to get away from that; he wanted it to be, ultimately, a kind of sad story.</p>



<p><strong>A common Google search asks whether zombies can exist. Can they?</strong></p>



<p>No. In fact, when I was at Harvard, they made me promise, as the director of medical student education in psychiatry at Harvard Medical School, to always say that my book was a made-up story, and that zombies cannot exist. There are people who can have injuries to their brains, and they can remind you of these cinematic characters. But we don’t need zombies to do ourselves in as a species.</p>



<p><strong>Another common search asks: If zombies existed, what would be the best way to kill them?</strong></p>



<p>According to Romero’s “Night of the Living Dead,” the ghouls can only be killed through shooting or hitting them in the head. By damaging zombies’ brain, they suffer an interruption of the brainstem, which regulates the brain’s regions that are responsible for all the vital functions such as heart rate, breathing, and blood pressure. Only then can you stop them altogether.</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<item>
		<title>How independent is the Justice Department now?</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/how-independent-is-the-justice-department-now/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[Terry Murphy]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Fri, 24 Oct 2025 16:29:58 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Nation & World]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Democracy]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Government]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Law]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[U.S. Politics]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418472</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Former federal legal officials were worried even before election, but Comey, James indictments crossed line, journalist says]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-classic has-colored-heading has-media-on-the-right"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		How independent is the Justice Department now?	</h1>

			<p class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Alarm is &#039;off the charts,&#039; says journalist who surveyed former federal officials		</p>
	
	
	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Liz Mineo	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-24">
			October 24, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			5 min read		</span>
	</div>

			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Emily Bazelon with Guy-Uriel Charles, Charles J. Ogletree, Jr. Professor of Law, Harvard Law School" class="wp-image-418473" height="992" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg 1980w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/102025_Justice_Department_21.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1980px) 100vw, 1980px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">Emily Bazelon with Guy-Uriel Charles, Charles J. Ogletree Jr. Professor of Law, Harvard Law School.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Stephanie Mitchell/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>

	
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-left is-layout-constrained wp-container-core-group-is-layout-12dd3699 wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>Even before the 2024 election, former federal legal officials were concerned about the potential politicization of the Department of Justice during a second Trump administration. But the recent indictments of former FBI Director James Comey and New York Attorney General Letitia James after direct pressure from the president have crossed a red line, said journalist <a href="https://emilybazelon.com/">Emily Bazelon</a>.</p>



<p>“I think crisis is a completely justified word to use,” said Bazelon, a Yale Law School graduate and New York Times Magazine staff writer. “What’s happening now is just an utter dismantling of all the norms and rules that were enacted after Watergate to try to have some separation between the White House’s political aspirations and the Justice Department.”</p>



<p>Bazelon was on campus Monday for the Francis Biddle Memorial Lecture, titled “Has Independence Ended for the Justice Department?” The Truman Capote Fellow for Creative Writing and Law at Yale Law School detailed the reaction within the DOJ and in the wider Washington legal community to White House moves to press for prosecutions of individuals considered Trump’s political enemies.</p>



<p>The department has for decades been assured prosecutorial independence in the wake of the public and congressional backlash against President Richard Nixon’s attempts to use the agency to impede the investigation into the Watergate scandal, she said.</p>



<p>“Presidents have been very careful, for their own political benefit, to separate themselves and to respect these norms and rules that have governed this sort of distancing [between the White House and the DOJ],” said Bazelon. “President Trump just sees it differently, and not entirely, but for the most part, he’s pretty much allowed to do that, at least until the courts stop him, and that, as probably many of you know, just hasn’t happened yet.”</p>



<p>The sense of crisis has been felt within the department, where about 4,000 of the agency’s more than 115,000 employees have resigned and more than 200 have been fired, Bazelon said. There is widespread concern among employees in Washington and at many U.S. attorneys’ offices around the nation about the perceived erosion of the department’s independence.</p>



<p>That view is shared by others in the nation’s legal establishment. In a recent <a href="https://www.americanbar.org/news/abanews/aba-news-archives/2025/10/independence-and-evidence-must-drive-federal-prosecutorial-actions/">statement</a>, the American Bar Association expressed its concern over “the use of federal prosecutorial power for apparent partisan ends,” calling it “an affront to the rule of law.”</p>



<p>The latest indictments emerged after a social media post on Sept. 20 in which Trump demanded that U.S. Attorney General Pam Bondi prosecute Comey and James.</p>



<p>Both had been involved in separate investigations of Trump. James, the New York attorney general, led a successful civil fraud investigation against the Trump Organization. Comey investigated Russian interference in the 2016 U.S. elections and the potential ties between the Trump campaign and Russia.</p>



<p>James was indicted on charges she made false claims on a mortgage application, and Comey was accused of lying to Congress and obstructing a congressional proceeding. Both have denied the charges.</p>



<p>Apprehension over Trump’s likely politicization of the Justice Department was high during his first term, said Bazelon, who <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2024/10/03/magazine/trump-justice-department-rivals.html">surveyed</a> 50 former federal legal officials in October 2024. A year later, she conducted another <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2025/10/06/magazine/legal-experts-trump-justice-department.html">survey</a> with 49 of the original participants, plus an additional one. Half of the participants were Democratic appointees and the other half Republican, but all were alarmed, she said.</p>



<p>“Their level of alarm at this point is basically off the charts, and I ran the survey before the indictments of James Comey and Letitia James started to come down,” said Bazelon. “And when we asked the question, ‘Do you think President Trump is using the Justice Department as a tool of retribution and reward?’ all 50 people said, ‘Yes.’</p>



<p>“People who were very high up in the George W. Bush administration are freaked out,” she said. “People who served in the first Trump administration, mostly off the record, are very freaked out.”</p>



<p>During the survey, participants also remarked on Congress’ lack of response to political interference in the Justice Department. Almost all noted the Supreme Court also has done little or nothing to push back. This stands in contrast to lower court judges, who have ruled against the administration in several cases, only to see their decisions rejected by the Supreme Court, which has a 6-3 conservative majority.</p>



<p>The departure of Erik Siebert, the U.S. attorney for the Eastern District of Virginia, who resigned after he declined to pursue criminal charges against James due to lack of evidence, is a warning sign that politically driven prosecutions will continue, said Bazelon.</p>



<p>“Erik Siebert is gone, and Lindsey Halligan, the current person acting as U.S. attorney in Virginia, is an insurance lawyer who has never prosecuted a case before,” said Bazelon. “That is, since Watergate, an unprecedented set of actions happening, and it doesn’t really look like there’s an end in sight to it.”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>

		<title>As states take lead in fixing U.S. schools, Harvard will serve as a hub</title>
		<link>https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/10/as-states-take-lead-in-fixing-u-s-schools-harvard-will-serve-as-a-hub/</link>
		
		<dc:creator><![CDATA[gazettebeckycoleman]]></dc:creator>
		<pubDate>Thu, 23 Oct 2025 19:20:57 +0000</pubDate>
				<category><![CDATA[Nation & World]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Education]]></category>
		<category><![CDATA[Research]]></category>
		<guid isPermaLink="false">https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/?p=418422</guid>

					<description><![CDATA[Grad School of Education will partner with nine states — from Rhode Island to Texas — to look for practical solutions to low test scores, chronic absenteeism]]></description>
										<content:encoded><![CDATA[<header
	class="wp-block-harvard-gazette-article-header alignfull article-header is-style-full-width-text-below title-above-image centered-image"
	style=" "
>
	
	<div class="article-header__content">
			<a
			class="article-header__category"
			href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/section/nation-world/"
		>
			Nation &amp; World		</a>
		
		<h1 class="article-header__title wp-block-heading ">
		As states take lead in fixing U.S. schools, Harvard will serve as a hub	</h1>

	
			</div>
		
<figure class="wp-block-image"><img decoding="async" alt="Grade-school students taking exams at their desks." class="wp-image-418424" height="992" loading="eager" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?w=1488" width="1488" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-leading-states-students-writing-desks-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="(max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">Frederick Bass/Getty Images</p></figcaption></figure>

	<div class="article-header__meta">
		<div class="wp-block-post-author">
			<address class="wp-block-post-author__content">
					<p class="author wp-block-post-author__name">
		Max Larkin	</p>
			<p class="wp-block-post-author__byline">
			Harvard Staff Writer		</p>
					</address>
		</div>

		<time class="article-header__date" datetime="2025-10-23">
			October 23, 2025		</time>

		<span class="article-header__reading-time">
			6 min read		</span>
	</div>

	
			<h2 class="article-header__subheading wp-block-heading">
			Grad School of Education will partner with nine states — from Rhode Island to Texas — to look for practical solutions to low test scores, chronic absenteeism		</h2>
		
</header>



<div class="wp-block-group alignwide has-global-padding is-content-justification-center is-layout-constrained wp-block-group-is-layout-constrained">
<p>When it comes to American public education, states wield a lot of power over policy — lately, more than they have in decades.</p>



<p>But that influence isn’t always accompanied by insight.</p>



<p>Sometimes, despite valiant efforts, superintendents and commissioners enact sweeping new policies affecting millions of students with little immediate feedback about whether and how they’re working.</p>



<p>Christina Grant knows that struggle: From 2021 to 2024, she served as state superintendent of schools in Washington, D.C. And now she’s part of a University initiative designed to help fill the knowledge gap in partnership with a first cohort of states announced this week.</p>



<p>“It’s very different to be able to look under the hood with a particular policy — where you’re getting answers not in five years, but in a year or a year and a half,” Grant said. “You want to go from hearing about what worked with a particular program to knowing.”</p>



<p>Grant was named executive director of the Center for Education Policy Research at the Harvard Graduate School of Education last June, at a troubled time for American schools. Four years after COVID-19 closed classrooms, test scores and attendance still haven’t returned to their pre-pandemic highs.</p>



<div class="flourish-embed flourish-scatter" data-src="visualisation/25809062?2555329"><script src="https://public.flourish.studio/resources/embed.js"></script><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://public.flourish.studio/visualisation/25809062/thumbnail" width="100%" alt="scatter visualization" /></noscript></div>



<p>Along with education economist Thomas Kane, CEPR’s faculty director, Grant began working toward the States Leading States Initiative, <a href="https://cepr.harvard.edu/news/new-states-leading-states-sls-initiative-launched-share-proven-strategies-state-policy">launched</a> in January and backed by a $10 million grant from the Walton Foundation that will sustain it for its first four years.</p>



<p>This week, CEPR announced its first cohort of nine very different states that share a mix of curiosity and practicality about reversing those trends.</p>



<p>“We wanted diversity along a number of different axes. So: big states and small states, states with veteran education leadership and others where a new team is in place — and then diversity of political leadership: red states and blue,” said Scott Sargrad, who will lead the initiative.</p>



<p>Under the program, Harvard’s data analysts will work with state education bureaucracies in Rhode Island and Colorado but also Texas and Tennessee.</p>



<p>Sargrad comes to Harvard after two stints at the U.S. Department of Education, an agency that is facing an <a href="https://news.harvard.edu/gazette/story/2025/04/envisioning-a-country-with-no-department-of-education/">existential threat</a> via an order from President Donald Trump to start closing operations. And that was before the current government shutdown.</p>



<p>With federal oversight of public schools — on the ebb since 2015 —&nbsp;at its lowest point in decades, States Leading States only looks more necessary to Kane.</p>



<p>“There’s a bit of a vacuum — and so the timing could not be better,” said Kane, who will be one educational economist on call for participating state leaders.</p>



<p>The stakes feel incredibly high to Kane: nothing less than “a culture change in K-12 education. We need to recognize that what sounds good on paper doesn’t always work, and that —&nbsp;when it comes to critical questions like lowering absenteeism —&nbsp;you have to be collecting evidence.”</p>



<div class="flourish-embed flourish-chart" data-src="visualisation/25808683?2555329"><script src="https://public.flourish.studio/resources/embed.js"></script><noscript><img decoding="async" src="https://public.flourish.studio/visualisation/25808683/thumbnail" width="100%" alt="chart visualization" /></noscript></div>



<p>“I can’t think of anything we could be doing that would make a bigger difference 10 or 20 years from now,” he said.</p>



<p>And the relationship won’t be limited to Zoom calls with CEPR’s offices on Church Street in Cambridge.</p>



<p>Instead, Grant said, “We’ll be placing <a href="https://sdp.cepr.harvard.edu/data-fellowship">Strategic Data Fellows</a> inside these systems. So those will either be individuals who already work at the departments and they’ll go through our fellowship, or we’re going to work with departments to recruit, select, and place fellows” in those nine states.</p>



<p>The goal is to get beyond the superficial, slow, and suggestive data that sometimes guide education policy&nbsp;and replace them with something like randomized controlled trials — the gold standard for empirical research.</p>



<p>“We want to give our partners more confidence than they could get by looking at simple correlations,” Sargrad said. “‘Scores went up; scores went down’ —&nbsp;that’s something, but it’s not super-convincing.”</p>



<p>Crucially, the policies under study will come from the states themselves. Kane, for instance, expects to lend his expertise to Ohio’s in-school cellphone ban, summer reading and math camps in Alabama, and literacy coaches in Texas.</p>



<figure class="wp-block-image size-full"><img loading="lazy" decoding="async" width="1920" height="1280" src="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg" alt="Tom Kane." class="wp-image-418425" srcset="https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg 1920w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=150,100 150w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=300,200 300w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=768,512 768w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=1024,683 1024w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=48,32 48w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=96,64 96w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=1488,992 1488w, https://news.harvard.edu/wp-content/uploads/2025/10/states-states-kane-1920.jpg?resize=1680,1120 1680w" sizes="auto, (max-width: 1920px) 100vw, 1920px" /><figcaption class="wp-element-caption"><p class="wp-element-caption--caption">“I can’t think of anything we could be doing that would make a bigger difference 10 or 20 years from now,” said Thomas Kane.</p><p class="wp-element-caption--credit">File photo by Veasey Conway/Harvard Staff Photographer</p></figcaption></figure>



<p>Tony Sanders,&nbsp;state superintendent of public schools in cohort member Illinois, is already looking forward to that objective inside look — whatever it reveals.</p>



<p>When it comes to policies that affect how children learn, Sanders argued, you have to be ruthlessly self-critical.</p>



<p>To illustrate, he borrowed a <a href="https://blog.x.company/tackle-the-monkey-first-90fd6223e04d">fable</a> from tech entrepreneur Astro Teller.</p>



<p>“If you’re going to teach a monkey how to recite Shakespeare in the park, you need a monkey and you need a pedestal,” Sanders said. “Which do you do first?”</p>



<p>Inevitably, Sanders said, many settle on the simpler task: building the pedestal. But “at the end of the day, if you can’t find a monkey that can recite Shakespeare, all you’ve done is set up a pedestal in the park.”</p>



<p>Sanders already has a list of policies he wants his staff to assess with help from Harvard. Those include the development of a plan for numeracy, or mathematical facility, to travel alongside existing work on literacy, and a cellphone ban supported by Gov. JB Pritzker that’s still being debated by the state’s legislature.</p>



<p>Illinois’ proposed phone ban would likely be less stringent than the one already in force in Ohio&nbsp;—&nbsp;and States Leading States will be outfitted to compare the two. They won’t have to wait long: CEPR staff expect their first early findings on policies to publish next spring.</p>



<p>As a former administrator, Grant admits that there is a level of trust involved in admitting Harvard and its scholars into the workings of a large education bureaucracy, where they’re likely to encounter programs failing to meet their potential alongside ones that work.</p>



<p>“That’s why I’m proud of every single state that signed on,” Grant said. “They don’t want to be making changes that don’t move the needle. They’re excited to know, ‘Am I off-course? And if so, how do I get back on?’”</p>
</div>
]]></content:encoded>
